Ibn Taymiyya's Opening Critique of Greek Logic
Author opens with a forceful rejection of Greek logic's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's central claim about Greek logic and explain its significance for Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Greek logic is unnecessary for the Muslim who has the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Ibn Taymiyya opens his critique with a bold, unambiguous statement: Greek logic is not needed by those who remember God. This sets the stage for his entire project, which seeks to dismantle the claim that Aristotelian logic is a prerequisite for sound reasoning. The phrase 'those who remember God' points to the sufficiency of divine revelation over human rational systems. In this lesson, we will extract the core of his claim and understand its revolutionary implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find the phrase 'al-mantiq al-yūnānī' (Greek logic) in the Arabic. What does Ibn Taymiyya mean by this term? It refers to the Aristotelian logical system.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term polemically to distinguish it from Islamic logic as developed by later Muslims.
2
Extract the main assertion
Look at the verb 'lā tahtāju ilayhi' (is not needed). Who is the subject? 'Alladhīna yadhkurūn Allāh' (those who remember God). So the claim is: Greek logic is not necessary for the God-conscious.
'Dhikr Allāh' can mean both remembrance and knowledge of God through revelation.
3
Consider the implied alternative
If Greek logic is not needed, what suffices? The context of the entire work implies that the Qur'an and Sunnah provide all necessary tools for correct reasoning.
This is an epistemological stance: revelation is sufficient.
4
Anticipate the significance
This opening claim challenges the widespread assumption among philosophers and some theologians that logic is an indispensable tool for protecting thought from error.
Ibn Taymiyya will later elaborate why the rules of Greek logic are neither universal nor necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المنطق اليوناني لا تحتاج إليه الذكر الله [؟]. ولا [؟] أن [؟] صادقة [؟] لماذا [؟] من [؟] خطا طائفة من عماني [؟] خطا طائفة من قضاياه [؟] في [؟] ما كتب [؟] يعم الجميع [؟]
Know that Greek logic is not needed by those who remember God [?]. And [؟] that [؟] truthful [؟] why [؟] from [؟] a group of [؟] erroneous [؟] a group of its propositions [؟] in [؟] what was written [؟] includes all [؟].
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Greek logic. He begins with a direct assertion: 'Know that Greek logic is not needed by those who remember God'—a clear rejection of its necessity for Islamic intellectual life. The subsequent text is too corrupt to reconstruct, but it likely elaborates on the errors of the logicians. The author's stance is foundational for the entire work: Greek logic is not a prerequisite for sound reasoning; rather, the Qur'an and Sunnah suffice.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's opening salvo establishes that Greek logic is not a necessary tool for Muslims, who have a superior source of guidance in divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a typical theological argument from a kalam text (e.g., the proof for the existence of God using huduth al-ajsam). Rewrite it as Ibn Taymiyya might have critiqued it, focusing on whether the argument relies on Greek logical presuppositions that a 'person who remembers God' could replace with direct scriptural reasoning.

Refutation of the Logicians' Definition of Speech
Author rejects the logicians' view that speech is any uttered sound, asserting it is only meaningful sentences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Islamic definition of kalam as a complete meaningful sentence and the logicians' definition as any uttered sound, and explain the author's refutation of the latter.

Core Idea
In Islam, speech (kalam) is only a complete meaningful sentence – not any uttered word.

The logicians of the Greek tradition define 'kalam' broadly as any articulated sound that conveys meaning, including single words. Ibn Taymiyya, drawing from the Islamic linguistic tradition, rejects this as a grave error. In this passage, he asserts the correct definition and proves it with a Prophetic hadith, showing that only complete sentences qualify as speech in the Islamic context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of kalam
Locate in the Arabic the Islamic definition ('al-jumla al-tamma al-mufida') and the logicians' definition (calling 'al-ism al-mufrad' speech). Notice the contrast.
The logicians' definition is broader.
2
Analyze the Islamic definition's components
Break down 'al-jumla al-tamma al-mufida': a jumla (sentence) that is tamma (complete) and mufida (beneficial/meaningful). What does each component exclude?
3
Examine the author's proof from hadith
The author cites the hadith: 'The best remembrance is la ilaha illa Allah'. Observe that this is a complete sentence. If a single noun were speech, then the word 'Allah' alone would suffice, but the Prophet taught the full sentence.
The proof is based on prophetic practice.
4
Evaluate the refutation's logic
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if a single noun were kalam, then the name 'Allah' alone would be considered speech, but it is not. Why does this fail the logicians' definition?
The logicians would counter that 'Allah' does convey meaning, but the author relies on the Islamic concept of kalam as a communicative unit.
5
Understand the stakes of definition
Consider how this definitional dispute affects other sciences: for example, the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz) is based on its sentences, not single words. Also, in fiqh, the definition of 'kalam' affects the validity of oaths and contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام عند أهل الإسلام هو الجملة التامة المفيدة. والمنطقيون قد يسمون الاسم المفرد كلاماً، وهذا خطأ. فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: 'أفضل الذكر لا إله إلا الله'، وهي جملة مفيدة. ولو كان الاسم المفرد كلاماً لكان قول 'الله' وحده كلاماً، وليس كذلك.
Speech according to the people of Islam is the complete beneficial sentence. The logicians sometimes call a single noun 'speech', and this is an error. For the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The best remembrance is la ilaha illa Allah', and it is a complete sentence. If a single noun were speech, then the word 'Allah' alone would be speech, and it is not so.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the Islamic concept of 'kalam' (speech) and refutes the logicians' broader definition. The author asserts that, for Muslims, kalam is exclusively the complete, meaningful sentence (al-jumla al-tamma al-mufida). The logicians, however, sometimes apply the term to a single noun (ism mufrad), which the author considers a mistake. He provides evidence from the Prophetic hadith: 'The best remembrance is la ilaha illa Allah', which is a complete sentence. If a single noun were considered speech, then simply uttering the name 'Allah' alone would count as speech, but the Prophet did not teach that. The argument relies on the Islamic legal and linguistic tradition where kalam implies a communicative act with a complete meaning.

Key Terms

الاسم المفرد

Key Takeaway
The Islamic definition of kalam as a complete meaningful sentence excludes single words, contrary to the logicians' broader definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logicians' Definition of Kalam
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that only complete sentences constitute speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' definition of kalam and evaluate its strength using Islamic evidence.

Core Idea
The logicians restrict kalam to complete sentences, but Islamic dhikr proves even a single name of Allah is speech.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks a foundational claim of the logicians: that only complete sentences qualify as meaningful speech (kalam). He does not merely argue theoretically; he brings a concrete example from the Prophet's own teachings—the phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah'—to show that a single nominal phrase carries full meaning and is undeniably considered speech in the Islamic tradition. This lesson will walk you through his critique and help you see how a single counterexample can dismantle a definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about kalam
Find the condition that logicians impose for something to be considered beneficial speech. What exactly do they require?
They require a complete sentence (jumlah tāmmah), like a verb+noun combination or an imperative, and exclude single nouns.
2
Find the counterexample from Islamic practice
Which specific phrase from the Prophet's teachings does Ibn Taymiyya use to refute the logicians? How is it used?
The Prophet said, 'The best remembrance is la ilaha illa Allah.' This is a single nominal phrase, not a complete sentence in the logicians' sense, yet it is the best dhikr.
3
Analyze why the counterexample refutes the definition
Why does Ibn Taymiyya consider this a decisive refutation? What is the relationship between the Prophetic teaching and the logical claim?
The Prophet deliberately used single phrases for dhikr, demonstrating that they convey complete meaning and are indeed kalam. This contradicts the logicians' condition.
4
Evaluate the broader epistemological implication
What does this critique reveal about the authority of logicians over religious terminology? How does it apply to other Islamic sciences?
Religious terms must be understood according to the Lawgiver's usage, not merely through logical or linguistic philosophy. Definitions in Islamic sciences should align with revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الكلام المبتدأ يكون الجملة التامة كـ (اشتر) أو فعل واسم، فأما الاسم المفرد فلا يكون كلاما مفيدا عند هؤلاء المنطقيين. وأما النماء وإن كان وحده كافٍ معدودا غيره أو كان المقصود به سهوا أو إشارة كما يسمون بالاهوات إلى التوضع، لكن مقصد به المعاني التي يقصد بالكلام، ولهذا هدى الناس من البيع: ما يفعله بعض الناس من ذكر اسم الله وحده بدل السفر كلام؛ فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «أفضل الذكر لا إله إلا الله»، وقد ورد أنه كان يعلمهم التحميد بالجمل التام مثل سبحان الله والحمد لله ولا إله إلا الله والله أكبر ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله، وما كان يذكر آخر كلا: لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنة. وأشار ذلك فطر طائفة من الناس أن ذكر الاسم المفرد مشروع بل طائفة منهم أفضل. وخاصة من قول: لا إله إلا الله، وحكى بعضهم أن ذكر الاسم المختمر وهو هو أفضل الاسم المطهر. وافر جهود النطان إلى تقولوا القط الأسد أمانا ولا هذامل دخلوا بذلك صورة[؟]. ثم إن هذا الاسم المسيول الذي لا يعلم السائل معناه إنا أحد عند عانها سيم حال السائل فيه إلى نوع: حدهما أن يكون نودكوه في الـ مول تعالی حمید اصغر اطنا المعبد الذي من حوله و برد جینا حاظ او روا لى المساعد فى حوله فاط رو الاذن البعير بالعامرات.
That the beginning of speech is the complete sentence like 'Buy' or verb and noun. As for a single noun, it is not beneficial speech according to these logicians. But even if it is alone and sufficient, counted as other, or the intended meaning is a slip or an indication as they call with gestures to the place, but rather the intended meanings are those meant by speech. For this reason, people are guided from the sale: what some people do of mentioning the name of Allah alone instead of travel is speech. Indeed the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The best remembrance is la ilaha illa Allah.' And it has been reported that he used to teach them tahmid with complete sentences such as Subhanallah, Alhamdulillah, La ilaha illa Allah, Allahu Akbar, La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah, and he would not mention other: 'Whoever says la ilaha illa Allah enters Paradise.' And this indicated that a group of people consider mentioning the single noun legitimate, and a group of them consider it superior. Especially the saying 'la ilaha illa Allah', and some narrated that mentioning the contracted name, i.e. Huwa, is superior to the explicit name. And the efforts of the people led to saying 'the lion cut security' and they did not enter thereby that form. Then this questioned name whose meaning the questioner does not know is one of two types: either it is mentioned in the word of Allah, 'Hamid asghar atna al-Ma'bud alladhi min hawlihi', etc.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' definition of 'kalam' (speech). The author (Ibn Taymiyya) begins by stating the logicians' position: that only a complete sentence (like 'buy' or a verb+noun combination) constitutes beneficial speech, and a single noun alone does not. He immediately challenges this by pointing out that even a single word, if it is understood and intended to convey a meaning, is indeed speech. He provides a powerful Islamic evidence: the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ declared 'La ilaha illa Allah' as the best remembrance (dhikr). This is a single nominal sentence (or phrase) that is certainly considered speech, and it is not a full 'jumla' in the logicians' sense. Furthermore, the Prophet taught his companions to glorify Allah using complete sentences like 'Subhanallah' and 'Alhamdulillah', but also emphasized the single phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah' as the key to Paradise. Some scholars even hold that mentioning the name 'Allah' alone is superior. The author then criticizes those who rely solely on language to understand the Lawgiver's intent, arguing that the true meanings of religious terms like 'khamr', 'nabidh', etc., require knowledge beyond mere philology—including legal reasoning and understanding of the Lawgiver's usage. The passage forcefully asserts that the logicians' restricted definition is contradicted by Islamic practice and consensus.

Key Terms
الكلام
الاسم المفرد
الذكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The silent gesture of a nod.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the logicians' definition of kalam is too narrow, as Islamic practice shows that even a single name of Allah conveys complete meaning and is considered speech.

The Failure of the Essential/Accidental Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's objections to the logical distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Core Idea
The logicians' distinctions between essential and accidental are not epistemologically sound.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational distinction in Aristotelian logic between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. He examines several criteria proposed by logicians—such as dependence of definition, priority to negation, and the nature of necessary concomitants—and finds each insufficient. This critique undermines the claim that definitions can capture the essence of things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' criteria
List the criteria the logicians use to distinguish essential from accidental: dependence of definition, division of necessary concomitants (lazim), and priority over negation.
These are reported by Ibn Taymiyya in the passage.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's rejection
For each criterion, understand why Ibn Taymiyya says it fails. He states that none of these distinctions constitute a valid distinction between essential and other than it.
The passage explicitly says: 'each one of these distinctions does not constitute a valid distinction.'
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on how this critique affects the theory of definition. If the essential/accidental distinction is unsound, definitions based on listing essential attributes are also problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون الذاتي هو الذي توقف المحد عليه خلاف العرضي. واللازم ينقسم إلى لازم لوجود الماهية دون حقيقتها، وإلى لازم للماهية كالزوجية والفردية للأربعة والثلاثة. والفرق بين لازم الماهية ولازم وجودها أن لازم وجودها لا يمكن أن تعقل الماهية موجودة دونه. وجعلوا للذاتي خاصية: أنه ما كان معاولا للنافية، ثم قالوا: من اللوازم ما يكون معاولا للنافية. وأما الذاتي فهو ما يكون سابقا على النافية، واللازم ما يكون تابعا. وكل واحد من هذه الفروق لا تصلح الفرق بين الذاتي وغيره كما قد بسطت كلامهم في غير هذا الموضع. ثم انتقلوا إلى القول في الحد التام: أنه تصوير الحقيقة بجميع صفاتها الذاتية، فهل تشترطون فيه أن يتصور جميع صفاته الذاتية المميزة له عن غيره أم لا؟ فإن شرطتم ذلك لزمكم أن تقولوا: الإنسان حيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وأما لفظ الحيوان فإنه يدل على هذه الصفات بالمطابقة، ولا يخلو عنها. وإن اكتفيتم ببعض الصفات المميزة كالناطق مثلا، فإن الناطق يدل على الحيوان كناية، لا على سائر الصفات. فأنتم بين أمرين: إما أن تذكروا جميع الصفات في الحد، وإما أن تكتفوا بالالتزام. والقصد إبطال قولهم إن الحد التام لا بد فيه من جميع الذاتيات.
They say: the essential (dhati) is that on which the definition depends, unlike the accidental. And the necessary concomitant (lazim) is divided into what is necessary for the existence of the essence without its reality, and what is necessary for the essence itself, like evenness and oddness for four and three. The difference between what is necessary for the essence and what is necessary for its existence is that what is necessary for its existence cannot be conceived without the essence existing. And they made for the essential a special property: that it is what is a cause for the negation, then they said: among the concomitants there is what is a cause for the negation. As for the essential, it is what is prior to the negation, and the concomitant is what is subsequent. And each one of these distinctions does not constitute a valid distinction between the essential and other than it, as I have already expounded their statements elsewhere. Then they turned to the discussion of the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm): that it is the depiction of the reality by all its essential attributes. So do you stipulate in it that one depicts all its essential attributes distinguishing it from others or not? For if you stipulate that, it would require you to say: man is a sentient animal that moves by will, whereas the word 'animal' indicates these attributes by correspondence and is not free from them. And if you are satisfied with some of the distinguishing attributes like 'rational' for example, then 'rational' indicates 'animal' by implication, not all the other attributes. So you are between two cases: either you mention all the attributes in the definition, or you are satisfied with implication. The intent is to refute their claim that the complete definition must include all the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage contains Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' distinctions between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes, and their theory of definition. He begins by summarizing the logicians' claims: the essential is what the definition depends on; the necessary concomitant (lazim) is divided into what is necessary for the existence of the essence (like heat for fire) and what is necessary for the essence itself (like evenness for four). They also claim that the essential is prior to the negation, while the concomitant is subsequent. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that none of these distinctions actually differentiate between the essential and non-essential, referring to a more detailed refutation elsewhere. He then moves to the definition (hadd), specifically the 'complete definition' which they claim must include all essential attributes. He poses a dilemma: either you must include all essential attributes, which leads to an endless list (e.g., 'man is a sentient animal that moves by will'), or you are satisfied with a partial set like 'rational', which then indicates the rest only by implication. Either way, their claim that the complete definition requires all essential attributes is untenable. This critique is central to Ibn Taymiyya's broader attack on the logicians' epistemology, particularly their view that definition produces certain knowledge.

Key Terms
الذاتي
العرضي
اللازم
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) He endorses the distinction based on dependence of definition.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the logicians' criteria for distinguishing essential from accidental are not epistemologically reliable, thus challenging the foundation of Aristotelian definition theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Impossibility of a Complete Definition
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' requirement of a complete definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and assess Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument against the logicians' requirement of including all essential attributes in a definition.

Core Idea
The logicians' perfect definition is either impossible or uninformative.

Ibn Taymiyya turns to the concept of the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm), which logicians claim must include all essential attributes of the thing defined. He poses a powerful dilemma: either you must enumerate every such attribute, which is impractical and leads to an endless regress, or you can rely on a partial list that implies the rest, but then the definition no longer directly conveys all essentials. Either way, the claim that definitions provide certain knowledge collapses.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' requirement
The logicians claim that a complete definition must include all essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya) distinguishing the thing from others.
This is the doctrine Ibn Taymiyya attacks.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Ibn Taymiyya presents two options: either list all essential attributes explicitly, or rely on a partial list that implies the rest (dalala or iltizam).
He illustrates with the definition of man: either list 'sentient animal moving by will' (all attributes) or just 'rational' (implying the others).
3
Explain why each horn is problematic
If you list all, the definition becomes endless and impractical. If you rely on implication, the definition does not directly present all essentials, undermining the claim of complete depiction.
4
Conclude the implication
The requirement of a complete definition is untenable; thus the logicians' theory of definition fails.
Ibn Taymiyya's intent is to 'refute their claim that the complete definition must include all essential attributes.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون الذاتي هو الذي توقف المحد عليه خلاف العرضي. واللازم ينقسم إلى لازم لوجود الماهية دون حقيقتها، وإلى لازم للماهية كالزوجية والفردية للأربعة والثلاثة. والفرق بين لازم الماهية ولازم وجودها أن لازم وجودها لا يمكن أن تعقل الماهية موجودة دونه. وجعلوا للذاتي خاصية: أنه ما كان معاولا للنافية، ثم قالوا: من اللوازم ما يكون معاولا للنافية. وأما الذاتي فهو ما يكون سابقا على النافية، واللازم ما يكون تابعا. وكل واحد من هذه الفروق لا تصلح الفرق بين الذاتي وغيره كما قد بسطت كلامهم في غير هذا الموضع. ثم انتقلوا إلى القول في الحد التام: أنه تصوير الحقيقة بجميع صفاتها الذاتية، فهل تشترطون فيه أن يتصور جميع صفاته الذاتية المميزة له عن غيره أم لا؟ فإن شرطتم ذلك لزمكم أن تقولوا: الإنسان حيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة، وأما لفظ الحيوان فإنه يدل على هذه الصفات بالمطابقة، ولا يخلو عنها. وإن اكتفيتم ببعض الصفات المميزة كالناطق مثلا، فإن الناطق يدل على الحيوان كناية، لا على سائر الصفات. فأنتم بين أمرين: إما أن تذكروا جميع الصفات في الحد، وإما أن تكتفوا بالالتزام. والقصد إبطال قولهم إن الحد التام لا بد فيه من جميع الذاتيات.
They say: the essential (dhati) is that on which the definition depends, unlike the accidental. And the necessary concomitant (lazim) is divided into what is necessary for the existence of the essence without its reality, and what is necessary for the essence itself, like evenness and oddness for four and three. The difference between what is necessary for the essence and what is necessary for its existence is that what is necessary for its existence cannot be conceived without the essence existing. And they made for the essential a special property: that it is what is a cause for the negation, then they said: among the concomitants there is what is a cause for the negation. As for the essential, it is what is prior to the negation, and the concomitant is what is subsequent. And each one of these distinctions does not constitute a valid distinction between the essential and other than it, as I have already expounded their statements elsewhere. Then they turned to the discussion of the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm): that it is the depiction of the reality by all its essential attributes. So do you stipulate in it that one depicts all its essential attributes distinguishing it from others or not? For if you stipulate that, it would require you to say: man is a sentient animal that moves by will, whereas the word 'animal' indicates these attributes by correspondence and is not free from them. And if you are satisfied with some of the distinguishing attributes like 'rational' for example, then 'rational' indicates 'animal' by implication, not all the other attributes. So you are between two cases: either you mention all the attributes in the definition, or you are satisfied with implication. The intent is to refute their claim that the complete definition must include all the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage contains Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' distinctions between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes, and their theory of definition. He begins by summarizing the logicians' claims: the essential is what the definition depends on; the necessary concomitant (lazim) is divided into what is necessary for the existence of the essence (like heat for fire) and what is necessary for the essence itself (like evenness for four). They also claim that the essential is prior to the negation, while the concomitant is subsequent. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that none of these distinctions actually differentiate between the essential and non-essential, referring to a more detailed refutation elsewhere. He then moves to the definition (hadd), specifically the 'complete definition' which they claim must include all essential attributes. He poses a dilemma: either you must include all essential attributes, which leads to an endless list (e.g., 'man is a sentient animal that moves by will'), or you are satisfied with a partial set like 'rational', which then indicates the rest only by implication. Either way, their claim that the complete definition requires all essential attributes is untenable. This critique is central to Ibn Taymiyya's broader attack on the logicians' epistemology, particularly their view that definition produces certain knowledge.

Key Terms
الحد التام
الذاتي
الصفات الذاتية

The Twofold Division of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
The logicians' division of knowledge into conception and assent and the argument for immediate knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) and explain why some knowledge must be immediate (badihi) to avoid circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge splits into conception and assent; each requires a foundation of self-evident truths to avoid circularity.

The logicians base their entire discipline on a fundamental division of knowledge. They argue that every piece of knowledge is either a concept or a judgment, and each is either self-evident or acquired. They then demonstrate why some knowledge must be self-evident: otherwise we would fall into circular reasoning or an infinite chain of causes. This foundational argument justifies the need for definitions and syllogisms as tools to acquire new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Find in the Arabic the terms 'tasawwur' and 'tasdiq'. Note that tasawwur is simple apprehension without judgment, while tasdiq involves affirmation or denial.
2
Classify each as immediate or acquired
For each type, the logicians say it can be either 'badihi' (self-evident) or 'nazari' (acquired through reasoning). Identify these terms.
3
Analyze the argument against everything being acquired
The logicians argue that if all knowledge were nazari, it would depend on something else, leading to either an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a vicious circle (dawr qabli). Locate this reasoning in the passage.
4
Recognize the necessity of immediate knowledge
Since regress and circularity are impossible, there must be some badihi knowledge as a foundation. This is the conclusion that justifies the need for starting points in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم فصل ( ملخص أصول المنطق واصطلاحاته) بنوا المنطق على الكلام في الحدّ ونوعه و القياس البرهاني» ونوعه. قالوا : موضوع المنطق لأن العلم إما تصور» وإما تصديق»، وكل منهما إما «بديهي» وإما «نظري»، فإنه من المعلوم أنه ليس الجميع بديهياً، ولا يجوز أن يكون الجميع نظرياً لافتقار النظري إلى البديهي، فيلزمَ الدَّورَ القَبْلي أو التسلسل في العلل ـ التي هي هنا أسباب العلم، وهي الأدلة - وهما ممتنعان . و «النظري» منهما لا بد له من طريق يُنال به فالطريق الذي ينال به التصور هو «الحدّ»، والطريق الذي يُنال به التصديق هو «القياس» . فَـ «الحد» اسم جامع لكل ما يعرف التصور، وهو «القول الشارح»، فيدخُل فيه أقسام الحقيقي» و«الرسمي» و«اللفظي»، أو هو الحقيقي خاصةً فيقرن به الرسمي، واللفظي ليس من هذا الباب ؛ أو الحد اسم للحقيقي والرسمي دون اللفظي. فإن كل نوع من الحد
[2] In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Section: Summary of the principles of logic and its terminologies. They built logic upon speech about definition and its types and demonstrative syllogism and its types. They said: The subject of logic is that knowledge is either conception or assent, and each of them is either immediate or acquired. For it is known that not everything is immediate, nor is it permissible for everything to be acquired, because the acquired depends on the immediate, which would necessitate prior circularity or infinite regress in the causes—which here are the means of knowledge, i.e., the proofs—and both are impossible. The acquired of them necessarily has a path by which it is attained. The path by which conception is attained is the definition, and the path by which assent is attained is the syllogism. So 'definition' is a comprehensive name for everything that gives knowledge of conception, and it is the 'explicatory statement'. Thus it includes the divisions: the real (haqiqi), the descriptive (rasmi), and the verbal (lafzi). Or it is specifically the real, and the descriptive is associated with it, while the verbal is not of this category. Or definition is the name for the real and the descriptive, excluding the verbal. For every type of definition...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a concise summary of the logicians' foundational principles. The author here is not stating his own view but reporting what the logicians say. They base logic on two main subjects: definition (hadd) and demonstrative syllogism (qiyas burhani). Their argument proceeds in steps: First, they divide knowledge into conception (tasawwur) — which is simple apprehension of a concept — and assent (tasdiq) — which is judgment about a proposition. Each of these is either immediate (badihi) — known without effort — or acquired (nazari) — needing proof. They then argue that not all knowledge can be immediate, nor can it all be acquired, because the acquired depends on the immediate, and if everything were acquired, there would be an infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, some knowledge must be immediate, and from it the acquired is derived. The path to acquire a conception is definition (hadd), which is an explicatory statement (qawl sharh). The path to acquire assent is syllogism (qiyas). Then the author notes that 'definition' can be understood in three ways: as a universal term covering real (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi) definitions; or as only real, with descriptive attached; or as real and descriptive but not verbal. The text breaks off as it begins to explain each type.

تصديق
بديهي
نظري
دور قبلي
تسلسل

The logicians argue that some knowledge must be self-evident to avoid the absurdities of infinite regress and circularity.

Types of Definition (Hadd)
The logicians' discussion on the types and scope of definition (hadd).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of definition (hadd) as discussed by the logicians and explain the different views on its scope.

Core Idea
Definition (hadd) is the path to conceive; its types are real, descriptive, and verbal, with debates over inclusion.

After establishing that conception is attained through definition, the logicians clarify what 'definition' means. They offer three interpretations: some include all three types—real, descriptive, and verbal; others restrict it to real only; and others include real and descriptive but not verbal. This lesson unpacks these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of definition
The passage states that definition is the path to acquire conception (tasawwur). Note that it is called an 'explicatory statement' (qawl sharh).
2
List the three types of definition
Find the terms: haqiqi (real), rasmi (descriptive), and lafzi (verbal). Understand that real definition gives the essence, descriptive gives distinguishing features, and verbal clarifies word meaning.
3
Examine the three positions on the scope of 'definition'
Some say 'hadd' includes all three; others say it is only the real definition, with descriptive attached separately; others include real and descriptive but not verbal. Locate these options in the Arabic.
4
Consider the implications
Each position affects what is considered a proper definition. Think about which would be most useful for scientific definitions.

Epistemic Foundation: Conception, Assent, and the Need for Logic
Author presents the epistemological justification for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logicians' argument that knowledge must divide into immediate and theoretical, and explain why this division justifies the need for definition and syllogism.

Core Idea
Logic's necessity arises from the impossibility of infinite regress in knowledge.

The passage opens by establishing that knowledge is either conception or assent, each either immediate or theoretical. Logicians argue that if all knowledge were theoretical, we would need an infinite chain of proofs, which is impossible. Hence there must be immediate knowledge, and logic provides the path from immediate to theoretical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the epistemic categories
Find where the author introduces tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), and badihi (immediate) and nazari (theoretical). These are the building blocks of epistemology in logic.
Conception is grasping a concept; assent is affirming a truth.
2
Extract the impossibility argument
The author states that not all knowledge can be theoretical, lest circularity (al-dawr al-qabli) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) occur. Identify the two rejected scenarios.
Both circularity and regress are deemed impossible by the logicians.
3
Link to the need for definition and syllogism
Since theoretical knowledge requires a path, that path is definition (hadd) for conception and syllogism (qiyas) for assent. Explain why each path is needed.
Definition makes concepts known; syllogism yields certainty.

They built logic upon discourse on definition and its type, and demonstrative syllogism and its type. They said: The subject matter of logic is that knowledge is only either conception or assent, and each of them is either immediate or theoretical. For it is known that not everything is immediate, nor can everything be theoretical because the theoretical requires the immediate, thus necessitating circular reasoning or infinite regress in causes — which here are the means of knowledge, i.e., the proofs — and both are impossible. And the theoretical of them must have a path by which it is attained; the path by which conception is attained is 'definition', and the path by which assent is attained is 'syllogism'. So 'definition' is a comprehensive name for everything that makes conception known, and it is 'explanatory speech', so it includes the subdivisions: real definition, descriptive definition, and verbal definition; or it is specifically real definition, and descriptive definition is paired with it, while verbal definition is not of this category; or definition is a name for real and descriptive definition excluding verbal definition. For each type of these three divisions is a convention of a group among them, as I have elaborated and mentioned their names elsewhere. And the syllogism: if its material is certain, it is specifically 'demonstrative'; if it is 'accepted' (in dialectic), it is 'dialectical'; if it is 'widely known', it is 'rhetorical'; if it is 'imaginative', it is 'poetic', with regard to materials; and if it is 'deceptive', it is 'sophistical'. Hence demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical may overlap; and some people make the rhetorical the 'probable', while others make it 'persuasive'. And they have other conventions, some agreeing with the convention of the First Teacher Aristotle, and some disagreeing with him. For many authors among them departed in many matters from the method of their First Teacher. But the intention here is not to elaborate this. 'Definition' is composed of essential attributes if it is a real definition, otherwise it must be composed of the five universals. And each of them is either shared between the defined and others, or it is distinguishing for it from others. The shared essential is 'genus', the distinguishing essential is 'differentia', and the combination of the two is 'species'. The shared accidental is 'accident', and the distinguishing accidental is 'property'. And 'property' may be used for what occurs to the species even if it is not common to all its individuals, but that property does not provide distinction. Similarly, 'species' may be used for relative species that are species relative to what is above them and genus relative to what is below them. But this and its like are details of logic that are not the intended topic of discussion here, for discussion of what they mentioned about genus and species has another place beyond what is recorded in this brief note. So these are the five universals. Opposite the universal is the 'particular', which is that the conception of which prevents the occurrence of sharing in it. Discourse on the composite is preceded by discourse on the simple term and the indication of the word to it. And the syllogism is composed of two premises. The premise is a proposition, either affirmative or negative; each of them is either universal or particular. So it is necessary to discuss propositions, their types, and their modalities. And they may be inferred by their contradictory, by their converse, and by the contradictory of their converse. For if they are sound, then their contradictory, their converse, and the contradictory of their converse are invalid. So we will discuss the contradiction of propositions, their simple conversion, and the contradictory of their converse. And the proposition is either categorical or conditional conjunctive or conditional disjunctive. So it is divided.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the foundational principles of Aristotelian logic as presented by Ibn Taymiyya. It is not a refutation but a clear exposition of the logicians' own framework, which he will later critique. The passage proceeds in the following order:

First, it states that logic is built upon the discussion of definition and syllogism. It explains the epistemological basis: knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), each being either immediate (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). The theoretical requires the immediate to avoid circularity or infinite regress. The path to conception is definition (hadd), and the path to assent is syllogism (qiyas).

Second, it explains the types of definition: real (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi). There is disagreement among logicians on which types count as 'definition'. The author notes that this is a matter of convention among different groups.

Third, it discusses the types of syllogism based on material: certain (demonstrative), accepted (dialectical), widely known (rhetorical), imaginative (poetic), and deceptive (sophistical). Some overlapping occurs, and some identify the rhetorical as probable or persuasive. The author notes that many later logicians departed from Aristotle.

Fourth, it explains that a real definition is composed of essential attributes, otherwise it must be composed of the five universals: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw'), accident ('arad 'aam), and property (khaassa). It clarifies the relations: shared essential is genus, distinguishing essential is differentia, their combination is species; shared accidental is accident, distinguishing accidental is property. It also notes that 'property' and 'species' can be used in relative senses.

Fifth, it introduces the five universals as a set, and contrasts the universal with the particular (juz'i), which cannot be shared in conception.

Finally, it outlines the need to discuss propositions (qadaya), their types (affirmative/negative, universal/particular), modalities, and immediate inferences (contradiction, conversion, contrapositive). It ends by noting that a proposition can be categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya), either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila).

The entire passage serves as a concise primer on the technical vocabulary of logic, which a student must master before understanding Ibn Taymiyya's subsequent critique.

التصديق
بديهي
نظري

Checkpoint

A) Because then there would be no need for logic.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge must be grounded in immediate truths to avoid circularity or infinite regress; logic provides the necessary path from immediate to theoretical by using definition and syllogism.

Types of Definition (Hadd) in Logic
Author presents the types of definition and logicians' disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi) definitions, and identify which types logicians include under the term 'hadd'.

Core Idea
'Hadd' is a contested term: it can include real, descriptive, and verbal definitions, or only some of them.

The passage explores the term 'hadd' (definition) and its subdivisions. It notes that logicians disagree on whether 'hadd' refers only to real definition (al-haqiqi), or also includes descriptive (al-rasmi) and verbal (al-lafzi). The author mentions this is a matter of convention among different schools, with some including all three, others only real and descriptive, and others only real. Understanding this prepares us to evaluate later critiques.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of 'hadd'
The passage says 'الحد اسم جامع لكل ما يعرف التصور' (definition is a comprehensive name for everything that makes conception known). Note that it is presented as 'al-qawl al-sharih' (explanatory speech).
2
Identify the three types listed
Look for the terms 'الحقيقي' (real), 'الرسمي' (descriptive), and 'اللفظي' (verbal). These are the three subdivisions of definition.
Real definition gives the essence; descriptive definition gives distinguishing accidental features; verbal definition explains the meaning of a word.
3
Map the disagreements
The author states that some groups restrict 'hadd' to real only, some include real and descriptive, some include all three. Note that this is a matter of convention (اصطلاح).
The author has elaborated elsewhere on which groups hold which view.

Types of Syllogism by Material
Author presents the classification of syllogisms by material quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify syllogisms based on the epistemological quality of their premises (certain, accepted, widely known, imaginative, deceptive) and identify their corresponding logical arts.

Core Idea
The value of a syllogism depends on its material: certain premises give demonstrative certainty; other types yield dialectical, rhetorical, poetic, or sophistical arguments.

The passage classifies syllogisms not by their form but by the quality of their material (madda). Demonstrative syllogism uses certain premises; dialectical uses accepted premises; rhetorical uses widely known; poetic uses imaginative; sophistical uses deceptive. This classification is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where the certainty of premises determines the value of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types of syllogistic material
The passage lists: يقينية (certain), مسلمة (accepted), مشهورة (widely known), مخيلة (imaginative), مموهة (deceptive). Find these terms in the Arabic.
These correspond to the five logical arts: demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetic, sophistical.
2
Understand the naming of each syllogism
Each material gives its name to the syllogism: برهاني for certain, جدلي for accepted, خطابي for widely known, شعري for imaginative, سوفسطائي for deceptive.
The passage notes that demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical may overlap.
3
Note the additional variations
Some scholars identify the rhetorical as 'probable' (ظني) or 'persuasive' (إقناعي). This shows further nuance.
The author notes that many later logicians departed from Aristotle's conventions.

كما قد يعبر بـ «النوع» عن الأنواع الإضافية التي هي بالنسبة إلى ما فوقها «نوع» ، وبالنسبة إلى ما تحتها (جنس). ولكن هذا وأمثاله من جزئيات المنطق التي ليس هنا المقصود الكلام فيها، فإن للكلام على ما ذكروه في «الجنس» و «النوع» مقاماً آخر ، غير ما عُلِّق في هذه العُجالة . فهذه الكليات الخمس». وبإزاء الكلّي «الجزئي»، وهو ما يُمْنَع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . والكلام في المركب مسبوق بالكلام على المفرد» و «دلالة اللفظ عليه. و «القياس» مؤلّف من مقدمتين». والمقدمة «قضيّة» إما «موجبة» وإما «سالبة» . وكل منهما إما «كلّيّة) وإما جزئية» . فلا بد من الكلام في «القضايا» وأنواعها وجهاتها . وقد يستدل عليها بـ «نقيضها»، وبـ «عکسها»، وبـ عکس نقيضها»; They built logic upon discourse on definition and its type, and demonstrative syllogism and its type. They said: The subject matter of logic is that knowledge is only either conception or assent, and each of them is either immediate or theoretical. For it is known that not everything is immediate, nor can everything be theoretical because the theoretical requires the immediate, thus necessitating circular reasoning or infinite regress in causes — which here are the means of knowledge, i.e., the proofs — and both are impossible. And the theoretical of them must have a path by which it is attained; the path by which conception is attained is 'definition', and the path by which assent is attained is 'syllogism'. So 'definition' is a comprehensive name for everything that makes conception known, and it is 'explanatory speech', so it includes the subdivisions: real definition, descriptive definition, and verbal definition; or it is specifically real definition, and descriptive definition is paired with it, while verbal definition is not of this category; or definition is a name for real and descriptive definition excluding verbal definition. For each type of these three divisions is a convention of a group among them, as I have elaborated and mentioned their names elsewhere. And the syllogism: if its material is certain, it is specifically 'demonstrative'; if it is 'accepted' (in dialectic), it is 'dialectical'; if it is 'widely known', it is 'rhetorical'; if it is 'imaginative', it is 'poetic', with regard to materials; and if it is 'deceptive', it is 'sophistical'. Hence demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical may overlap; and some people make the rhetorical the 'probable', while others make it 'persuasive'. And they have other conventions, some agreeing with the convention of the First Teacher Aristotle, and some disagreeing with him. For many authors among them departed in many matters from the method of their First Teacher. But the intention here is not to elaborate this. 'Definition' is composed of essential attributes if it is a real definition, otherwise it must be composed of the five universals. And each of them is either shared between the defined and others, or it is distinguishing for it from others. The shared essential is 'genus', the distinguishing essential is 'differentia', and the combination of the two is 'species'. The shared accidental is 'accident', and the distinguishing accidental is 'property'. And 'property' may be used for what occurs to the species even if it is not common to all its individuals, but that property does not provide distinction. Similarly, 'species' may be used for relative species that are species relative to what is above them and genus relative to what is below them. But this and its like are details of logic that are not the intended topic of discussion here, for discussion of what they mentioned about genus and species has another place beyond what is recorded in this brief note. So these are the five universals. Opposite the universal is the 'particular', which is that the conception of which prevents the occurrence of sharing in it. Discourse on the composite is preceded by discourse on the simple term and the indication of the word to it. And the syllogism is composed of two premises. The premise is a proposition, either affirmative or negative; each of them is either universal or particular. So it is necessary to discuss propositions, their types, and their modalities. And they may be inferred by their contradictory, by their converse, and by the contradictory of their converse. For if they are sound, then their contradictory, their converse, and the contradictory of their converse are invalid. So we will discuss the contradiction of propositions, their simple conversion, and the contradictory of their converse. And the proposition is either categorical or conditional conjunctive or conditional disjunctive. So it is divided.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the foundational principles of Aristotelian logic as presented by Ibn Taymiyya. It is not a refutation but a clear exposition of the logicians' own framework, which he will later critique. The passage proceeds in the following order:

First, it states that logic is built upon the discussion of definition and syllogism. It explains the epistemological basis: knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), each being either immediate (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). The theoretical requires the immediate to avoid circularity or infinite regress. The path to conception is definition (hadd), and the path to assent is syllogism (qiyas).

Second, it explains the types of definition: real (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi). There is disagreement among logicians on which types count as 'definition'. The author notes that this is a matter of convention among different groups.

Third, it discusses the types of syllogism based on material: certain (demonstrative), accepted (dialectical), widely known (rhetorical), imaginative (poetic), and deceptive (sophistical). Some overlapping occurs, and some identify the rhetorical as probable or persuasive. The author notes that many later logicians departed from Aristotle.

Fourth, it explains that a real definition is composed of essential attributes, otherwise it must be composed of the five universals: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw'), accident ('arad 'aam), and property (khaassa). It clarifies the relations: shared essential is genus, distinguishing essential is differentia, their combination is species; shared accidental is accident, distinguishing accidental is property. It also notes that 'property' and 'species' can be used in relative senses.

Fifth, it introduces the five universals as a set, and contrasts the universal with the particular (juz'i), which cannot be shared in conception.

Finally, it outlines the need to discuss propositions (qadaya), their types (affirmative/negative, universal/particular), modalities, and immediate inferences (contradiction, conversion, contrapositive). It ends by noting that a proposition can be categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya), either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila).

The entire passage serves as a concise primer on the technical vocabulary of logic, which a student must master before understanding Ibn Taymiyya's subsequent critique.

Key Takeaway
The certainty of a syllogism's conclusion is determined by the epistemological quality of its premises, not just its form.

?

The Five Universals: A Foundational Tool for Definition
Author presents the five universals and their classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five universals (genus, differentia, species, accident, property) and classify each as essential or accidental, shared or distinguishing.

Core Idea
The five universals provide the building blocks for every complete definition: two essential (genus and differentia) and three accidental (species, accident, property).

The passage introduces the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams) as the components of definition. It explains that a real definition is composed of essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya), otherwise it is made up of the five universals. Each universal is classified as either shared (mushtarak) or distinguishing (mumayyiz), and either essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi). Genus and differentia are essential; species combines them. Accident and property are accidental. This taxonomy is vital for constructing and critiquing definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The universals are classified by two criteria: essential vs. accidental, and shared vs. distinguishing. Find these categories in the Arabic.
Essential = ذاتي, accidental = عرضي; shared = مشترك, distinguishing = مميز.
2
Map each universal to its category
Shared essential is genus (جنس), distinguishing essential is differentia (فصل), their combination is species (نوع). Shared accidental is accident (عرض عام), distinguishing accidental is property (خاصة).
The passage also notes that 'property' and 'species' can be used in relative senses.
3
Understand the role in definition
A real definition (hadd haqiqi) uses the essential attributes: genus and differentia. Other definitions use the five universals. Recognize which universals are necessary for a proper definition.
The passage states that if a definition is not real, it must be composed of the five universals.

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the foundational principles of Aristotelian logic as presented by Ibn Taymiyya. It is not a refutation but a clear exposition of the logicians' own framework, which he will later critique. The passage proceeds in the following order:

Disagreement on the Meaning of Qiyas
Author reports the disagreement on the term 'qiyas'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on the definition of qiyas and explain the difference between qiyas al-shumul and qiyas al-tamthil.

Core Idea
The term 'qiyas' in Islamic logic is contested, not fixed.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports a fundamental disagreement: does the word 'qiyas' refer to both analogical reasoning and categorical syllogism, or is it restricted to one? Understanding this dispute is crucial for grasping how different scholars frame logic and its relationship to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qiyas
The passage contrasts 'qiyas al-tamthil' (analogy) and 'qiyas al-shumul' (categorical syllogism). Define each: tamthil draws a conclusion from a single shared attribute; shumul uses a universal middle term.
These are distinct logical operations, though both are called qiyas.
2
Identify the three positions
The majority says qiyas applies to both literally; al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi say it is literal for tamthil and metaphorical for shumul; Ibn Hazm and others say the opposite.
The author groups Ibn Hazm with 'logicians and theologians'.
3
Understand the implications
The dispute shows that technical terminology is not fixed; this undermines the claim that logical terms have precise, univocal meanings.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to criticize the logicians' reliance on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكرنا تنازع الناس في اسم «القياس » ، هل يتناولهما جميعاً، كما عليه جمهورُ الناس؛ أو هو حقيقةٌ في التمثيل» مجاز في «قياس الشمول»، كما اختاره أبو حامد الغَزَالي، وأبو محمد المقدسي؛ أو بالعكس، كما اختاره ابن حَزْمٍ وغيره من أهل المنطق والكلام على هذا مبسوط في مواضع.
And we have mentioned the people's dispute over the term 'qiyas': whether it encompasses both [types] together, as the majority of people hold; or it is literal in 'tamthil' and metaphorical in 'qiyas shumul', as chosen by Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and Abu Muhammad al-Maqdisi; or the opposite, as chosen by Ibn Hazm and others among the logicians and theologians. The discussion on this is elaborated in various places.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement (tanazu') among Islamic scholars regarding the correct usage of the word 'qiyas'. The majority (jumhur) use qiyas to include both the comprehensive syllogism and analogical reasoning. However, some logicians and theologians restrict the term to one type: al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi consider it (haqiqa) in tamthil (analogy) and metaphorical (majaz) in shumul (categorical), while Ibn Hazm and others take the opposite position. This debate shows that the technical terminology of logic is not universally agreed upon. Ibn Taymiyya uses this to undermine the logicians' claim to a fixed, precise vocabulary.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): The term is disputed; some restrict it to analogy, others to categorical syllogism, others include both. [Note: In fiqh, qiyas almost always means analogical reasoning; in logic, it often means categorical syllogism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view did Ibn Hazm hold on the term 'qiyas'?
A) It applies to both types literally.
B) It is literal for tamthil and metaphorical for shumul.
C) It is literal for shumul and metaphorical for tamthil.
D) It is a purely figurative term.

Key Takeaway
The term 'qiyas' in Islamic thought has historically been disputed between referring to analogical reasoning (tamthil) and categorical syllogism (shumul), with three distinct positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A fiqh scholar says: 'I am using qiyas to prove this ruling.' Another scholar objects: 'That is not qiyas, that is istihsan.' Based on the passage, what might the first scholar mean by 'qiyas'? (Answer: Possibly qiyas al-tamthil, analogical reasoning.)
?
Identify which position each scholar represents: Al-Ghazali, Ibn Hazm, and the majority. Then explain in one sentence why their disagreement matters for the authority of logic.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logicians' Unproven Negation
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' claim is an unproven negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that non-obvious conceptualizations require definition and explain why he considers it an unproven negation.

Core Idea
The logicians' foundational claim is a negation without proof, undermining their entire system.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches his attack on the very foundation of Aristotelian logic: the claim that all non-obvious concepts are attained only through definition. He points out that this claim itself is a negative statement, and it is not self-evident. The logicians have provided no proof for it. Therefore, their entire instrument of thought is built on an unsupported assertion. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that non-obvious conceptualizations (tasawwurat ghayr badihiyya) are only attained by definition (hadd). This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Classify the claim as a negative proposition
Ibn Taymiyya notes that this is a negative statement (qadiyya saliba): it negates the possibility of attaining conceptualizations by any other means. Negative propositions require proof just like affirmative ones.
3
Assess the self-evidence of the claim
The claim is not self-evident (badihi) – it is not immediately obvious to every rational person. Therefore, it falls under the category of theoretical (nazari) matters requiring demonstration.
4
Demand proof for the claim
Since the claim is neither self-evident nor proven, it amounts to a statement without knowledge (qawl bila 'ilm). Ibn Taymiyya asks: 'Min ayna lahum dhalik?' – from where do they have that?
5
Reflect on the implication
If the very foundation of logic is an unproven claim, then the entire system claiming to be an instrument protecting the mind from error is itself based on error. This is a devastating reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : المقام السَّلبي في «الحدود والتصورات» في قولهم : إن التصورات غير البديهية لا تنال إلا بالحد» والكلام على هذا من وجوه : أحدها: أن يقال : لا ريب أن النافي عليه الدليل، إذا لم يكن نفيه بديهياً، كما أن المثبت الدليل. فالقضية - سواء كانت سلبية أو إيجابية - إذا لم تكن بديهية فلا بد لها من دليل . وأما السَّلب بلا علم فهو قول بلا علم فقول القائل إنه لا تحصل هذه التصورات إلا بالحد قضية سالبة، وليست بديهية. فمن أين لهم ذلك ! وإذا كان هذا قولاً بلا علم كان في أوّل ما أسسوه القولُ بلا علم فكيف يكون القول بلا علم أساساً لميزان العلم ولما يزعمون أنه آلة قانونية تَعْصِمُ مراعاتُها الذهن أن يَزِلّ في فكره» ؟
The first station: the negative station regarding definitions and conceptualizations, regarding their statement that non-obvious conceptualizations are not attained except by definition. And the discussion on this has several aspects: One of them: that it is said: there is no doubt that the one who negates must have proof, if his negation is not obvious, just as the one who affirms must have proof. So a proposition - whether negative or affirmative - if it is not obvious, must have proof. And as for negation without knowledge, it is a statement without knowledge. So the statement of the one who says 'these conceptualizations are not obtained except by definition' is a negative proposition, and it is not obvious. So from where do they have that? And if this is a statement without knowledge, then at the very foundation of what they have built is a statement without knowledge. So how can a statement without knowledge be the basis for the scale of knowledge and for what they claim is a legal instrument whose observance protects the mind from slipping in its thinking?
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the first argument within the first negative station. Ibn Taymiyya attacks the foundational claim of logic: that non-axiomatic conceptualizations (tasawwurat ghayr badihiyya) can only be obtained through definition. He points out that this claim is a negative statement (qadiyya saliba) and is not self-evident (badihiyya). Therefore, it requires proof. The logicians have provided no proof. Hence, their entire edifice is built on a statement without knowledge (qawl bila 'ilm). Ibn Taymiyya highlights the irony: the very instrument that is supposed to protect the mind from error is itself based on an unsupported claim. This is a devastating critique: if the foundation is unsound, the entire system collapses.

Key Terms
بديهي
قضية سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is a negative statement that is not self-evident and lacks proof
C) Because it is a self-evident truth
D) Because it is based on sense perception

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the logicians' core claim is an unproven negation, thus undermining their claim to provide a sound instrument for knowledge.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Definitions Only Distinguish, They Do Not Convey Essences
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that definitions produce conceptual knowledge and presents the theologians' view that definitions merely distinguish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the theological position on definitions (hadd) and distinguish it from the Greek logicians' view, and trace the historical entry of Greek logic into Islamic thought via Ghazali.

Core Idea
Definitions distinguish; they do not reveal essences.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies a fundamental disagreement between Islamic theologians and Greek logicians regarding the purpose of definitions. While logicians claim definitions convey the reality of a thing, the theologians insist definitions merely serve to distinguish one thing from others. Ibn Taymiyya also traces how the Greek view entered Islamic discourse through Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, whom he criticizes for adopting Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views on definitions
Find where the author states that the benefit (fa'ida) of a definition is tamyiz (distinction) and contrasts it with the claim of Greek logicians that definitions provide taswir (depiction of reality).
2
Locate the historical account of Ghazali's role
The author criticizes Ghazali for introducing Greek logical views on definitions into Islamic thought through works like al-Mustasfa and al-Qistas al-Mustaqim, and for falsely claiming prophetic origins.
3
Examine the list of theologians who upheld the tamyiz view
Note the diversity: Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and others. This shows a wide consensus against the Greek view.
4
Understand the restriction on definitional components
The mutakallimun require definitions to be coextensive and coexclusive (طرداً وعكساً) and forbid including common elements like genus or general accident. They define by properties that uniquely distinguish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الإيجابي في «الحدود والتصورات) فيقال : المحققون من النظار يعلمون أن الحد فائدته التمييز بين المحدود وغيره» الفرق بين طريقتي المتكلمين والمنطقيين في الحدود كالاسم. ليس فائدته تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته»؛ وإنما يدعي هذا أهل المنطق اليوناني، أتباع أرِسْطُو، ومن سلك سبيلهم وحذا حذوهم تقليداً لهم من الإسلاميين وغيرهم . فأما جماهير أهل النظر والكلام من المسلمين وغيرهم فعلى خلاف هذا. وإنما دخل هذا في كلام من تكلم في أصول الدين والفقه» بعد أبي حامد في أواخر المئة الخامسة وأوائل المئة السادسة. فإن أبا حامدٍ وَضَعَ مقدمة منطقية في أوّل «المستصفى»، وزعم أن من لم يُحِطْ بها علماً فلا ثقة له بشيء من/ علومه. وصنف في ذلك مِحَكَ النظر» و «مغيار العلم»؛ و دواماً اشتدّت به ثقته. وأعجَبُ من ذلك أنه وضع الكلام على كتابا ] (۲) سمّاه القِسْطاس» «المستقيم ونسبه إلى أنه تعلمه من الأنبياء، وإنما تعلمه من ابن سينا، وابن سينا تعلّمه من كتب أرسطو . وهؤلاء الذين تكلموا في «الأصول» بعد أبي حامد هم الذين تكلموا في الحدود» بطريقة أهل المنطق اليوناني . وأما سائر طوائف النظار [۱۲] من جميع الطوائف - المعتزلة، والأشعرية، والكرَّاميَّة، والشيعة، وغيرهم ممن صنف في هذا الشأن من أتباع الأئمة الأربعة وغيرهم المحدود وغيره ـ فعندهم إنما تفيد الحدودُ «التمييز بين المحدود وغيره . بل أكثرهم لا يسوغون الحدَّ إلا بما يميز المحدود عن غيره ، ولا يجوزون أن يُذكر في الحد ما يعم المحدود وغيره، «جنساً» أو «عرَضاً عاماً ، وإنما يحدون بما يلازم المحدود «طرداً وعكساً» سواء سمي «فصلا» و «خاصة» ونحو ذلك مما يتميز به المحدود من غيره . وهذا مشهور في كتب أهل النظر في مواضع يطول وصفها من كتب المتكلمين من أهل الإثبات ،وغيرهم كأبي الحسن الأشعري والقاضي أبي بكر، وأبي إسحاق، وأبي بكر بن فُوْرَك ، والقاضي أبي يعلى وابن عقيل وأبي المعالي الجويني، وأبي الميمون النسفي الحنفي، وغيرهم ؛ وقبلهم أبو علي ، وأبو هاشم، وعبد الجبار، وأمثالهم من شيوخ المعتزلة ؛ وكذلك ابن النُّوْبَخْت، والمُوسوي ، والطوسي، وغيرهم من شيوخ الشيعة؛ وكذلك محمد بن الهَيْصَمْ ، وغيره من شيوخ الكرامية. فإنهم إذا تكلموا في «الحدّ» قالوا: إن حدَّ الشيء وحقيقته خاصَّتُه التي تميّزه . قال أبو القاسم الأنصاري في «شرح الإرشاد»
The positive position regarding 'definitions and conceptualizations.' It is said: The investigators among the thinkers know that the benefit of a definition is the distinction between the defined and other things. The difference between the method of the theologians and the logicians in definitions: it is like a name. Its benefit is not to depict the defined and to define its reality; rather, this is claimed by the Greek logicians, followers of Aristotle, and those who follow their path and imitate them from among Muslims and others. As for the masses of the people of speculation and theology from among Muslims and others, they are contrary to this. This only entered into the speech of those who spoke in the principles of religion and jurisprudence after Abu Hamid in the late fifth century and early sixth century. Indeed, Abu Hamid placed a logical introduction at the beginning of 'al-Mustasfa,' and claimed that whoever does not encompass it with knowledge has no confidence in any of his sciences. He authored regarding that 'Mihakk al-Nazar' and 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm'; and his confidence in it became extremely strong. And more astonishing than that, he placed the discourse in a book he named 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim' and attributed it to having learned it from the prophets, whereas he only learned it from Ibn Sina, and Ibn Sina learned it from the books of Aristotle. Those who spoke in 'al-Usul' after Abu Hamid are the ones who spoke about 'definitions' along the method of Greek logic. As for the remaining groups of thinkers from all sects – the Mu'tazila, the Ash'ariyya, the Karramiyya, the Shi'a, and others among those who authored in this matter from the followers of the four Imams and others – according to them, definitions only provide 'distinction between the defined and other things.' Moreover, most of them do not allow a definition except by what distinguishes the defined from other things, and they do not permit mentioning in the definition what is common to the defined and other things, whether it is called 'genus' or 'general accident,' but rather they define by what is concomitant with the defined 'by coextensiveness and coexclusiveness,' whether it is called 'differentia' or 'property' and the like, by which the defined is distinguished from other things. This is well-known in the books of the people of speculation in places too lengthy to describe, from the books of the theologians of the affirmers and others, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, Abu Ishaq, Abu Bakr ibn Furak, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, Abu al-Maimun al-Nasafi al-Hanafi, and others; and before them Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and their peers from the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila; and likewise Ibn al-Nawbakht, al-Musawi, al-Tusi, and others from the shaykhs of the Shi'a; and likewise Muhammad ibn al-Haytham and others from the shaykhs of the Karramiyya. For when they spoke about 'definition,' they said: The definition of a thing and its reality is its property that distinguishes it. Abu al-Qasim al-Ansari said in 'Sharh al-Irshad':
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the logicians' doctrine regarding definitions. The author, Ibn Taymiyya, first states the correct view: the benefit of a definition is merely to distinguish the defined from other things (tamyiz). He contrasts this with the Greek logicians' claim that definitions depict the reality of the defined (taswir). He then provides a historical critique, pointing out that the Greek logical approach to definitions entered Islamic thought through Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, who introduced it in works like al-Mustasfa and even absurdly claimed to have learned the 'balanced scale' from prophets when in fact he took it from Ibn Sina and Aristotle. The mainstream theologians (mutakallimun) from all sects—Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Karramiyya, Shi'a, etc.—held that definitions only distinguish. They do not allow in a definition anything common to the defined and others (like a genus or general accident), but restrict it to what is coextensive and coexclusive with the defined, whether called differentia or property. The author then lists numerous prominent theologians from each group to emphasize consensus. This passage guards against the misconception that definitions can convey the essence of a thing; rather, they function like a name that identifies but does not describe inner reality. In Islamic law, for example, defining 'khamr' as 'intoxicating drink' distinguishes it from other beverages but does not explain its substance.

Key Terms
الحد
التمييز
التصوير
المتكلمون

A) To convey the essence (mahiyya) of the thing.
B) To distinguish the defined from other things.
C) To provide a detailed description of the thing's parts.
D) To prove the existence of the thing.

Key Takeaway
Theologians hold that definitions only distinguish; they do not reveal essences, and the Greek view entered Islam via Ghazali's adoption of Aristotelian logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
