What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
6 min
The Mutakallimun's Condition for Definition: Exclusivity
Exposing the mutakallimun's exclusive-coextension requirement for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a definition satisfies the mutakallimun's condition of exclusivity (tard wa 'aks).

Core Idea
A definition must mirror the defined: apply to all and only it.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya explains the mutakallimun's distinctive approach to definition. Unlike logicians who require genus and differentia, the mutakallimun demand that a definition be coextensive (tard wa 'aks) – it must include every instance of the defined and exclude everything else. This stricter condition avoids including common elements that blur the boundary of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum (the term being defined) in the definition. For example, in the passage, the term 'knowledge' ('ilm) is discussed.
The definiendum is what we want to clarify.
2
Check for any common term
Examine if the definiens includes a term that applies to things outside the definiendum. The mutakallimun reject genus and common accident because they are not exclusive.
A genus like 'animal' applies to both humans and horses, so it is invalid.
3
Verify tard (coverage)
Ask: Does the definition apply to every single instance of the defined? If any instance lacks the property, tard fails.
E.g., if 'knowledge' is defined as 'awareness', every act of knowledge involves awareness, so tard holds.
4
Verify 'aks (exclusion)
Ask: Does the definition fail to apply to everything that is not the defined? If something outside the defined also has the property, 'aks fails.
If 'awareness' also applied to sense perception outside knowledge, it would fail 'aks.
5
Decide validity
If both tard and 'aks are satisfied, the definition is valid according to the mutakallimun. If not, it is invalid.
The passage uses the example of 'knowledge' to show how even a broad property can be made exclusive by specifying the most specific description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - فائدة الحد عند جميع المتكلمين التمييز بين المحدود وغيره إلا بما يميز المحدود عن غيره، ولا يجوزون أن يُذكر في الحد ما يعم المحدود وغيره، جنساً أو عرَضاً عاماً، وإنما يحدون بما يلازم المحدود طرداً وعكساً، سواء سمي فصلاً أو خاصة أو نحو ذلك مما يتميز به المحدود من غيره. وهذا مشهور في كتب أهل النظر في مواضع يطول وصفها من كتب المتكلمين من أهل الإثبات وغيرهم كأبي الحسن الأشعري والقاضي أبي بكر، وأبي إسحاق، وأبي بكر بن فُوْرَك، والقاضي أبي يعلى وابن عقيل وأبي المعالي الجويني، وأبي الميمون النسفي الحنفي، وغيرهم؛ وقبلهم أبو علي، وأبو هاشم، وعبد الجبار، وأمثالهم من شيوخ المعتزلة؛ وكذلك ابن النُّوْبَخْت، والمُوسوي، والطوسي، وغيرهم من شيوخ الشيعة؛ وكذلك محمد بن الهَيْصَمْ، وغيره من شيوخ الكرامية. فإنهم إذا تكلموا في «الحدّ» قالوا: إن حدَّ الشيء وحقيقته خاصَّتُه التي تميّزه. قال أبو القاسم الأنصاري في «شرح الإرشاد»: قال إمام الحرمين: القصد من التحديد في اصطلاح المتكلمين «التعرض لخاصة الشيء وحَقِيقَتِهِ التي يقع بها الفصل بينه وبين غيره». قال الأستاذ: حدُّ الشيء «معناه الذي لأجله كان بالوصف المقصود بالذكر». قال أبو المعالي: ولو قال قائل حدُّ الشيء معناه واقتصر عليه كان سديداً، أو قال: حدّ الشيء حقيقته أو خاصته كان حسناً. قال: فإن قيل إذا قلتُم، حدُّ (العلم) أو حقيقته (ما يعلم به، فلم تذكروا خاصة العلم، لأن العلم يشتمل على مختلفات ومتماثلات لا تجتمع جميعها في خاصة واحدة، فإن المجتمعين في الأخص متماثلان». فنقولُ: إنما غرضُنا أن نبين أن المذكور حداً هو «خاصة وصف المحدود في مقصود الحدّ»، إذ ليس الغرضُ بالسؤال عن «العلم التعرُّضَ لتفصيله، وإنما الغرض معرفة العلميّة بأخص وصف العلم الذي يشترك فيه ما تختلف منه وما يتماثل بما ذكرناه، حيث قلنا إنه «المعرفة» أو «ما يعلم به» أو «التبيين». وهذا على طريقة الأستاذ. ومن رام ذكر حدّ في قبيل المعلومات فإنما غرضه الوقوفُ على صفة يشترك فيها القبيلُ المسئول عنه على وجه يتضح للسائل. قال أبو المعالي: فإن قيل الحدُّ يرجع إلى قول المخبر، أو إلى صفة في المحدود! قلنا: ما صار إليه كافَةُ الأئمة أن الحدَّ صفة المحدود، سكت عنه الواصفون أم نطقوا، وهو بمعنى الحقيقة». وقد ذكر القاضي في «التقريب» أن الحد قول الحادّ المنبيء عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود ووافق الأصحابُ في أنَّ حقيقة الشيء ومعناه راجعان إلى صفةٍ دون قول القائل، وإنما بُيِّن ذلك في الحد لمشابهته الوصف ومشابهة الحقيقة الصفة، ونحن نفصل بين الوصف والصفة. ثم قال القاضي من الأشياء ما يُحَدِّ، ومنها ما لا يُحَدِّ. وما من محقق إلا وله حقيقة. ومن صار إلى أن الحدّ يرجع إلى حقيقة المحدود يقول: ما من ذي حقيقة إلا وله حد.
Second section - The benefit of definition according to all mutakallimun is discrimination between the defined and other than it, only by what distinguishes the defined from other than it. They do not permit that what is common to the defined and other than it be mentioned in the definition, whether a genus or a common accident. Rather, they define by what is concomitant with the defined both exclusively and inclusively (tardan wa 'aksan), whether it is called differentia or property or the like by which the defined is distinguished from other than it. This is well-known in the books of the people of theoretical inquiry in places too lengthy to describe from the books of the mutakallimun, both the affirmers (of attributes) and others, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, Abu Ishaq, Abu Bakr ibn Furak, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, Abu al-Maimun al-Nasafi al-Hanafi, and others; and before them Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and their like from the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila; and likewise Ibn al-Nawbakht, al-Musawi, al-Tusi, and others from the shaykhs of the Shi'a; and likewise Muhammad ibn al-Haytham and others from the shaykhs of the Karramiyya. For when they speak of 'definition' they say: The definition of a thing and its reality is its exclusive property that distinguishes it. Abu al-Qasim al-Ansari said in his commentary on al-Irshad: Imam al-Haramayn said: The purpose of definition in the terminology of the mutakallimun is 'to address the exclusive property of the thing and its reality by which separation occurs between it and other than it.' The teacher (al-Ustadh) said: The definition of a thing is 'its meaning for which it is with the intended description.' Abu al-Ma'ali said: If someone said the definition of a thing is its meaning and limited himself to that, it would be correct, or if he said the definition of a thing is its reality or its exclusive property, it would be good. He said: If it is said: When you say the definition of 'knowledge' or its reality is 'that by which something is known,' you have not mentioned the exclusive property of knowledge, because knowledge includes different and similar things that do not all combine in one exclusive property, for those that combine in the more specific are similar. So we say: Our purpose is only to show that what is mentioned as a definition is 'the exclusive property of the description of the defined in the purpose of definition,' since the purpose of the question about 'knowledge' is not to address its detail, but rather the purpose is to know the knowingness by the most specific description of knowledge in which what differs and what is similar share by what we mentioned, where we said it is 'awareness' or 'that by which something is known' or 'clarification.' This is according to the method of the teacher. And whoever intends to mention a definition of a category of known things, his purpose is only to come upon a description that is shared by the category inquired about in a way that becomes clear to the questioner. Abu al-Ma'ali said: If it is said: Does the definition refer back to the statement of the informant or to a quality in the defined? We say: What the entirety of the imams have concluded is that the definition is a quality of the defined, whether the describers remain silent about it or speak, and it is in the sense of reality. And al-Qadi mentioned in al-Taqrib that the definition is the statement of the definer indicating the quality that is shared by the individuals of the defined, and the companions agreed that the reality of a thing and its meaning refer back to a quality, not to the statement of the speaker, and it was only clarified in definition due to its resemblance to description and the resemblance of reality to quality, and we distinguish between description and quality. Then al-Qadi said: Among things, some are definable and some are not. And there is no realized thing except that it has a reality. And whoever holds that the definition refers back to the reality of the defined says: There is nothing with a reality except that it has a definition.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'hadd' (definition) according to the mutakallimun (theologians) and clarifies its ontological status. The passage is expository: Ibn Taymiyya presents the view of the mutakallimun, supporting it with quotations from leading figures across various theological schools.

He begins by stating that for all mutakallimun, the purpose of definition is to distinguish the defined from everything else. Therefore, a definition must consist only of what exclusively applies to the defined (tardan wa 'aksan) — i.e., it must be coextensive, present whenever the defined is present and absent whenever it is absent. They reject the inclusion of any broader term, whether a genus (jins) or a common accident ('arad 'amm). They do not distinguish between differentia (fasl) and property (khassa) as long as it serves to distinguish.

Ibn Taymiyya then lists renowned mutakallimun from the Ash'ariyya, Mu'tazila, Shi'a, and Karramiyya who adhered to this view. When they discuss 'definition', they say that the definition of a thing and its reality (haqiqa) is its exclusive property (khassa) that distinguishes it.

He then quotes Abu al-Qasim al-Ansari from his commentary on al-Juwayni's al-Irshad. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni states that in the terminology of the mutakallimun, the purpose of definition is to present the exclusive property and reality of the thing by which it is separated from others. His teacher al-Ustadh (al-Isfarayini) said the definition is the meaning for which the thing exists with the intended description. Al-Juwayni adds that it is acceptable to simply say the definition is the meaning, reality, or exclusive property.

Next, a potential objection is raised: if you define knowledge ('ilm) as 'that by which something is known,' you have not mentioned a single exclusive property because knowledge includes both likes and unlikes. The response is that the purpose is to give the most specific description of the genus 'knowledge' by which all its instances are known, such as 'awareness' or 'clarification.' The goal is to provide a shared description that clarifies the category for the questioner.

Then the discussion shifts to the ontological status of the definition. Is it a statement (qawl) of the definer or a quality (sifa) inherent in the defined? The imams agree that the definition is a quality of the defined, regardless of whether anyone describes it. It is equivalent to the reality (haqiqa). Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) in al-Taqrib had said that the definition is the definer's statement that indicates the quality shared by the individuals, but he and the scholars concur that the reality and meaning of a thing refer back to a quality, not to the speaker's statement. The distinction is made between 'wasf' (description, a statement) and 'sifa' (quality, an attribute). Finally, al-Qadi notes that some things are definable, others not, but every realized thing has a reality, and whoever holds that definition refers to reality says that everything with a reality has a definition.

الخاصة
طرداً وعكساً

C) It must be a statement from a recognized authority.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun require that a definition be coextensive with the defined, rejecting any term that applies to other things.

What Is Mantiq?
advanced
7 min
Is the Definition a Statement or a Quality?
Resolving the ontological status of definition: quality vs. statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue whether a definition is a mere statement (qawl) or an inherent quality (sifa) based on the mutakallimun's consensus.

Core Idea
Definitions point to real attributes, not just words.

After establishing the condition of exclusivity, Ibn Taymiyya presents a deeper question: Is the definition itself a verbal statement, or does it refer to an actual attribute in the thing? The mutakallimun, as quoted through al-Juwayni and al-Qadi, agree that the definition is a quality (sifa) of the defined, not merely the speaker's description (wasf). This distinction has profound implications for how definitions relate to reality and essence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'wasf' and 'sifa'
Recognize that 'wasf' (description) is a verbal statement about an object, while 'sifa' (quality) is an actual attribute inherent in the object. The passage explicitly separates the two.
The mutakallimun insist that the definition (hadd) is a sifa, not a wasf.
2
Identify the two positions
One position: definition is the statement of the definer (qawl al-mukhbit). Another: definition is the quality in the defined. The passage presents al-Qadi's view that the definition is a statement that indicates the quality, but the reality (haqiqa) is the quality itself.
Al-Qadi's al-Taqrib reconciles the linguistic and ontological aspects.
3
Follow the consensus
Al-Juwayni states that the entirety of the imams have concluded that the definition is a quality of the defined, whether spoken or not. This means the definition is identical to the reality (haqiqa) of the thing.
The definition as a statement is merely a pointer; the true nature is the quality.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the definition of 'knowledge' as 'awareness'. According to the consensus, the real definition is the quality of awareness in the knower, not the words we utter. The words serve to indicate that quality.
This example is discussed in the passage itself.
5
Reflect on the implication
If definition is a quality, then defining something is discovering its real essence. If definition were merely a statement, it could be arbitrary. The mutakallimun tie definition firmly to ontology.
This aligns with the Islamic emphasis on reality-based knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - فائدة الحد عند جميع المتكلمين التمييز بين المحدود وغيره إلا بما يميز المحدود عن غيره، ولا يجوزون أن يُذكر في الحد ما يعم المحدود وغيره، جنساً أو عرَضاً عاماً، وإنما يحدون بما يلازم المحدود طرداً وعكساً، سواء سمي فصلاً أو خاصة أو نحو ذلك مما يتميز به المحدود من غيره. وهذا مشهور في كتب أهل النظر في مواضع يطول وصفها من كتب المتكلمين من أهل الإثبات وغيرهم كأبي الحسن الأشعري والقاضي أبي بكر، وأبي إسحاق، وأبي بكر بن فُوْرَك، والقاضي أبي يعلى وابن عقيل وأبي المعالي الجويني، وأبي الميمون النسفي الحنفي، وغيرهم؛ وقبلهم أبو علي، وأبو هاشم، وعبد الجبار، وأمثالهم من شيوخ المعتزلة؛ وكذلك ابن النُّوْبَخْت، والمُوسوي، والطوسي، وغيرهم من شيوخ الشيعة؛ وكذلك محمد بن الهَيْصَمْ، وغيره من شيوخ الكرامية. فإنهم إذا تكلموا في «الحدّ» قالوا: إن حدَّ الشيء وحقيقته خاصَّتُه التي تميّزه. قال أبو القاسم الأنصاري في «شرح الإرشاد»: قال إمام الحرمين: القصد من التحديد في اصطلاح المتكلمين «التعرض لخاصة الشيء وحَقِيقَتِهِ التي يقع بها الفصل بينه وبين غيره». قال الأستاذ: حدُّ الشيء «معناه الذي لأجله كان بالوصف المقصود بالذكر». قال أبو المعالي: ولو قال قائل حدُّ الشيء معناه واقتصر عليه كان سديداً، أو قال: حدّ الشيء حقيقته أو خاصته كان حسناً. قال: فإن قيل إذا قلتُم، حدُّ (العلم) أو حقيقته (ما يعلم به، فلم تذكروا خاصة العلم، لأن العلم يشتمل على مختلفات ومتماثلات لا تجتمع جميعها في خاصة واحدة، فإن المجتمعين في الأخص متماثلان». فنقولُ: إنما غرضُنا أن نبين أن المذكور حداً هو «خاصة وصف المحدود في مقصود الحدّ»، إذ ليس الغرضُ بالسؤال عن «العلم التعرُّضَ لتفصيله، وإنما الغرض معرفة العلميّة بأخص وصف العلم الذي يشترك فيه ما تختلف منه وما يتماثل بما ذكرناه، حيث قلنا إنه «المعرفة» أو «ما يعلم به» أو «التبيين». وهذا على طريقة الأستاذ. ومن رام ذكر حدّ في قبيل المعلومات فإنما غرضه الوقوفُ على صفة يشترك فيها القبيلُ المسئول عنه على وجه يتضح للسائل. قال أبو المعالي: فإن قيل الحدُّ يرجع إلى قول المخبر، أو إلى صفة في المحدود! قلنا: ما صار إليه كافَةُ الأئمة أن الحدَّ صفة المحدود، سكت عنه الواصفون أم نطقوا، وهو بمعنى الحقيقة». وقد ذكر القاضي في «التقريب» أن الحد قول الحادّ المنبيء عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود ووافق الأصحابُ في أنَّ حقيقة الشيء ومعناه راجعان إلى صفةٍ دون قول القائل، وإنما بُيِّن ذلك في الحد لمشابهته الوصف ومشابهة الحقيقة الصفة، ونحن نفصل بين الوصف والصفة. ثم قال القاضي من الأشياء ما يُحَدِّ، ومنها ما لا يُحَدِّ. وما من محقق إلا وله حقيقة. ومن صار إلى أن الحدّ يرجع إلى حقيقة المحدود يقول: ما من ذي حقيقة إلا وله حد.
Second section - The benefit of definition according to all mutakallimun is discrimination between the defined and other than it, only by what distinguishes the defined from other than it. They do not permit that what is common to the defined and other than it be mentioned in the definition, whether a genus or a common accident. Rather, they define by what is concomitant with the defined both exclusively and inclusively (tardan wa 'aksan), whether it is called differentia or property or the like by which the defined is distinguished from other than it. This is well-known in the books of the people of theoretical inquiry in places too lengthy to describe from the books of the mutakallimun, both the affirmers (of attributes) and others, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, Abu Ishaq, Abu Bakr ibn Furak, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, Abu al-Maimun al-Nasafi al-Hanafi, and others; and before them Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and their like from the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila; and likewise Ibn al-Nawbakht, al-Musawi, al-Tusi, and others from the shaykhs of the Shi'a; and likewise Muhammad ibn al-Haytham and others from the shaykhs of the Karramiyya. For when they speak of 'definition' they say: The definition of a thing and its reality is its exclusive property that distinguishes it. Abu al-Qasim al-Ansari said in his commentary on al-Irshad: Imam al-Haramayn said: The purpose of definition in the terminology of the mutakallimun is 'to address the exclusive property of the thing and its reality by which separation occurs between it and other than it.' The teacher (al-Ustadh) said: The definition of a thing is 'its meaning for which it is with the intended description.' Abu al-Ma'ali said: If someone said the definition of a thing is its meaning and limited himself to that, it would be correct, or if he said the definition of a thing is its reality or its exclusive property, it would be good. He said: If it is said: When you say the definition of 'knowledge' or its reality is 'that by which something is known,' you have not mentioned the exclusive property of knowledge, because knowledge includes different and similar things that do not all combine in one exclusive property, for those that combine in the more specific are similar. So we say: Our purpose is only to show that what is mentioned as a definition is 'the exclusive property of the description of the defined in the purpose of definition,' since the purpose of the question about 'knowledge' is not to address its detail, but rather the purpose is to know the knowingness by the most specific description of knowledge in which what differs and what is similar share by what we mentioned, where we said it is 'awareness' or 'that by which something is known' or 'clarification.' This is according to the method of the teacher. And whoever intends to mention a definition of a category of known things, his purpose is only to come upon a description that is shared by the category inquired about in a way that becomes clear to the questioner. Abu al-Ma'ali said: If it is said: Does the definition refer back to the statement of the informant or to a quality in the defined? We say: What the entirety of the imams have concluded is that the definition is a quality of the defined, whether the describers remain silent about it or speak, and it is in the sense of reality. And al-Qadi mentioned in al-Taqrib that the definition is the statement of the definer indicating the quality that is shared by the individuals of the defined, and the companions agreed that the reality of a thing and its meaning refer back to a quality, not to the statement of the speaker, and it was only clarified in definition due to its resemblance to description and the resemblance of reality to quality, and we distinguish between description and quality. Then al-Qadi said: Among things, some are definable and some are not. And there is no realized thing except that it has a reality. And whoever holds that the definition refers back to the reality of the defined says: There is nothing with a reality except that it has a definition.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

The Purpose of Hadd: Capturing the Specific Property and Reality
Imam al-Haramayn defines the purpose of hadd as mentioning the specific property and reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the mutakallimun's purpose of hadd and identify its core components (khassa and haqiqa).

Core Idea
The hadd is the thing's own essence, not just our description.

Imam al-Haramayn opens this discussion by stating the very purpose of definition in the terminology of the mutakallimun. Understanding that purpose is essential before we can evaluate any definition. This lesson examines that foundational statement and what it means for a definition to capture 'the specific property and reality' of a thing.

Guided Path
1
Locate the purpose statement
Find where Imam al-Haramayn explicitly states the purpose of definition. It begins with 'al-qasdu min al-tahdid...'
He says the purpose is 'to mention the specific property and the reality by which the thing is distinguished from others.'
2
Define khassa and haqiqa
In this context, 'khassa' is the distinctive characteristic that sets the thing apart, while 'haqiqa' is its essence or reality. They are used almost interchangeably.
These terms are central to the mutakallimun's understanding of hadd.
3
See the example of 'ilm
The passage later uses knowledge ('ilm) as an example. The proposed definitions 'ma'rifa' or 'tabyin' are intended to be the most specific shared property that makes knowledge what it is.
This shows how the purpose applies in practice.
4
Distinguish from enumeration
The mutakallimun do not aim to list all attributes; they aim to capture the one essential property that unifies all instances and distinguishes the thing from others.
A common misunderstanding is that a definition must be exhaustive; here it is about precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الحد قال إمام الحرمين : القصد من التحديد في اصطلاح المتكلمين «التعرض لخاصة الشيء وحَقِيقَتِهِ التي يقع بها الفصل بينه وبين غيره . قال الأستاذ : حدُّ الشيء «معناه الذي لأجله كان بالوصف المقصود بالذكر . قال أبو المعالي : ولو قال قائل حدُّ الشيء معناه واقتصر عليه كان سديداً، أو قال : حدّ الشيء حقيقته أو خاصته كان حسناً . قال : فإن قيل إذا قلتُم ، حدُّ (العلم) أو حقيقته (ما يعلم به، فلم تذكروا خاصة العلم، لأن العلم يشتمل على مختلفات ومتماثلات لا تجتمع جميعها في خاصة واحدة، فإن المجتمعين في الأخص متماثلان». فنقولُ : إنما غرضُنا أن نبين أن المذكور حداً هو «خاصة وصف المحدود في مقصود الحدّ»، إذ ليس الغرضُ بالسؤال عن «العلم التعرُّضَ لتفصيله، وإنما الغرض معرفة العلميّة بأخص وصف العلم الذي يشترك فيه ما تختلف منه وما يتماثل بما ذكرناه، حيث قلنا إنه «المعرفة» أو «ما يعلم به» أو «التبيين». وهذا على طريقة الأستاذ. ومن رام ذكر حدّ في قبيل المعلومات فإنما غرضه الوقوفُ على صفة يشترك فيها القبيلُ المسئول عنه على وجه يتضح للسائل . صفة المحدود قال أبو المعالي : فإن قيل الحدُّ يرجع إلى قول المخبر، أو إلى صفة في دون قول القائل المحدود!» قلنا : ما صار إليه كافَةُ الأئمة أن الحدَّ صفة المحدود، سكت عنه الواصفون أم نطقوا، وهو بمعنى الحقيقة». وقد ذكر القاضي في «التقريب» أن الحد قول الحادّ المنبيء عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود ووافق الأصحابُ في أنَّ حقيقة الشيء ومعناه راجعان إلى صفةٍ دون قول القائل، وإنما بُيِّن ذلك في الحد لمشابهته الوصف ومشابهة الحقيقة الصفة، ونحن نفصل بين الوصف والصفة .
The definition: Imam al-Haramayn said: The purpose of definition (tahdid) in the terminology of the mutakallimun is 'to mention the specific property (khassa) of the thing and its reality (haqiqa) by which the distinction between it and other than it occurs.' Al-Ustadh said: The definition (hadd) of a thing is 'its meaning for the sake of which it is, by the description intended to be mentioned.' Abu al-Ma'ali said: If one were to say 'the definition of a thing is its meaning' and stop there, that would be correct; or if one said 'the definition of a thing is its reality or its specific property,' that would be good. He said: If it is said: When you say 'the definition of knowledge ('ilm) or its reality is that by which one knows' (ma yu'lamu bihi), you have not mentioned the specific property of knowledge, because knowledge includes diverse and similar things that do not all come together under one specific property; for two things that share a more specific property are similar. We say: Our purpose is only to show that what is mentioned as a definition is 'the specific property of the description of the defined in the intended purpose of the definition.' For the purpose of asking about 'knowledge' is not to specify its details, but rather the purpose is to know 'knowledgeness' by the most specific description of knowledge that is shared by what differs and what is similar among it, by what we have mentioned, where we said that it is 'cognition' (ma'rifa) or 'that by which one knows' or 'clarification' (tabyin). This is according to the method of al-Ustadh. And whoever desires to mention a definition for a category of known things, his purpose is only to arrive at a description shared by the category asked about in a manner that becomes clear to the questioner. The description of the defined: Abu al-Ma'ali said: If it is said: Does the definition refer to the statement of the informant, or to a description in the defined regardless of the statement of the speaker? We say: The consensus of the imams is that the definition is a description of the defined, whether the describers remain silent or speak, and it is in the sense of reality. Al-Qadi mentioned in 'al-Taqrib' that the definition is the statement of the definer indicating the description shared by the individuals of the defined. And the companions agreed that the reality of a thing and its meaning refer back to a description, not to the statement of the speaker. This is clarified in the definition because of its resemblance to description (wasf) and the resemblance of reality to property (sifa). And we distinguish between description (wasf) and property (sifa).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the concept of 'hadd' (definition) as understood by the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians). It opens with a quote from Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) stating that the purpose of definition is to mention the specific property and reality of a thing that distinguishes it from others. This immediately establishes that a definition must capture what is unique to the thing. Then al-Ustadh (likely Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) defines hadd as 'the meaning for the sake of which the thing is,' i.e., the essential attribute that makes it what it is. Abu al-Ma'ali (Juwayni) adds that simply saying 'the definition is the meaning' is acceptable, or calling it 'reality' or 'specific property' is also good. This shows that the mutakallimun equate hadd with the thing's essence (haqiqa) and its distinctive characteristic. The passage then addresses an objection: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'that by which one knows' fails to capture the specific property of knowledge because knowledge includes diverse types. The response is that the purpose is not to list all details but to give the most specific shared description of 'ilm, which they propose as 'ma'rifa' (cognition) or 'tabyin' (clarification). This illustrates that hadd, for the mutakallimun, is about the essential property that unifies all instances, not a comprehensive enumeration. The discussion then shifts to whether hadd is a statement or a property. The dominant view is that hadd is the property (sifa) of the defined, not the definer's speech; the definition's wording merely indicates that property. Al-Qadi (Baqillani) is cited as saying that hadd is the statement indicating the shared property, but the reality is the property itself. The distinction between 'wasf' (description, the act of describing) and 'sifa' (property, the attribute itself) is emphasized. Throughout, the passage explains that definitions in Islamic theology aim to capture the intrinsic reality of things, not just verbal formulations.

B) To mention the specific property and reality that distinguish the thing from others

Defining Knowledge: Balancing Universality and Specificity
Objection regarding the definition of 'ilm and the response clarifying the methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a definition of 'ilm that captures its common essence across diverse instances, responding to the objection that knowledge includes diverse types.

Core Idea
A good definition unifies all instances under a single shared property.

The passage presents a subtle objection: if we define 'ilm as 'that by which one knows,' we don't seem to capture a specific property, because knowledge includes many different types. The response clarifies that the goal is to find the most specific description shared by all instances of 'ilm. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: defining 'ilm as 'that by which one knows' does not mention a specific property (khassa), because knowledge encompasses diverse types (e.g., sensation, inference) and also similar types. A single specific property cannot cover all.
The key is that the objector expects a definition to list every detail.
2
See the response
The mutakallimun respond that the purpose is not to detail all aspects, but to find the most specific description shared by all instances of 'ilm. They propose 'ma'rifa' (cognition) or 'tabyin' (clarification) as that shared essence.
This is the 'most specific description' that unifies all knowledge.
3
Identify the proposed definitions
In the passage, they say 'we said it is cognition (ma'rifa) or that by which one knows (ma yu'lamu bihi) or clarification (tabyin).' These are attempts to capture the shared property.
These are not exhaustive; they are examples of the most specific shared description.
4
Apply to other categories
The same principle applies to any category: the definition aims for a description that all members share and that distinguishes them from other categories.
This method is used in kalam to define classes of things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الحد قال إمام الحرمين : القصد من التحديد في اصطلاح المتكلمين «التعرض لخاصة الشيء وحَقِيقَتِهِ التي يقع بها الفصل بينه وبين غيره . قال الأستاذ : حدُّ الشيء «معناه الذي لأجله كان بالوصف المقصود بالذكر . قال أبو المعالي : ولو قال قائل حدُّ الشيء معناه واقتصر عليه كان سديداً، أو قال : حدّ الشيء حقيقته أو خاصته كان حسناً . قال : فإن قيل إذا قلتُم ، حدُّ (العلم) أو حقيقته (ما يعلم به، فلم تذكروا خاصة العلم، لأن العلم يشتمل على مختلفات ومتماثلات لا تجتمع جميعها في خاصة واحدة، فإن المجتمعين في الأخص متماثلان». فنقولُ : إنما غرضُنا أن نبين أن المذكور حداً هو «خاصة وصف المحدود في مقصود الحدّ»، إذ ليس الغرضُ بالسؤال عن «العلم التعرُّضَ لتفصيله، وإنما الغرض معرفة العلميّة بأخص وصف العلم الذي يشترك فيه ما تختلف منه وما يتماثل بما ذكرناه، حيث قلنا إنه «المعرفة» أو «ما يعلم به» أو «التبيين». وهذا على طريقة الأستاذ. ومن رام ذكر حدّ في قبيل المعلومات فإنما غرضه الوقوفُ على صفة يشترك فيها القبيلُ المسئول عنه على وجه يتضح للسائل . صفة المحدود قال أبو المعالي : فإن قيل الحدُّ يرجع إلى قول المخبر، أو إلى صفة في دون قول القائل المحدود!» قلنا : ما صار إليه كافَةُ الأئمة أن الحدَّ صفة المحدود، سكت عنه الواصفون أم نطقوا، وهو بمعنى الحقيقة». وقد ذكر القاضي في «التقريب» أن الحد قول الحادّ المنبيء عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود ووافق الأصحابُ في أنَّ حقيقة الشيء ومعناه راجعان إلى صفةٍ دون قول القائل، وإنما بُيِّن ذلك في الحد لمشابهته الوصف ومشابهة الحقيقة الصفة، ونحن نفصل بين الوصف والصفة .
The definition: Imam al-Haramayn said: The purpose of definition (tahdid) in the terminology of the mutakallimun is 'to mention the specific property (khassa) of the thing and its reality (haqiqa) by which the distinction between it and other than it occurs.' Al-Ustadh said: The definition (hadd) of a thing is 'its meaning for the sake of which it is, by the description intended to be mentioned.' Abu al-Ma'ali said: If one were to say 'the definition of a thing is its meaning' and stop there, that would be correct; or if one said 'the definition of a thing is its reality or its specific property,' that would be good. He said: If it is said: When you say 'the definition of knowledge ('ilm) or its reality is that by which one knows' (ma yu'lamu bihi), you have not mentioned the specific property of knowledge, because knowledge includes diverse and similar things that do not all come together under one specific property; for two things that share a more specific property are similar. We say: Our purpose is only to show that what is mentioned as a definition is 'the specific property of the description of the defined in the intended purpose of the definition.' For the purpose of asking about 'knowledge' is not to specify its details, but rather the purpose is to know 'knowledgeness' by the most specific description of knowledge that is shared by what differs and what is similar among it, by what we have mentioned, where we said that it is 'cognition' (ma'rifa) or 'that by which one knows' or 'clarification' (tabyin). This is according to the method of al-Ustadh. And whoever desires to mention a definition for a category of known things, his purpose is only to arrive at a description shared by the category asked about in a manner that becomes clear to the questioner. The description of the defined: Abu al-Ma'ali said: If it is said: Does the definition refer to the statement of the informant, or to a description in the defined regardless of the statement of the speaker? We say: The consensus of the imams is that the definition is a description of the defined, whether the describers remain silent or speak, and it is in the sense of reality. Al-Qadi mentioned in 'al-Taqrib' that the definition is the statement of the definer indicating the description shared by the individuals of the defined. And the companions agreed that the reality of a thing and its meaning refer back to a description, not to the statement of the speaker. This is clarified in the definition because of its resemblance to description (wasf) and the resemblance of reality to property (sifa). And we distinguish between description (wasf) and property (sifa).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the concept of 'hadd' (definition) as understood by the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians). It opens with a quote from Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) stating that the purpose of definition is to mention the specific property and reality of a thing that distinguishes it from others. This immediately establishes that a definition must capture what is unique to the thing. Then al-Ustadh (likely Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) defines hadd as 'the meaning for the sake of which the thing is,' i.e., the essential attribute that makes it what it is. Abu al-Ma'ali (Juwayni) adds that simply saying 'the definition is the meaning' is acceptable, or calling it 'reality' or 'specific property' is also good. This shows that the mutakallimun equate hadd with the thing's essence (haqiqa) and its distinctive characteristic. The passage then addresses an objection: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'that by which one knows' fails to capture the specific property of knowledge because knowledge includes diverse types. The response is that the purpose is not to list all details but to give the most specific shared description of 'ilm, which they propose as 'ma'rifa' (cognition) or 'tabyin' (clarification). This illustrates that hadd, for the mutakallimun, is about the essential property that unifies all instances, not a comprehensive enumeration. The discussion then shifts to whether hadd is a statement or a property. The dominant view is that hadd is the property (sifa) of the defined, not the definer's speech; the definition's wording merely indicates that property. Al-Qadi (Baqillani) is cited as saying that hadd is the statement indicating the shared property, but the reality is the property itself. The distinction between 'wasf' (description, the act of describing) and 'sifa' (property, the attribute itself) is emphasized. Throughout, the passage explains that definitions in Islamic theology aim to capture the intrinsic reality of things, not just verbal formulations.

What Is Mantiq?

5 min
The Nature of Hadd: Property, Not Statement
Debate over whether hadd is a statement or a property, with consensus that it is a property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the position that hadd is a property (sifa) of the defined and distinguish it from a mere verbal description (wasf).

Core Idea
The definition's reality is in the thing, not in our words.

A key question arises: is the definition (hadd) actually the statement the definer utters, or is it a real property in the thing being defined? The passage presents a clear consensus: the definition is the property itself, regardless of whether anyone speaks it. This lesson explores that conclusion and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
Find where the passage asks: 'Does the definition refer to the statement of the informant, or to a description (sifa) in the defined, regardless of the statement of the speaker?'
This is a fundamental ontological question about definitions.
2
Find the consensus answer
Abu al-Ma'ali states: 'The consensus of the imams is that the definition is a description (sifa) of the defined.' This means the definition is not the words but the actual attribute.
They even say 'whether the describers remain silent or speak'.
3
Understand the distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'wasf' (the act of describing or the descriptive statement) and 'sifa' (the property itself). The definition (hadd) is the sifa, not the wasf.
The verbal definition only indicates the property; it is not the property itself.
4
Relate to definitions in practice
When we define 'ilm as 'cognition,' the real definition is the property of being cognitive that exists in the knower, not the words 'cognition' themselves.
This view grounds definitions in reality, not language.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Ideal Logician's Definition and Its Conditions
Ibn Taymiyya defines the logicians' ideal definition and cites al-Ghazali on its difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions of a correct ḥadd (definition) and explain why logicians consider it extremely difficult to achieve.

Core Idea
A perfect definition must include all essentials and exclude all non-essentials – a nearly impossible ideal.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya begins by describing the logicians' concept of a perfect definition. He then reports al-Ghazali's startling confession that such definitions are beyond normal human ability. Understanding this ideal is crucial for evaluating the later critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of a correct definition
The passage states that a correct definition must be such that nothing enters it that is not part of the defined, and nothing that is part of the defined exits it.
2
Understand the consequence
If these conditions are met, the definition is fixed and correct. The example of 'knowledge' is given to illustrate correspondence.
3
Recognize the difficulty
Al-Ghazali confesses that guarding against all errors in definition is beyond human power except rarely.
This sets up the need for a simpler approach.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Ibn Taymiyya uses this admission to question the practicality of the logicians' method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - استعصاء الحد على طريقة المنطقيين
معناه، ولا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه، ولا يخرج منه شيء هو فيه . والحد إذا أحاط بالمحدود على هذا السبيل وجب أن يكون حداً ثابتاً صحيحاً. فكلُّما حُدّ به «العلم» وغيره وكانت حصر المحدود وتمييزه من غيره وإحاطته به حال ما حددنا به «العلم» وجب الاعتراف بصحته. وقد ثبت أن كل علم تعلّق بمعلوم» فإنه «معرفةٌ له على ما هو به» و «كل معرفة بمعلوم» فإنها «علم» به ، فوجب توفيق الحد الذي حددنا به «العلم» وجعلناه تفسيراً لمعنى منه بأنه «علم» .
قلت : فقد بين القاضي أن كلّ ما أحاط بالمحدود بحيث لا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو منه كان حداً صحيحاً .
اعتراف الغزالي باستعصاء الحد
وقد ذكر الغزالي في كتابه الكبير في المنطق الذي سمّاه معيار العلم» مذهب المتكلّمين هذا بعد أن ذكر استعصاء الحدّ على طريقة المنطقيين، فقال :
في
الفصل السابع
استعصاء الحدّ على القُوَى البشرية إلا عند غاية التشمير والجهد
و من عَرَف ما ذكرناه من مُثَارات الاشتباه في الحدّ عَرَف أن القوة البشرية لا تقوى على التحفّظ عن كل ذلك إلا على الندور، وهي كثيرة، وأعصاها على الذهن اربعة أمور
أحدها: أنا شَرَطنا أن نأخذ «الجنس الأقرب». ومن أين للطالب أن لا يغفل عنه ؟ فيأخذ «جنساً يظن أنه أقرب، وربما يوجد ما هو أقرب منه ، فيَحُدُّ «الخمر» بأنه «مائع مُسْكِر ويَذْهَل عن «الشَّراب» الذي هو تحته وهو أقرب منه ، ويحدُّ «الإنسان» بأنه «جسم ناطق مائت ويغفل عن «الحيوان»، وأمثاله .
الثاني : أنا إذا شرطنا أن تكون «الفصول» «ذاتية» كلُّها، و«اللازم» الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوَهْم يشتبه بـ «الذاتي» غاية الاشتباه، ودَرْكُ ذلك من أغمض الأمور. فمن أين له أن لا يغفل فيأخذ «لازماً» فيورده بدل «الفصل» ويظن أنه «ذاتي» !
الثالث : أنا شرطنا أن يأتي بجميع الفصول الذاتية حتى لا يُخل بواحد، ومن أين يأمن من شذوذ بعضها عنه لا سيما إذا وجد «فصلاً» حصل به التمييز والمساواة في الاسم في الحمل ، كَـ «الجسم، ذى النفس، الحَسّاس ومساواته لفظ «الحيوان» مع إغفال المتحرك بالإرادة». وهذا من أغمض ما يُدرك .
الرابع: إن «الفَضْل» مقوّم لـ«النوع» مقسم لـ «الجنس». فإذا لم يُراع شرط في القسمة أَخَذَ فصولاً ليست «أوّلية» لـ «الجنس»، وهو غير مرضي في الحدود. فإن الجسم كما أنه ينقسم إلى النامي» و «غير النامي» انقساماً ذاتياً، فكذلك ينقسم إلى «الحساس» و«غير الحساس» وإلى «الناطق» و«غير الناطق. فمهما قيل : «الجسم» ينقسم إلى الناطق» و «غير الناطق» فقد قسم بما ليس هو الفصلُ» القاسمُ أوّليّاً، بل ينبغي أن يقسم أولاً إلى النامي» و«غير النامي»، ثم «النامي» ينقسم إلى الحيوان) و غير «الحيوان»، ثم «الحيوان» إلى الناطق» و «غير الناطق». وكذلك الحيوان» ينقسم إلى «ذى رِجلين» وإلى «ذى أرجل»، ولكن هذا التقسيم ليس بـ «فصول أولية»، بل ينبغي أن يقسم الحيوان» إلى «ماش» و«غير ماش»، ثم الماشي» ينقسم إلى «ذى رجلين» و «ذى أرجُل»، إذ «الحيوان» لم يستعد لـ «الرجلين» و «الأرجل باعتبار كونه حيواناً بل باعتبار كونه «ماشياً»، واستعد لكونه ماشياً باعتبار كونه حيواناً) .
فرعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحد، وهي في غاية العُسر. ولذلك، لما عَسُر، اكتفى المتكلّمون بالتمييز وقالوا: إن الحد هو «القول الجامع المانع ولم يشترطوا فيه إلا التمييز .
Second Station - The Inaccessibility of Definition on the Method of the Logicians
Its meaning: nothing enters it that is not part of it, and nothing that is part of it exits it. If the definition encompasses the defined in this manner, it must be a fixed, correct definition. So whenever 'knowledge' and other things are defined with it, and it encompasses the defined, distinguishes it from others, and surrounds it as we defined 'knowledge' with it, the acknowledgment of its correctness is necessary. And it has been established that every knowledge relates to a known thing, so it is 'cognition of it as it is', and every cognition of a known thing is 'knowledge' of it. So the definition with which we defined 'knowledge' must correspond, and we made it an explanation of its meaning, that it is 'knowledge'.
I say: The judge has shown that everything that encompasses the defined such that nothing enters it that is not part of it and nothing that is part of it exits it is a correct definition.
Al-Ghazali's Acknowledgment of the Inaccessibility of Definition
Al-Ghazali mentioned in his great book on logic called 'The Criterion of Knowledge' the doctrine of the theologians after mentioning the inaccessibility of definition on the method of the logicians. He said:
Chapter Seven
The Inaccessibility of Definition to Human Powers Except with Utmost Exertion and Effort
Whoever knows what we have mentioned of the causes of confusion in definition knows that human power cannot guard against all that except rarely, and they are many. The most difficult for the mind are four matters:
First: We stipulated that we take the 'nearest genus'. How can the seeker not neglect it? So he takes a genus that he thinks is nearest, but perhaps there is something nearer than it. So he defines 'wine' as 'an intoxicating liquid' and neglects 'beverage' which is under it and is nearer to it. He defines 'human' as 'a rational, mortal body' and neglects 'animal', and the like.
Second: When we stipulate that the 'differentiae' are all 'essential', and the 'inseparable concomitant' which does not separate in existence and imagination is extremely confused with the 'essential'. Apprehending that is among the most obscure matters. So how can he not neglect and take a 'concomitant' and present it instead of the 'differentia', thinking it is 'essential'?
Third: We stipulated that he bring all essential differentiae so that he does not omit any. How can he be sure that none of them escapes him, especially if he finds a 'differentia' that achieves distinction and equality in name in predication, like 'body, possessing soul, sentient' equaling the word 'animal' while neglecting 'moving by will'. This is among the most obscure things perceived.
Fourth: The 'differentia' constitutes the 'species' and divides the 'genus'. If the condition in division is not observed, he takes differentiae that are not 'primary' for the 'genus', and that is not satisfactory in definitions. For the body, as it is divided into 'growing' and 'non-growing' by an essential division, likewise it is divided into 'sentient' and 'non-sentient' and into 'rational' and 'non-rational'. So whenever it is said: 'The body is divided into rational and non-rational', he has divided it by what is not the primary dividing differentia. Rather, it should be divided first into 'growing' and 'non-growing', then 'growing' is divided into 'animal' and 'non-animal', then 'animal' into 'rational' and 'non-rational'. Likewise, 'animal' is divided into 'having two legs' and 'having many legs', but this division is not by 'primary differentiae'. Rather, 'animal' should be divided into 'walking' and 'non-walking', then 'walking' is divided into 'having two legs' and 'having many legs', since 'animal' is not prepared for 'two legs' and 'many legs' in virtue of being animal, but in virtue of being 'walking', and it is prepared for being walking in virtue of being animal.
Observing the order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the art of definition, and it is extremely difficult. Therefore, since it was difficult, the theologians sufficed with distinguishing and said: Definition is 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement', and they stipulated only distinction in it.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion by Ibn Taymiyya on the difficulty of definition according to the logicians' method. He begins by stating the second station (maqam) which is the inaccessibility (isti'ṣa') of definition. He first explains the ideal definition: it must encompass the defined such that nothing non-essential enters and nothing essential exits. Such a definition, if achieved, would be valid and correct. He then mentions that al-Ghazali, in his book 'Mi'yar al-Ilm', acknowledged this difficulty and stated that human powers can hardly achieve a perfect definition except rarely. Al-Ghazali lists four causes of confusion. The first is taking the nearest genus: a definer may take a genus that seems near but overlooks a nearer one, e.g., defining wine as 'intoxicating liquid' instead of 'intoxicating beverage', or defining human as 'rational mortal body' instead of 'animal'. The second cause is confusing essential differentiae with necessary concomitants: an inseparable concomitant may be mistaken for an essential differentia, a very subtle distinction. The third cause is forgetting to include all essential differentiae; one might think a single differentia suffices for complete distinction, e.g., defining animal as 'sentient body with soul' but omitting 'voluntary motion'. The fourth cause concerns the order of division: the differentia should be primary for the genus, not taken from a higher or lower level. For example, 'body' should first be divided into 'growing' and 'non-growing', then 'growing' into 'animal' and 'non-animal', then 'animal' into 'rational' and 'non-rational', not directly into 'rational' and 'non-rational'. Ibn Taymiyya then concludes that observing all these conditions is extremely difficult, so the theologians (mutakallimun) sufficed with a simpler definition: 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement' (al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ), only requiring distinction, not the full logician's definition.

Key Terms
الحد (al-ḥadd): Definition that encompasses the defined, distinguishing it from all others, with the condition that nothing non-essential enters and nothing essential exits. [Note: In logic, al-ḥadd is a technical definition requiring genus and differentia; but Ibn Taymiyya here critiques this and favors the simpler definition of the mutakallimun.]
القول الجامع المانع (al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ): A comprehensive and exclusive statement that distinguishes the defined from all others, without requiring genus and differentia. [Note: This is the simpler definition favored by theologians; it contrasts with the more complex logical definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be brief.
B) It must include all and exclude all.
C) It must use genus and differentia.
D) It must be approved by scholars.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' ideal definition is an all-encompassing, exclusive statement, but its realization is acknowledged as extremely rare.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Four Causes of Confusion in Definition (Al-Ghazali's Analysis)
Al-Ghazali enumerates four causes that make definition nearly impossible for humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain each of the four causes that make definition on the logicians' method extremely difficult.

Core Idea
Four subtle errors – missing the nearest genus, confusing essential with concomitant, omitting a differentia, and misordering divisions – make perfect definition almost impossible.

Having admitted the difficulty, al-Ghazali lists four specific pitfalls that even skilled logicians fall into. Ibn Taymiyya reports them to show why the theologians' simpler approach to definition is more practical. Let's examine each cause in detail.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first cause: nearest genus
The definer may take a genus that seems near but overlooks a closer one, e.g., defining wine as 'intoxicating liquid' instead of 'intoxicating beverage'.
2
Analyze the second cause: essential vs. concomitant
An inseparable concomitant (lāzim) can be mistaken for an essential differentia (faṣl dhātī). This is very subtle.
3
Analyze the third cause: missing a differentia
One might think a single differentia suffices for complete distinction but omit another essential one.
4
Analyze the fourth cause: order of division
The differentia must be primary for the genus; otherwise, the division is out of order. Example: body divided into rational/non-rational is wrong; first divide into growing/non-growing.
5
Understand the theologians' solution
Because of these difficulties, theologians adopted a simpler definition: 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement' (al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - استعصاء الحد على طريقة المنطقيين
معناه، ولا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه، ولا يخرج منه شيء هو فيه . والحد إذا أحاط بالمحدود على هذا السبيل وجب أن يكون حداً ثابتاً صحيحاً. فكلُّما حُدّ به «العلم» وغيره وكانت حصر المحدود وتمييزه من غيره وإحاطته به حال ما حددنا به «العلم» وجب الاعتراف بصحته. وقد ثبت أن كل علم تعلّق بمعلوم» فإنه «معرفةٌ له على ما هو به» و «كل معرفة بمعلوم» فإنها «علم» به ، فوجب توفيق الحد الذي حددنا به «العلم» وجعلناه تفسيراً لمعنى منه بأنه «علم» .
قلت : فقد بين القاضي أن كلّ ما أحاط بالمحدود بحيث لا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو منه كان حداً صحيحاً .

Whoever knows what we have mentioned of the causes of confusion in definition knows that human power cannot guard against all that except rarely, and they are many. The most difficult for the mind are four matters:
First: We stipulated that we take the 'nearest genus'. How can the seeker not neglect it? So he takes a genus that he thinks is nearest, but perhaps there is something nearer than it. Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion by Ibn Taymiyya on the difficulty of definition according to the logicians' method. He begins by stating the second station (maqam) which is the inaccessibility (isti'ṣa') of definition. He first explains the ideal definition: it must encompass the defined such that nothing non-essential enters and nothing essential exits. Such a definition, if achieved, would be valid and correct. He then mentions that al-Ghazali, in his book 'Mi'yar al-Ilm', acknowledged this difficulty and stated that human powers can hardly achieve a perfect definition except rarely. Al-Ghazali lists four causes of confusion. The first is taking the nearest genus: a definer may take a genus that seems near but overlooks a nearer one, e.g., defining wine as 'intoxicating liquid' instead of 'intoxicating beverage', or defining human as 'rational mortal body' instead of 'animal'. The second cause is confusing essential differentiae with necessary concomitants: an inseparable concomitant may be mistaken for an essential differentia, a very subtle distinction. The third cause is forgetting to include all essential differentiae; one might think a single differentia suffices for complete distinction, e.g., defining animal as 'sentient body with soul' but omitting 'voluntary motion'. The fourth cause concerns the order of division: the differentia should be primary for the genus, not taken from a higher or lower level. For example, 'body' should first be divided into 'growing' and 'non-growing', then 'growing' into 'animal' and 'non-animal', then 'animal' into 'rational' and 'non-rational', not directly into 'rational' and 'non-rational'. Ibn Taymiyya then concludes that observing all these conditions is extremely difficult, so the theologians (mutakallimun) sufficed with a simpler definition: 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement' (al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ), only requiring distinction, not the full logician's definition.

Key Terms
الجنس الأقرب (al-jins al-aqrab): The nearest genus under which the defined falls directly, as 'animal' is for human, not 'body'. [Note: Standard term used across classical logic; no significant shift.]
الفصل الذاتي (al-faṣl al-dhātī): Essential differentia that constitutes the species and distinguishes it from other species under the same genus. [Note: Stable term; used in all major logic texts.]
اللازم (al-lāzim): An inseparable concomitant that accompanies the essence but is not part of it. [Note: In logic, lāzim is often confused with essential property; Ibn Taymiyya highlights this confusion.]
القول الجامع المانع (al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ): A comprehensive and exclusive statement that distinguishes the defined from all others, without requiring genus and differentia. [Note: This is the simpler definition favored by theologians; it contrasts with the more complex logical definition.]

A) Taking a genus that is not the nearest.
B) Confusing essential differentia with necessary concomitant.
C) Using a definition that is too broad.

Key Takeaway
The four causes show that constructing a perfect logical definition is fraught with subtle pitfalls, leading theologians to adopt a more practical approach.

What Is Mantiq?

Critique of Logic's Necessity for Knowledge
Author argues that truth was known before Aristotle and without his logical system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's historical and epistemological grounds for rejecting the claim that Aristotelian logic is necessary for knowledge.

Core Idea
Truth was known before Aristotle and without his system; logic is a conventional art, not the sole path to knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya confronts the claim that Aristotelian logic is indispensable for attaining certain knowledge. He uses historical and empirical reasoning to challenge this: previous nations knew realities without this 'conventional art,' and most people today still do. This lesson dissects his critique and its implications for the status of logic in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The logicians claim that their art (the Aristotelian logical system) provides definitions of realities and that realities are not known except through it. This is the target.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's historical argument
He notes that nations before Aristotle knew realities without his convention, and that most nations after him also know realities without it. He specifies Aristotle's timeline (vizier to Alexander, ~300 BC) to show that knowledge existed long before.
3
Analyze his epistemological argument
He appeals to introspection: when rational people reflect, they find themselves knowing realities without studying logic. This is a direct refutation of the claim that logic is necessary.
4
Determine the type of critique
Ibn Taymiyya's critique here is primarily empirical and historical. He uses observed facts (people know without logic) and historical precedence. He downgrades logic from a universal tool to a 'conventional art' (sina'a wad'iyya), implying it is a human invention, not a necessary condition for knowledge.

Source Text

والحقائق قبل كانت الأمم قبلهم تعرف حقائق الأشياء بدون هذا الوضع، وعامة الأمم بعدهم تعرف حقائق الأشياء بدون وضعهم فإن أرسطو كان وزيراً للإسكندر بن فيلبس المقدوني، وليس هذا ذا القرنين المذكور في القرآن كما يظنُّه كثير منهم، بل هذا كان قبل المسيح بنحو ثلاث مئة سنة. وجماهير العقلاء من جميع الأمم يعرفون الحقائق من غير تعلم منهم لوضع أرسطو، وهم إذا تدبَّروا أنفسهم وجدوا أنفسهم تَعْلَم حقائق الأشياء بدون هذه الصناعة الوضعيّة. ثم إن هذه الصناعة زعموا أنها تفيد تعريف حقائق الأشياء، ولا تُعرف إلا بها.
And realities before [Aristotle] — the nations before them knew the realities of things without this convention, and the majority of nations after them know the realities of things without their convention. For Aristotle was a vizier to Alexander son of Philip the Macedonian, and he is not Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Qur'an as many of them think; rather, this one was about three hundred years before Christ. And the masses of rational people from all nations know realities without learning from Aristotle's convention; and when they reflect on themselves, they find themselves knowing the realities of things without this conventional art. Then they claim that this art provides the definition of realities, and that they are not known except through it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the necessity of Aristotelian logic for obtaining knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that truth (reality) was known by previous nations without Aristotle's invented logical system, and that most subsequent nations also know realities without it. He notes that Aristotle was a vizier to Alexander the Great, who lived about 300 years before Christ, clarifying that this Alexander is not Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Qur'an (as some mistakenly believe). He argues that the masses of rational people across all nations know the realities of things without studying Aristotle's logic; introspection shows that they naturally know realities without this conventional art. The logicians, however, claim that their art alone provides definitions of realities and that realities cannot be known except through it — a claim Ibn Taymiyya here rejects as false.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logic as Necessary for Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya lists Aristotle's logical works and argues that truth is known without them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that truth is known without Aristotle's logic and evaluate his grounds.

Core Idea
Truth was known before Aristotle and is known after him without his artificial logic.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges the foundational claim of the logicians that their craft is necessary for knowing truth. He lists the books of Aristotle's Organon, but his purpose is not to describe them. Rather, he uses their specific Greek names to subtly underscore that this is a foreign, human invention. He then provides historical evidence and a universal argument: rational people across nations know truths without studying logic. The burden of proof, he insists, is on those who claim logic is indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states what the logicians claim about logic. The passage says they claim logic 'provides the definition of truths' and that truths 'are not known except through it.' This is the claim he refutes.
2
List the logical works mentioned
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates the parts of Aristotle's Organon: Hermeneutics (expressions), Prior Analytics (absolute syllogism), Posterior Analytics (demonstrative), Topics (dialectical), Rhetoric (rhetorical), Poetics (poetic), and Sophistics (sophistical). Notice that he presents them as Greek terms Arabized.
3
Note the historical correction
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that Aristotle was not the Prophet Dhul-Qarnayn but a minister of Alexander, living about 300 years before Christ. This underscores that Aristotle's logic is a human invention, not divinely revealed.
4
Follow the critique's structure
Ibn Taymiyya argues that pre-Aristotelian nations knew truths without his logic, and the majority of post-Aristotelian rational people also know truths without it. He invites the reader to introspect to confirm this.
5
Evaluate the conclusory statement
The passage ends with Ibn Taymiyya declaring both logicians' claims false: that logic defines truths and that truths are known only through it. Assess the strength of his argument based on the evidence provided.

The establishment of logic. The book The Refutation of the Logicians. The second part, which contains the premises, they call 'Hermenia', meaning 'Expressions'. The third, which contains absolute syllogism, they call 'Prior Analytics'. The demonstrative they call 'Posterior Analytics'. The dialectical they call 'Topics'. The rhetorical they call 'Rhetoric'. The poetic they call 'Poetics'. The sophistical they call 'Sophistics'. These are Greek expressions; when the Arabs spoke them, they Arabized them and brought them near to their languages, like all other non-Arabic words that the Arabs spoke; this is why their forms differ. The truths before—the nations before them knew the truths of things without this establishment, and the majority of nations after them know the truths of things without their establishment. For Aristotle was a minister to Alexander son of Philip the Macedonian, and this is not Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Quran as many of them suppose; rather, this was about three hundred years before Christ. The masses of rational people from all nations know truths without learning from Aristotle's establishment; and when they reflect upon themselves, they find themselves knowing the truths of things without this artificial craft. Then they claimed that this craft provides the definition of the truths of things, and that they are not known except through it. Both of these are false.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage opens with a description of the logical works of Aristotle: the books of the Organon (Hermeneutics, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, Rhetoric, Poetics, Sophistics). Ibn Taymiyya then notes that these are Greek terms Arabized. His main critique follows: he argues that nations before Aristotle knew truths without his logic, and nations after him also know truths without it. He clarifies that Aristotle was not the Prophet Dhul-Qarnayn but a minister of Alexander who lived three centuries before Christ. The masses of rational people know truths without learning logic. Their claim that logic is necessary for defining truths is thus false. The lesson concludes that both claims—that logic defines truths and that truths are only known through it—are false.

Key Terms
وضع المنطق (wad' al-mantiq): The artificial craft of logic as established by Aristotle [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses wad' to imply that logic is a human invention, not a natural or necessary science.]
القياس المطلق (al-qiyas al-mutlaq): Absolute syllogism, referring to categorical syllogism in the Prior Analytics [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian logic; Ibn Taymiyya critiques its supposed necessity.]

The Indication of Definition is Like the Indication of a Noun
Ibn Taymiyya argues definitions are like nouns in that they only indicate, not teach, new concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definitions do not produce new conceptualization and are equivalent to nouns.

Core Idea
Definitions are just extended names—they cannot teach you anything new about an unknown reality.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a foundational claim of the logicians: that definitions (hudud) are the primary means to acquire new concepts (tasawwur). He argues the opposite: definitions merely unpack what is already implied by the name. The Bedouin and the Quranic examples illustrate how even a single noun can convey a complete thought when context is present.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya’s central thesis
What is the relationship he asserts between definition and noun? Look for the claim that the indication of the definition (dalalat al-hadd) is like the indication of the noun (dalalat al-ism).
2
Analyze the Bedouin anecdote
How does the Bedouin’s reaction to the accusative phrase 'Muhammadan rasul Allah' show the need for a complete sentence? The Bedouin asked 'He did what?' because the accusative implied an incomplete predicate; a single word like 'food' was understood as a predicate of an omitted subject.
3
Extract the Quranic evidence
How do verses like 'Say: Allah' demonstrate that a single noun can stand for a full proposition? In these verses, a noun functions as a predicate, implying a complete sentence without an explicit subject.
4
Trace the circularity argument
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does the logicians’ claim that definitions teach new concepts lead to a vicious circle? Because to know a definition, you must already know the meaning of the words it uses; if definitions were for acquiring new concepts, you would need prior knowledge.
5
Compare with standard Mantiq
How does Ibn Taymiyya redefine the purpose of definition from 'producing conceptualization' to 'detailing a name'? He cites the logicians' own statement that definition is 'a detailing of what the name indicates in summary'.
