المقام الثاني - دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم
ولهذا لما مَرَّ بعضُ العرب بمؤذن يقول أشهد أن محمداً رسول الله - بالنصب - قال : «فَعَلَ ماذا؟» فإذا قيل: ما هذا قيل «طعام، فهذا خبر مبتدإ محذوف باتفاق الناس، تقديره هذا طعام»، كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ اللَّهُ ﴾ [سورة الزمر : ٣٨]، وقوله : ﴿ قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ الَّذِي جَاءَ بِهِ مُوسَىٰ نُورًا وَهُدًى لِّلنَّاسِ ۖ تَجْعَلُونَهُ قَرَاطِيسَ تُبْدُونَهَا وَتُخْفُونَ كَثِيرًا ۖ وَعُلِّمْتُم مَّا لَمْ تَعْلَمُوا أَنتُمْ وَلَا آبَاؤُكُمْ ۖ قُلِ اللَّهُ ۖ ثُمَّ ذَرْهُمْ فِي خَوْضِهِمْ يَلْعَبُونَ ﴾ إلى قوله - ﴿ قُلِ اللَّهُ ﴾ [سورة الأنعام ٩١]، وكقوله : ﴿ سَيَقُولُونَ ثَلَاثَةٌ رَّابِعُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ وَيَقُولُونَ خَمْسَةٌ سَادِسُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ رَجْمًا بِالْغَيْبِ وَيَقُولُونَ سَبْعَةٌ وَثَامِنُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢٢] أي هم ثلاثة» و«هم خمسة» و«هم سبعة». ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَقُلِ الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢٩]، أي، «هذا الحقُ من ربكم، ليس كما يظنه بعض الجهال، أى، قل القول الحق، فإنّ هذا لو أريد لنصب لفظَ «الحق». والمراد إثبات أن القرآن حق، ولهذا قال : الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ، ليس المراد هنا بقول حق مطلق؛ بل هذا المعنى مذكور في قوله: ﴿ وَإِذَا قُلْتُمْ فَاعْدِلُوا ﴾ [سورة الأنعام : ١٥٢]، وقوله : ﴿ أَلَمْ يُؤْخَذْ عَلَيْهِم مِّيثَاقُ الْكِتَابِ أَن لَّا يَقُولُوا عَلَى اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْحَقَّ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف : ١٦٩].
ثم إذا قُدّر أن الحد هو المفرد فالمفردات أسماء، وغاية السائل أن يتصوَّر مسماها، لكن مر من أين له إذا تصور مُسَمَّاها أن هذا المسمَّى هو المسئول عنه، وأن هذا المسمى هو حقيقة المحدود؟ فإن قيل : يفيده مجرد تصوّر المسمى من غير أن يُحكم أنه هو ذلك أو غير ذلك، بل دلالة الحد تصوّر «إنسان»، قيل : فحينئذ يكون هذا كمجرّد دِلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه، الاسم على مسماه، كما لو قيل : «الإنسان». وهذا يحقق ما قلناه من أن دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، وهذا هو قول أهل الصواب الذين يقولون: «الحدّ تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم دلالة إجمال». وحينئذ فيقال : لا نزاع بين العقلاء أن مجرّد الاسم لا يوجب تصوير المسمى لمن لم يتصوره بدون ذلك، وإنما الاسم يفيد الدلالة عليه والإشارة إليه. ولهذا قالوا : إن المقصود باللغات ليس هو دلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه. وذلك لأن اللفظ المفرد لا يُعرف دلالته على المعنى إن لم يُعرف أنه موضوع له، ولا يُعرف أنه موضوع له حتى يتصوّر اللفظ والمعنى. فلو كان المقصود بالوضع استفادة معاني الألفاظ المفردة لزم الدور. وإذا لم يكن المقصود من الأسماء تصوير معانيها المفردة، ودلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، لم يكن المقصود من الحدّ تصوير معناه المفرد وإذا كان دلالة الاسم على مسمّاه مسبوقاً بتصور مسمّاه وَجَبَ أن تكون دلالة الحدّ على المحدود مسبوقاً بتصور المحدود. وإذا كان كل من المحدود والمسمَّى متصوّراً بدون الاسم والحد، وكان تصور المسمّى والمحدود مشترطاً في دلالة الحدّ والاسم على معناه، امتنع أن تتصور المحدودات بمجرد الحدود، كما يمتنع تصور المسمّيات بمجرد الأسماء. وهذا هو المطلوب .
الكلام المفيد ولهذا كان من المتفق عليه بين جميع أهل الأرض أن الكلام المفيد لا يكون إلا جملة تامة جملة تامة، كاسمين، أو فعل واسم. وهذا مما اعترف به المنطقيون، وقسموا الألفاظ إلى «اسم» و«كلمة» و«حرف» - يسمّى «أداة» ؛ وقالوا : المراد بـ «الكلمة» ما يريده النحاة بلفظ «الفعل». لكنهم مع هذا يتناقضون ويجعلون ما هو «اسم» عند النحاة «حرفاً» في اصطلاحهم. كالضمائر - ضمائر الرفع والنصب والجر المتصلة، والمنفصلة - مثل قولك: «رأيتُه» و«مَرَّ بي»، فإن هذه أسماء، ويسمّيها النحاة الأسماء المضمرة؛ والمنطقيون يقولون : إنها لغة اليونان من باب الحروف»، ويسمّونها «الخوالف»، كأنها خَلَفُ الأسماء عن الظاهرة .
فأما الاسم المفرد فلا يكون كلاماً مفيداً عند أحدٍ من أهل الأرض، بل ولا أهل
The second station: The indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun.
For this reason, when a Bedouin passed by a mu'adhdhin saying "I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of God" with accusative, he said: "He did what?" So when it is said: "What is this?" he said "food" — this is a predicate of an omitted subject by agreement of the people, its estimated form being "this is food", like His statement: "And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say 'Allah'" [Az-Zumar:38], and His statement: "Say: 'Who sent down the Book that Moses brought as light and guidance for the people?' ... " up to His saying: "Say: 'Allah'" [Al-An'am:91], and like His statement: "They will say three, their fourth is their dog; and they will say five, their sixth is their dog, guessing at the unseen; and they will say seven, their eighth is their dog" [Al-Kahf:22] — meaning "they are three", "they are five", and "they are seven". And from it is His statement: "And say the truth from your Lord" [Al-Kahf:29], i.e., "this truth is from your Lord, not as some ignoramuses think", i.e., say the truthful statement, for if that were intended, the word "truth" would be given accusative. And the intended meaning is to establish that the Qur'an is truth, therefore He said: "the truth from your Lord" — what is meant here is not an absolute truth; rather this meaning is mentioned in His saying: "And when you speak, be just" [Al-An'am:152] and His saying: "Has the covenant of the Book not been taken from them that they should not say about Allah except the truth" [Al-A'raf:169].
Then, if it is assumed that the definition is the single word, then singular words are nouns, and the utmost aim of the questioner is to conceptualize its referent. But where does he get, when he conceptualizes its referent, that this referent is the thing asked about, and that this referent is the reality of the defined? If it is said: "The mere conceptualization of the referent benefits him without judging that it is that or other, rather the indication of the definition is the conceptualization of 'man'?", it is said: Then in that case this is like the mere indication of a single word for its meaning, the noun for its referent, as if it were said: "man". And this confirms what we have said: that the indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun. And this is the statement of the people of correctness who say: "The definition is a detailing of what the noun indicates by way of summary." Then it is said: There is no dispute among rational people that the mere noun does not cause the conceptualization of the referent for one who has not conceptualized it without that; rather the noun only indicates and points to it. This is why they said: The purpose of languages is not the indication of a single word for its meaning. Because the single word is not known for its indication to the meaning unless it is known that it is assigned to it; and it is not known that it is assigned to it until the word and the meaning are conceptualized. So if the purpose of assignment were the acquisition of the meanings of single words, circularity would result. And if the purpose of nouns is not the conceptualization of their single meanings, and the indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun, then the purpose of the definition is not the conceptualization of its single meaning. And if the indication of the noun of its referent is preceded by the conceptualization of its referent, then the indication of the definition of the defined must be preceded by the conceptualization of the defined. And if each of the defined and the referent are conceptualized without the definition and the noun, and the conceptualization of the referent and the defined is a condition for the indication of the definition and the noun of their meaning, then it is impossible to conceptualize the defined things by means of definitions alone, just as it is impossible to conceptualize the named things by means of nouns alone. And this is what is sought.
Beneficial speech: and for this reason it is agreed upon by all people of the earth that beneficial speech is only a complete sentence, such as two nouns, or a verb and a noun. And this is what the logicians acknowledged, and they divided words into "noun", "word", and "particle" — called "tool"; and they said: By "word" is meant what the grammarians mean by the term "verb". But despite this, they contradict themselves and make what is a "noun" in the grammarians' terms a "particle" in their terminology, like the pronouns — the connected and detached pronouns of nominative, accusative, and genitive — such as your saying: "I saw him" and "he passed by me". For these are nouns, and the grammarians call them "implicit nouns"; but the logicians say: in the language of the Greeks they are from the category of "particles", and they call them "substitutes", as if they are substitutes for the explicit nouns.
As for the single noun, it is not beneficial speech according to anyone among the people, nor even among
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logical theory that definitions (hadd) provide new conceptualization (tasawwur) of the defined thing. He opens by stating that the indication of a definition is like the indication of a noun (ism). To illustrate, he gives the example of a Bedouin hearing a mu'adhdhin say 'ashhadu anna Muhammadan rasul Allah' with the accusative case ending on 'Muhammadan', which prompted the Bedouin to ask 'fa'ala madha?' (He did what?) because the syntax implied an incomplete sentence. Ibn Taymiyya then explains that when one says 'food' in answer to 'what is this?', it is understood as a predicate of an omitted subject ('this is food'), a construction recognized by all. He cites several Quranic verses where a noun phrase stands as a complete statement with an implicit predicate. This shows that even a single word can convey a full proposition when context is supplied.

Next, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the core argument. If the definition is a single word (mufrad), then it is like a name. The questioner's aim is to conceptualize the named thing. But how does he know, upon conceptualizing the referent, that this referent is exactly what he was asking about? If it is said that the definition merely provides a concept without identification, then the definition is no different from the name itself. He quotes the logicians' own statement that 'the definition is a detailing of what the name indicates in summary', thereby agreeing that the definition adds nothing new to the name's indication. He then argues that since a name does not produce conceptualization for someone who doesn't already have it (it only points and indicates), and if definitions are like names, then definitions also cannot produce new conceptualization. To support this, he points out a circularity: if the purpose of language was to convey meanings of single words, one would need to know the word and its meaning already; thus the actual purpose is to form propositions (sentences). Therefore, definitions cannot be the source of conceptualization of the defined; the defined must already be conceptualized through other means. The conclusion is that definitions are useless for teaching unknown realities, a direct challenge to the classical logical doctrine.

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the topic of beneficial speech. All agree that beneficial speech is a complete sentence, not a single word. The logicians themselves classify words into noun, verb, and particle. Yet they contradict themselves by treating pronouns (which are nouns in Arabic) as particles, following Greek language conventions. This inconsistency reinforces his point: if definitions are like nouns (single words), they cannot be beneficial speech and thus cannot teach new conceptualizations. The passage ends with the assertion that a single noun is not beneficial speech to anyone.

Key Terms
الحد
الاسم
الدلالة
التصور

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logicians' Classification of Pronouns
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the logicians' inconsistency in classifying pronouns, reinforcing his critique of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' treatment of pronouns as particles and explain why it supports his view on definition.

Core Idea
The logicians contradict themselves by treating Arabic pronouns as particles, exposing the arbitrariness of their linguistic categories.

In the latter part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya shifts from the conceptual argument to a linguistic one. He points out that the logicians, while acknowledging that beneficial speech is a complete sentence, contradict themselves by classifying pronouns (which are nouns in Arabic grammar) as particles following Greek conventions. This inconsistency further undermines their reliance on definitions as isolated words.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim about beneficial speech
What do all people agree is the form of beneficial speech? He says it is a complete sentence (jumlah tammah), such as two nouns or a verb and a noun.
2
Contrast the logicians' classification
How do the logicians divide words? Into 'ism' (noun), 'kalima' (verb), and 'harf' (particle). Where do they place pronouns? They consider pronouns as particles, following Greek conventions.
3
Compare with Arabic grammar
How do Arabic grammarians classify pronouns? They call them 'al-asma' al-mudmara' (implicit nouns), treating them as nouns. Why does Ibn Taymiyya argue the logicians are inconsistent? Because they claim to follow Arabic but import Greek categories.
4
Connect to the definition argument
How does this linguistic inconsistency relate to the earlier claim that definitions are like nouns? If definitions are single words (nouns), and single nouns are not beneficial speech, then definitions cannot teach new concepts. The logicians' misclassification reinforces that their framework is flawed.

المقام الثاني - دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم
ولهذا لما مَرَّ بعضُ العرب بمؤذن يقول أشهد أن محمداً رسول الله - بالنصب - قال : «فَعَلَ ماذا؟» فإذا قيل: ما هذا قيل «طعام، فهذا خبر مبتدإ محذوف باتفاق الناس، تقديره هذا طعام»، كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ اللَّهُ ﴾ [سورة الزمر : ٣٨]، وقوله : ﴿ قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ الَّذِي جَاءَ بِهِ مُوسَىٰ نُورًا وَهُدًى لِّلنَّاسِ ۖ تَجْعَلُونَهُ قَرَاطِيسَ تُبْدُونَهَا وَتُخْفُونَ كَثِيرًا ۖ وَعُلِّمْتُم مَّا لَمْ تَعْلَمُوا أَنتُمْ وَلَا آبَاؤُكُمْ ۖ قُلِ اللَّهُ ۖ ثُمَّ ذَرْهُمْ فِي خَوْضِهِمْ يَلْعَبُونَ ﴾ إلى قوله - ﴿ قُلِ اللَّهُ ﴾ [سورة الأنعام ٩١]، وكقوله : ﴿ سَيَقُولُونَ ثَلَاثَةٌ رَّابِعُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ وَيَقُولُونَ خَمْسَةٌ سَادِسُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ رَجْمًا بِالْغَيْبِ وَيَقُولُونَ سَبْعَةٌ وَثَامِنُهُمْ كَلْبُهُمْ ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢٢] أي هم ثلاثة» و«هم خمسة» و«هم سبعة». ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَقُلِ الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢٩]، أي، «هذا الحقُ من ربكم، ليس كما يظنه بعض الجهال، أى، قل القول الحق، فإنّ هذا لو أريد لنصب لفظَ «الحق». والمراد إثبات أن القرآن حق، ولهذا قال : الْحَقُّ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ، ليس المراد هنا بقول حق مطلق؛ بل هذا المعنى مذكور في قوله: ﴿ وَإِذَا قُلْتُمْ فَاعْدِلُوا ﴾ [سورة الأنعام : ١٥٢]، وقوله : ﴿ أَلَمْ يُؤْخَذْ عَلَيْهِم مِّيثَاقُ الْكِتَابِ أَن لَّا يَقُولُوا عَلَى اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْحَقَّ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف : ١٦٩].
ثم إذا قُدّر أن الحد هو المفرد فالمفردات أسماء، وغاية السائل أن يتصوَّر مسماها، لكن مر من أين له إذا تصور مُسَمَّاها أن هذا المسمَّى هو المسئول عنه، وأن هذا المسمى هو حقيقة المحدود؟ فإن قيل : يفيده مجرد تصوّر المسمى من غير أن يُحكم أنه هو ذلك أو غير ذلك، بل دلالة الحد تصوّر «إنسان»، قيل : فحينئذ يكون هذا كمجرّد دِلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه، الاسم على مسماه، كما لو قيل : «الإنسان». وهذا يحقق ما قلناه من أن دلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، وهذا هو قول أهل الصواب الذين يقولون: «الحدّ تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم دلالة إجمال». وحينئذ فيقال : لا نزاع بين العقلاء أن مجرّد الاسم لا يوجب تصوير المسمى لمن لم يتصوره بدون ذلك، وإنما الاسم يفيد الدلالة عليه والإشارة إليه. ولهذا قالوا : إن المقصود باللغات ليس هو دلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه. وذلك لأن اللفظ المفرد لا يُعرف دلالته على المعنى إن لم يُعرف أنه موضوع له، ولا يُعرف أنه موضوع له حتى يتصوّر اللفظ والمعنى. فلو كان المقصود بالوضع استفادة معاني الألفاظ المفردة لزم الدور. وإذا لم يكن المقصود من الأسماء تصوير معانيها المفردة، ودلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، لم يكن المقصود من الحدّ تصوير معناه المفرد وإذا كان دلالة الاسم على مسمّاه مسبوقاً بتصور مسمّاه وَجَبَ أن تكون دلالة الحدّ على المحدود مسبوقاً بتصور المحدود. وإذا كان كل من المحدود والمسمَّى متصوّراً بدون الاسم والحد، وكان تصور المسمّى والمحدود مشترطاً في دلالة الحدّ والاسم على معناه، امتنع أن تتصور المحدودات بمجرد الحدود، كما يمتنع تصور المسمّيات بمجرد الأسماء. وهذا هو المطلوب .
الكلام المفيد ولهذا كان من المتفق عليه بين جميع أهل الأرض أن الكلام المفيد لا يكون إلا جملة تامة جملة تامة، كاسمين، أو فعل واسم. وهذا مما اعترف به المنطقيون، وقسموا الألفاظ إلى «اسم» و«كلمة» و«حرف» - يسمّى «أداة» ؛ وقالوا : المراد بـ «الكلمة» ما يريده النحاة بلفظ «الفعل». لكنهم مع هذا يتناقضون ويجعلون ما هو «اسم» عند النحاة «حرفاً» في اصطلاحهم. كالضمائر - ضمائر الرفع والنصب والجر المتصلة، والمنفصلة - مثل قولك: «رأيتُه» و«مَرَّ بي»، فإن هذه أسماء، ويسمّيها النحاة الأسماء المضمرة؛ والمنطقيون يقولون : إنها لغة اليونان من باب الحروف»، ويسمّونها «الخوالف»، كأنها خَلَفُ الأسماء عن الظاهرة .
فأما الاسم المفرد فلا يكون كلاماً مفيداً عند أحدٍ من أهل الأرض، بل ولا أهل
The second station: The indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun.
For this reason, when a Bedouin passed by a mu'adhdhin saying "I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of God" with accusative, he said: "He did what?" So when it is said: "What is this?" he said "food" — this is a predicate of an omitted subject by agreement of the people, its estimated form being "this is food", like His statement: "And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say 'Allah'" [Az-Zumar:38], and His statement: "Say: 'Who sent down the Book that Moses brought as light and guidance for the people?' ... " up to His saying: "Say: 'Allah'" [Al-An'am:91], and like His statement: "They will say three, their fourth is their dog; and they will say five, their sixth is their dog, guessing at the unseen; and they will say seven, their eighth is their dog" [Al-Kahf:22] — meaning "they are three", "they are five", and "they are seven". And from it is His statement: "And say the truth from your Lord" [Al-Kahf:29], i.e., "this truth is from your Lord, not as some ignoramuses think", i.e., say the truthful statement, for if that were intended, the word "truth" would be given accusative. And the intended meaning is to establish that the Qur'an is truth, therefore He said: "the truth from your Lord" — what is meant here is not an absolute truth; rather this meaning is mentioned in His saying: "And when you speak, be just" [Al-An'am:152] and His saying: "Has the covenant of the Book not been taken from them that they should not say about Allah except the truth" [Al-A'raf:169].
Then, if it is assumed that the definition is the single word, then singular words are nouns, and the utmost aim of the questioner is to conceptualize its referent. But where does he get, when he conceptualizes its referent, that this referent is the thing asked about, and that this referent is the reality of the defined? If it is said: "The mere conceptualization of the referent benefits him without judging that it is that or other, rather the indication of the definition is the conceptualization of 'man'?", it is said: Then in that case this is like the mere indication of a single word for its meaning, the noun for its referent, as if it were said: "man". And this confirms what we have said: that the indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun. And this is the statement of the people of correctness who say: "The definition is a detailing of what the noun indicates by way of summary." Then it is said: There is no dispute among rational people that the mere noun does not cause the conceptualization of the referent for one who has not conceptualized it without that; rather the noun only indicates and points to it. This is why they said: The purpose of languages is not the indication of a single word for its meaning. Because the single word is not known for its indication to the meaning unless it is known that it is assigned to it; and it is not known that it is assigned to it until the word and the meaning are conceptualized. So if the purpose of assignment were the acquisition of the meanings of single words, circularity would result. And if the purpose of nouns is not the conceptualization of their single meanings, and the indication of the definition is like the indication of the noun, then the purpose of the definition is not the conceptualization of its single meaning. And if the indication of the noun of its referent is preceded by the conceptualization of its referent, then the indication of the definition of the defined must be preceded by the conceptualization of the defined. And if each of the defined and the referent are conceptualized without the definition and the noun, and the conceptualization of the referent and the defined is a condition for the indication of the definition and the noun of their meaning, then it is impossible to conceptualize the defined things by means of definitions alone, just as it is impossible to conceptualize the named things by means of nouns alone. And this is what is sought.
Beneficial speech: and for this reason it is agreed upon by all people of the earth that beneficial speech is only a complete sentence, such as two nouns, or a verb and a noun. And this is what the logicians acknowledged, and they divided words into "noun", "word", and "particle" — called "tool"; and they said: By "word" is meant what the grammarians mean by the term "verb". But despite this, they contradict themselves and make what is a "noun" in the grammarians' terms a "particle" in their terminology, like the pronouns — the connected and detached pronouns of nominative, accusative, and genitive — such as your saying: "I saw him" and "he passed by me". For these are nouns, and the grammarians call them "implicit nouns"; but the logicians say: in the language of the Greeks they are from the category of "particles", and they call them "substitutes", as if they are substitutes for the explicit nouns.
As for the single noun, it is not beneficial speech according to anyone among the people, nor even among
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logical theory that definitions (hadd) provide new conceptualization (tasawwur) of the defined thing. He opens by stating that the indication of a definition is like the indication of a noun (ism). To illustrate, he gives the example of a Bedouin hearing a mu'adhdhin say 'ashhadu anna Muhammadan rasul Allah' with the accusative case ending on 'Muhammadan', which prompted the Bedouin to ask 'fa'ala madha?' (He did what?) because the syntax implied an incomplete sentence. Ibn Taymiyya then explains that when one says 'food' in answer to 'what is this?', it is understood as a predicate of an omitted subject ('this is food'), a construction recognized by all. He cites several Quranic verses where a noun phrase stands as a complete statement with an implicit predicate. This shows that even a single word can convey a full proposition when context is supplied.

Next, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the core argument. If the definition is a single word (mufrad), then it is like a name. The questioner's aim is to conceptualize the named thing. But how does he know, upon conceptualizing the referent, that this referent is exactly what he was asking about? If it is said that the definition merely provides a concept without identification, then the definition is no different from the name itself. He quotes the logicians' own statement that 'the definition is a detailing of what the name indicates in summary', thereby agreeing that the definition adds nothing new to the name's indication. He then argues that since a name does not produce conceptualization for someone who doesn't already have it (it only points and indicates), and if definitions are like names, then definitions also cannot produce new conceptualization. To support this, he points out a circularity: if the purpose of language was to convey meanings of single words, one would need to know the word and its meaning already; thus the actual purpose is to form propositions (sentences). Therefore, definitions cannot be the source of conceptualization of the defined; the defined must already be conceptualized through other means. The conclusion is that definitions are useless for teaching unknown realities, a direct challenge to the classical logical doctrine.

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the topic of beneficial speech. All agree that beneficial speech is a complete sentence, not a single word. The logicians themselves classify words into noun, verb, and particle. Yet they contradict themselves by treating pronouns (which are nouns in Arabic) as particles, following Greek language conventions. This inconsistency reinforces his point: if definitions are like nouns (single words), they cannot be beneficial speech and thus cannot teach new conceptualizations. The passage ends with the assertion that a single noun is not beneficial speech to anyone.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' inconsistent classification of pronouns reveals that their linguistic categories are not universal and cannot be used to support the claim that definitions provide independent conceptualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of Circular Definitions of Unity and Plurality
Critique of logicians' definitions of unity, plurality, and number as circular or mere pointers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why definitions of primitive concepts like unity, plurality, and number in terms of each other are circular and function only as pointers (tanbihat).

Core Idea
Primitive concepts cannot be defined without circularity; definitions of them are mere pointers.

Logicians often attempt to define basic concepts like unity and plurality, but Ibn Taymiyya reveals that such definitions are either circular or only serve to alert the mind to what is already known. In this passage, he dissects several definitions from Ibn Sina and others, showing that they fail as proper logical definitions. By understanding this critique, you will learn to recognize when a definition is merely a pointer rather than a genuine definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under critique
Locate the definitions of 'plurality', 'unity', and 'number' that Ibn Taymiyya discusses. For example, 'Unity is a thing in which there is no plurality' and 'Number is a plurality composed of units'.
These definitions are from the logicians, not Ibn Taymiyya's own definitions.
2
Trace the circularity in each definition
Notice how 'plurality' is defined using 'unity', and 'unity' using 'plurality'. Similarly, 'number' is defined as 'plurality' composed of units, but 'plurality' itself means composition of units. This creates a vicious circle.
Circular definitions fail to provide new knowledge.
3
Recognize the function of a pointer (tanbih)
Ibn Taymiyya argues that such definitions are not real definitions but pointers. They merely draw attention to a concept already known primitively. The negative definition of unity ('no plurality') is not a definition but a negative indication.
Pointers are useful for clarification but not for establishing a concept's essence.
4
Evaluate the alternative view: primitive concepts are self-evident
Ibn Taymiyya's position is that unity, plurality, and number are among the first things we conceive (badīhiyyāt). They do not require definition. Any attempt to define them either presupposes them or leads to circularity.
This aligns with the Islamic epistemological tradition that certain concepts are known inherently.

حدد «العدد» في حد «الكثرة» «الوحدة»، ونكون أيضاً أخذنا في حدّها «العدد» و«التقدير»، وذلك إنما يفهم بـ «الكثرة» أيضاً. فما أغنى علينا أن نقول في هذا الباب شيئاً يعتد به، لكنه يُشبه أن تكون الكثرة أيضاً أعرف عند تخيلنا، ويشبه أن تكون الوحدة والكثرة من الأمور التي نتصوّرها بُدّيّاً. فذكر الكثرة نتخيلها أولاً، والوحدة نعقلها من غير مبدإ لتصورها. ثم إن كان ولا بد، فخيالي. ثم تعريفنا الكثرة بالوحدة تعريفاً عقلياً، وهنالك نأخذ الوحدة متصوّرة بذاتها. ومن أوائل التصوّر يكون تعريفنا الوحدة بالكثرة تنبيهاً يستعمل فيه المذهب الخيالي النومي إلى معقول عندنا، لا يتصور حاضراً في الذهن. فإذا قالوا: «إن الوحدة هي الشيء الذي ليست فيه كثرة» دلّوا على أن المراد بهذه اللفظة الشيء المعقول عندنا بديّاً الذي يقابل هذا الآخر، وليس هو؛ فينبه عليه بسلب هذا عنه. والعجب ممن يحدّد العدد ويقول: «إن العدد كثرة مؤلفة من وحدة أو آحاد». والكثرة نفس العدد، ليس كالجنس للعدد، وحقيقة الكثرة أنها مؤلَّفة من وحدات. فقولهم: «إن الكثرة مؤلفة من وحدات» كقولهم إن الكثرة كثرة، فإن الكثرة ليس إلا اسماً للمؤلف من الوحدات. فإن قال قائل: إن الكثرة قد تؤلف من أشياء غير الوحدات مثل الناس والدواب، فنقول: إنه كما أن هذه الأشياء ليست كثرات، بل أشياء موضوعة للكثرة، وكما أن تلك الأشياء هي وحدان لا واحدات، وكذلك هي كثيرة لا كثرة. والذين يحسبون أنهم إذا قالوا إن العدد كمّية منفصلة ذات ترتيب فقد تخلصوا من هذا، فما تخلصوا. فإن الكمية تُخرج تصوّرها للنفس إلى أن تعرف بالجزء أو القسمة أو المساواة. أما الجزء والقسمة فإنما يمكن تصوّرهما بالكثرة. وأما المساواة فإن الكمية أعرف منها عند العقل الصريح، لأن المساواة من الأعراض الخاصة بالكمية التي يجب أن توخذ في حدها الكمية، فيقال «إن المساواة هي اتحاد في الكمية». والترتيب الذي أخذ في حد العدد أيضاً ما لا يفهم إلا بعد فهم العدد. فيجب أن يعلم أن هذه كلها تنبيهات مثل التنبيه بالأمثلة والأسماء المترادفة، هي تنبيهات فحسب، فإن هذه المعاني متصوّرة كلها أو بعضها لذواتها، وإنما يُدلّ عليها بهذه الأشياء لينبه عليها ويميّز فقط. قلت: فهذا الكلام الذي ذكره ابن سينا هنا قد تلقوه عنه بالقبول، كما يذكر مثل ذلك أبو حامد والرازي، والسُّهروردي وغيرهم.
He defined 'number' in the definition of 'plurality' 'unity', and we also took in its definition 'number' and 'estimation', but that is only understood by 'plurality' as well. How sufficient is it for us to say something significant in this matter, but it resembles that plurality is also more known in our imagination, and it resembles that unity and plurality are among things we conceive primitively. So mentioning plurality we imagine it first, and unity we comprehend without a principle for its conception. Then if it must be, then imaginatively. Then our definition of plurality by unity is a rational definition, and there we take unity conceived by itself. And from the first of conception, our definition of unity by plurality is a pointer in which the imaginative atomistic doctrine is used to refer to what is rational for us, not conceived present in the mind. So when they say: 'Unity is a thing in which there is no plurality', they indicate that what is meant by this expression is the thing conceived by us primitively which corresponds to this other, and it is not; so it is pointed to by negating this from it. How amazing is he who defines number and says: 'Number is a plurality composed of one or ones'. Plurality is itself number, not like a genus for number, and the reality of plurality is that it is composed of units. So their saying: 'Plurality is composed of units' is like their saying plurality is plurality, for plurality is only a name for what is composed of units. If someone says: 'Plurality can be composed of things other than units, like people and animals', we say: just as these things are not pluralities, but things subject to plurality, and just as those things are 'units' not 'ones', similarly they are 'many' not 'plurality'. Those who think that when they say number is a discrete quantity having order they have escaped this have not escaped. For quantity, its conception leads the soul to know it by part or division or equality. As for part and division, they can only be conceived by plurality. As for equality, quantity is more known than it according to clear reason, because equality is one of the specific accidents of quantity which must be taken in its definition, so it is said: 'Equality is union in quantity'. Order which is taken in the definition of number is also not understood except after understanding number. So it must be known that all these are pointers like pointing by examples and synonymous names; they are only pointers, for these meanings are conceived wholly or partially by themselves, and they are only pointed to by these things to alert to them and distinguish. I say: This speech which Ibn Sina mentioned here they have accepted from him, as is mentioned similarly by Abu Hamid, al-Razi, al-Suhrawardi and others.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of definitions of number, unity, and plurality. Ibn Taymiyya begins by summarizing the circularity problem: if you define 'plurality' using 'unity' and vice versa, or define 'number' using 'plurality', you end up with a circle because these concepts are primitive. He then examines a specific definition: 'Unity is that which has no plurality'. He argues this is merely a negative pointer, not a real definition, because the concept of unity is already known primitively. Next, he critiques the definition of number as 'a plurality composed of units'—this is like saying 'plurality is plurality', since the reality of plurality is already being composed of units. He anticipates an objection that plurality can be composed of non-units like people and animals, and responds that such things are not 'units' but 'ones', and they are 'many' not 'plurality'—thus the definition still fails. Then he attacks the definition of number as 'discrete quantity having order' by showing that the concepts involved (quantity, part, division, equality, order) all presuppose number or plurality. Therefore, all these definitions are not true definitions but mere 'pointers' (tanbihat) that alert the mind to already known concepts. Ibn Taymiyya's own stance is that the logicians' definitions are futile because the primitive notions are self-evident. He concludes by noting that Ibn Sina's view on this has been accepted by later scholars like al-Ghazali, al-Razi, and al-Suhrawardi, implying that even the leading logicians admitted the definitions are notional.

Key Terms
الوحدة (al-wahda: unity)
الكثرة (al-kathra: plurality)
العدد (al-‘adad: number)
الكمية (al-kammiyya: quantity)
تنبيه (tanbīh: pointer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is defining 'unity' as 'that which does not have plurality' not a proper definition?
A) Because unity is a genus of plurality and defining it by negation is invalid.
B) Because unity is a self-evident concept that is known without definition; the definition merely points to it.
C) Because the definition uses 'plurality' which itself requires definition by 'unity'.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Definitions of primary concepts can only serve as pointers; true definitions require a genus and differentia that are not circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a definition in Islamic theology (kalam) of 'kalam Allah' (speech of God) as 'speech consisting of letters and sounds' and explain why this might be a pointer rather than a genuine definition, given that the concept of speech is primitive.
?
Construct a brief analysis: the definition of 'iman' (faith) as 'assent in the heart, utterance on the tongue, and action of the limbs' — does this definition have a clear genus and differentia, or does it point to a composite concept? Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Religious Terms: Defined by Revelation
Ibn Taymiyya defines the category of religious terms and illustrates their dependence on revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the category of 'religious terms' (al-asma' al-shar'iyya) and explain why their precise definitions require revelation.

Core Idea
Religious terms are known generally, but their precise limits come only from revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya introduces the concept of religious terms—terms that are commonly known yet cannot be precisely defined without the Prophet's guidance. This lesson clarifies why such terms are unique and how they form a distinct category in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find Ibn Taymiyya's statement that some terms are known in general but not precisely defined except from the Prophet.
Look at the beginning: '...its meaning is known in a general way... they do not know their referent in a comprehensive and exclusive way except from the Messenger.'
2
List the examples
Note the examples: salah, zakat, siyam, hajj, khamr, riba, maysir.
These are fundamental Islamic terms.
3
Analyze the debatable case
Examine the dispute about whether funeral prayer, prostrations, circumambulation fall under 'salah' in the hadith about purification.
This shows the need for further explanation.
4
Synthesize the category
Conclude that 'religious terms' require revelation for their full definition (hadd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون مشهوراً، لكن لا يعلم حده، بل يعلم معناه على سبيل الإجمال، كاسم «الصلوة» و«الزكوة» و«الصيام» والحج. فإن هذه، وإن كان جمهور المخاطبين يعلمون معناها على سبيل الإجمال، فلا يعلمون مسمّاها على سبيل التحديد الجامع المانع إلا من جهة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وهي التي يقال لها: «الأسماء الشرعية». كما إذا قيل : صلوة «الجنازة وسجدتا السهو» و «سجود الشكر» و«الطواف هل تدخل في مسمى «الصلوة» في قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مفتاح الصلوة الطهور، وتحريمها التكبير، وتحليلها التسليم»؟ فقيل : كل ذلك صلوة تجب لها «الطهارة»، وقيل لا تجب الطهارة لشيء من ذلك؟ وقيل تجب لِما تحريمه التكبير وتحليله التسليم ، كـ«صلوة الجنازة وسجدتي السهو» ، دون الطواف و «سجود التلاوة». وكذلك اسم «الخمر» و«الربوا» و«المَيْسِر»، ونحو ذلك. تعلم أشياء من مسمياتها، ومنها ما لا يعلم إلا ببيان آخر. فإنه قد يكون الشيء داخلاً في اسم «الربوا» والمَيْسِر والإنسان لا يعلم ذلك إلا بدليل على ذلك ـ شرعي، أو غيره. ومن هذا قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، لما سُئل عن حد «الغيبة»، فقال: «ذِكْرُكَ أخاك بما يكره. فقال له أرأيت إن كان في أخي ما أقول؟» فقال: «إن كان فيه ما تقول فقد اغْتَبْتَه ، وإن لم يكن فيه ما تقول فقد بَهَنَّه». وكذلك قوله لما قال : «لا يدخل الجنة مَنْ في قلبه مثقال ذرة من كبر». فقال رجل : يا رسول الله ! الرجلُ يحب أن يكون ثوبه حسناً، ونعله حسناً، أفمن الكبر ذلك؟» فقال : ( لا ) . الكبر بَطَرُ الحق وغمط الناس . وكذلك لما قيل له: «ما الإسلام؟» و «ما الإيمان؟» و«ما الإحسان؟» ولما سئل عن أشياء : أهي من الخمر ؟ » وغير ذلك. وبالجملة، فالحاجة إلى معرفة هذه الحدود ماسَّة لكل أمة، وفي كل لغة . فإن معرفتها من ضرورة التخاطب الذي هو النطق، الذي لا بد منه لبني آدم .
And it may be well-known, but its definition is not known, rather its meaning is known in a general way, like the name 'prayer', 'zakat', 'fasting', and 'hajj'. For these, although the majority of addressees know their meaning in a general way, they do not know their referent in a comprehensive and exclusive way except from the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, and these are called 'religious terms'. As when it is said: funeral prayer, the two prostrations of forgetfulness, the prostration of thanksgiving, and circumambulation—do they enter into the name 'prayer' in his saying, peace be upon him: 'The key to prayer is purification, its prohibition is the takbir, and its release is the taslim'? So it was said: all of that is prayer, and purification is obligatory for it; and it was said: purification is not obligatory for any of that; and it was said: it is obligatory for that whose prohibition is the takbir and its release is the taslim, like the funeral prayer and the two prostrations of forgetfulness, but not circumambulation or the prostration of recitation. Likewise the name 'wine', 'usury', 'gambling', and the like. Some things belonging to their referents are known, and some are not known except by another explanation. For a thing may enter into the name 'usury' and 'gambling' but a person does not know that except by a proof—legal or otherwise. And from this is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he was asked about the definition of 'backbiting': he said: 'Your mentioning your brother with something he dislikes.' He said: 'What if what I say is true about my brother?' He said: 'If it is true what you say, then you have backbitten him, and if it is not true, then you have slandered him.' And likewise his saying when he said: 'No one enters paradise who has an atom's weight of pride in his heart.' A man said: O Messenger of God, a man loves that his garment be nice and his sandal be nice—is that from pride?' He said: 'No, pride is rejection of the truth and looking down on people.' And likewise when it was said to him: 'What is Islam?' and 'What is Iman?' and 'What is Ihsan?' and when he was asked about things: 'Are they from wine?' etc. In summary, the need to know these definitions is pressing for every nation and in every language. For knowing them is necessary for the discourse which is speech, which is indispensable for the sons of Adam.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'religious terms' (al-asma' al-shar'iyya) and illustrates the need for revelation in defining them. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that some terms, though commonly known in a general sense, cannot be precisely defined (hadd) except through the Prophet. Examples include salah, zakat, siyam, and hajj. He then highlights a debate about whether certain acts (e.g., funeral prayer, prostrations of forgetfulness, circumambulation) fall under the name 'salah' in the hadith 'The key to prayer is purification...' Different scholars held different views, showing that the precise boundary of such terms is not fixed by language alone. Similarly, terms like khamr, riba, and maysir have known instances but require further evidence to determine inclusion of borderline cases. Ibn Taymiyya then cites two Prophetic definitions: the definition of ghiba (backbiting) as 'your mentioning your brother with something he dislikes,' with the nuance that if true it is backbiting, if false it is slander (buhtan); and the definition of kibr (pride) as 'rejection of truth and looking down on people' (batar al-haqq wa ghamt al-nas). These examples show that religious terms are defined by revelation and not by mere linguistic convention. The passage concludes by emphasizing the universal necessity of such definitions for all nations and languages, as they are essential for communication.

Key Terms
الأسماء الشرعية
حد
الغيبة
الكبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because their precise definition is known only from revelation
C) Because they are used in all languages
D) Because they are abstract concepts

Key Takeaway
Religious terms are defined by revelation, not by linguistic convention alone.

Prophetic Definitions: Ghiba and Kibr
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples of Prophetic definitions to illustrate how religious terms are defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the Prophetic definitions of backbiting (ghiba) and pride (kibr) and identify their genus and differentia.

Core Idea
The Prophet's definitions are precise, comprehensive, and exclusive, serving as models for religious definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya presents two clear-cut definitions from the Prophet that illustrate how religious terms are defined. These definitions are not merely linguistic but are authoritative and logically structured.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of ghiba
Find the hadith: 'Your mentioning your brother with something he dislikes.' Note that truthfulness is not a defense.
The Prophet distinguishes between backbiting (true) and slander (false).
2
Identify the genus and differentia for ghiba
Genus: mentioning (dhikr); differentia: your brother with something he dislikes. This is a precise limitation.
The definition covers all instances and excludes praise or mentioning in the presence.
3
Extract the definition of kibr
Find: 'Pride is rejection of the truth and looking down on people.'
This consists of two elements: batar al-haqq (rejection of truth) and ghamt al-nas (looking down on people).
4
Test completeness
Check if these definitions are jami' mani' (comprehensive and exclusive). Does the definition of kibr exclude loving nice clothes? The Prophet explicitly excludes that.
The example shows the definition must be applied carefully.

Divisions of Verbal Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya classifies verbal definitions into synonymous and non-synonymous equivalents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between synonymous (muradif) and non-synonymous equivalent (mukafi') verbal definitions and identify examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Verbal definitions clarify terms by synonyms or descriptive equivalents.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya categorizes verbal definitions into two types: those that give a synonym (muradif) and those that give a non-synonymous equivalent (mukafi') that describes the essence with another attribute. Understanding this distinction helps you see how classical Islamic scholars defined key concepts using both literal synonyms and broader descriptions. We will explore his examples from the Qur'anic and linguistic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the two categories Ibn Taymiyya mentions: synonymous (muradif) and equivalent non-synonymous (mukafi').
The mukafi' indicates the same essence but adds a different attribute.
2
Understand the equivalent type
Note that the mukafi' is not a synonym but a descriptive equivalent that conveys the essence via another attribute.
3
Examine the examples
For al-sirat al-mustaqim, the answers 'Islam', 'following the Quran', etc., are mukafi'. For al-sarim, 'al-muhannad' is a muradif synonym.
4
Apply to other Islamic terms
Think of how you might define 'salah' verbally: synonym 'prayer' or equivalent 'act of worship with specific pillars'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام «الحدود اللفظية» وهذا الذي يقال له حدّ بحسب الاسم، والمقول في جواب «ما هو؟» مرادف أو النوع. وقد يكون اسماً مرادفاً، وقد يكون مكافياً غير مرادف بحيث يدل على الذات صفة أخرى. كما إذا قال ما الصراط المستقيم؟ فقال: هو «الإسلام»، واتباع القرآن»، أو «طاعة الله ورسوله»، و«العلم النافع والعمل الصالح». أو «ما الصارم؟ فقيل : هو «المهند». وما أشبه ذلك .
Divisions of 'verbal definitions'. And this is what is called a definition according to the name, and what is said in answer to 'What is it?' is a synonym or the species. It may be a synonymous name, and it may be an equivalent non-synonymous term that indicates the essence with another attribute. As when one says: 'What is the straight path?' and he says: 'It is Islam, following the Quran, or obedience to God and His Messenger, beneficial knowledge and righteous action.' Or 'What is a sword?' and it is said: 'It is the blade (al-muhannad).' And the like.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage classifies 'verbal definitions' (al-hudud al-lafziyya). Ibn Taymiyya defines a verbal definition as an expression that answers 'What is it?' (ma huwa?) and provides either a synonym (ism muradif) or a non-synonymous equivalent (mukafi') that describes the essence with another attribute. He gives examples: for 'straight path' (al-sirat al-mustaqim), one may say 'Islam,' 'following the Quran,' or 'obedience to God and His Messenger, beneficial knowledge and righteous action'—these are non-synonymous equivalent descriptions. For 'sharp sword' (al-sarim), one may say 'al-muhannad' (a fine sword), which is a synonym. Thus, verbal definitions can be either synonymous or descriptive equivalents, and they serve to clarify the meaning of a term by providing a more familiar or precise expression.

Key Terms
حد لفظي (hadd lafzi): A definition that is expressed in words, providing the meaning of a name by a synonym or equivalent description. [Note: No significant shift — this is a standard categorization in usul al-fiqh and logic.]
مرادف (muradif): A synonym: a word that means the same as the defined term. [Note: In Arabic lexicography, muradif is used for absolute synonyms; later logicians may distinguish shades.]
مكافئ (mukafi'): A non-synonymous equivalent: a word that indicates the same essence but adds a different attribute. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this term specifically for definitions that describe the essence via a property.]

Three Types of Verbal Definitions: Synonym, Equivalent, and Example
Author enumerates three methods of verbal definition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of verbal definitions (synonym, equivalent, example) in Islamic logical texts and apply them to clarify terms in Quranic exegesis and Fiqh.

Core Idea
Verbal definitions go beyond synonyms: they include equivalent expressions and concrete examples, all essential for precise communication.

In classical Islamic logic, a verbal definition (al-hadd al-lafzi) is the answer to 'What is it?' (ma huwa). Ibn Taymiyya explains that this definition can be given in three ways: by a synonym, by an equivalent non-synonymous expression, or by pointing to an example. These methods are not only theoretical but are used in everyday discourse and in interpreting key Islamic concepts. In this lesson, we will explore each type with examples from the Quranic description of the Straight Path and the categorization of believers in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal need for verbal definitions
The author states that definitions are necessary for discourse across all nations and languages. Notice that this is a foundational claim for why definitions matter.
2
Understand the first type: synonym (muradif)
The definiens is a word that can replace the definiendum without change in meaning. Example: 'al-sarim' defined as 'al-muhannad' (a type of Indian sword).
3
Understand the second type: equivalent (mukafin)
The definiens indicates the essence with an additional attribute. Example: 'al-sirat al-mustaqim' defined as 'Islam' or 'following the Qur'an' — these are not exact synonyms but convey the essence with extra description.
4
Understand the third type: example (tamthil)
The answer points to a concrete instance. Example: showing a loaf for 'bread'. Also, the detailed example of 'muqtasid, sabiq, zalim' in prayer — each is defined by describing their actions, which serve as examples of the categories.
5
Apply the framework
Given a term from Islamic sciences, identify which type of verbal definition is most appropriate and explain why.

The second station - the fourth aspect: Sections of verbal definitions. In summary, the need to know these definitions is pressing for every nation, in every language. For knowing them is necessary for discourse, which is speech, which is indispensable for human beings. Sections of verbal definitions. This is what is called a definition with respect to the name, and what is said in answer to 'What is it?' is a synonym or the type. It may be a synonymous name, or it may be an equivalent non-synonymous name that indicates the essence along with another attribute. As when it is said 'What is the straight path?' and he says: 'It is Islam,' or 'following the Qur'an,' or 'obedience to Allah and His messenger,' or 'beneficial knowledge and righteous action.' Or 'What is a sharp sword?' and it is said: 'It is the Indian sword.' And similar to that. And the answer may be by example. As when asked about the word 'bread' and he sees a loaf and says: 'This!' For knowledge of an individual gives knowledge of the species. An example of the moderate, the foremost, and the wrongdoer: when asked about 'the moderate,' 'the foremost,' and 'the wrongdoer,' he says: 'The moderate is one who prays the obligatory prayer on time and does not add; the wrongdoer is one who delays it beyond its time; and the foremost is one who prays it at the beginning of its time and adds supererogatory prayers.' And similar to that of explanation, which is exemplification that benefits the definition of the named by the example to bring it to mind, not because the questioner did not know the one who prays at the beginning of time nor during it nor the one who delays it beyond its time, but he did not know that these three are examples of the wrongdoer, the moderate, and the foremost. So when he knows that, he analogizes to what resembles it of the one who confines himself to the obligatory, the one who exceeds it, and the one who falls short of it.

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of verbal definitions (al-hudud al-lafziyya). The author begins by asserting the universal need for such definitions for human discourse. He then specifies that a verbal definition is given in answer to 'What is it?' (ma huwa). The first type is a synonym (ism muradif), where the definiens is interchangeable with the definiendum. The second type is an equivalent non-synonymous definition (mukafin ghayr muradif), which indicates the essence with an additional attribute. Examples from Islamic teachings illustrate this: defining 'al-sirat al-mustaqim' (the straight path) as 'Islam' or 'following the Qur'an,' and defining 'al-sarim' (sharp sword) as 'al-muhannad' (a specific type of Indian sword). The third type is definition by example (tamthil). This occurs when the answer points to a concrete instance, such as showing a loaf of bread when asked about 'bread.' The author elaborates with a detailed example from the Quranic categories of 'al-muqtasid,' 'al-sabiq,' and 'al-zalim' (the moderate, the foremost, the wrongdoer) in matters of prayer. The explanation describes each by their actions, serving as a definition through exemplification. The purpose is to bring the meaning to mind, not because the questioner is ignorant of the actions themselves, but because he does not know that these are examples of the categories. Once known, he can analogize to similar cases. Thus, the passage classifies verbal definitions into synonym, equivalent, and example, with clear illustrations from Islamic sources.

Three Categories of Names in Revelation: Linguistic, Religious, and Conventional
Author classifies names in revelation into three categories based on the source of their definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any name from the Quran and Sunnah into one of the three categories: linguistic, religious, or conventional.

Core Idea
Not all words in revelation are defined by language; some need Sharia or custom.

When interpreting the Quran and Sunnah, we often assume that the meanings of words are simply what we find in dictionaries. Ibn Taymiyya corrects this assumption by showing that there are three distinct sources of definitions: language, religious law, and conventional usage. Understanding this classification is essential to avoid errors in understanding the primary texts of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the term's meaning
Is the term a natural object (sun, moon), a religious obligation/prohibition (prayer, usury), or a social contract (marriage, sale)? This initial identification helps you decide which category applies.
2
Determine if the term's meaning is fixed by language alone
If the term refers to a physical object or action that does not vary across legal contexts, it is linguistic. For example, 'sun' and 'moon' are purely linguistic.
3
Check if the term's definition requires knowledge of Sharia
If the term is a specific act of worship or a prohibited category defined by revelation, it is religious. For example, 'prayer' (salat) and 'usury' (riba) require Sharia to define their precise boundaries.
4
Recognize terms defined by custom
If the term's meaning is established by common usage in human transactions and varies with custom, it is conventional. For example, 'marriage' (nikah) and 'sale' (bay') are understood through customary practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن معرفة حدود هذه الأسماء في الغالب تحصلُ بغير سؤال لمن يباشر المتخاطبين بتلك اللغة، أو يقرأ كتبهم ؛ فإن معرفة معاني اللغات تقع كذلك . وهذه الحدود قد يَظُنّ بعض الناس أنها حدود لغوية يكفي في معرفتها العلم باللغة، والكتب المصنفة في اللغة وكتب الترجمة ؛ وليس كذلك على الإطلاق . بل الأسماء المذكورة في الكتاب والسنة ثلاثة أصناف : (ألف) منها ما يعرف حده بـ «اللغة» كالشمس والقمر والكوكب ونحو ذلك ؛ ( ب ) ومنها ما لا يعرف إلا بـ «الشرع» كأسماء الواجبات الشرعية والمحرمات الشرعية، كالصلوة، والحج، والربوا، والميسر ؛ (ج) ومنها ما يعرف بـ«العرف العادي - وهو عرف الخطاب باللفظ كاسم النكاح، والبيع، والقبض، وغير ذلك. هذا في معرفة حدودها التي هي مسمياتها على العموم .
Then, the knowledge of the definitions of these names is generally obtained without questioning by one who interacts with speakers of that language, or reads their books; for knowledge of the meanings of languages occurs likewise. And these definitions, some people may think they are linguistic definitions sufficient to know them by knowledge of the language, and the books compiled in the language and translation books; but it is not so absolutely. Rather, the names mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah are three categories: (a) those whose definition is known by language, like the sun, the moon, the star, and the like; (b) those which are known only by religious law (shar'), like the names of religious obligations and religious prohibitions, such as prayer, pilgrimage, usury, gambling; (c) and those which are known by conventional usage ('urf 'adi) – and it is the custom of discourse with the word – like the name of marriage, sale, possession, and others. This is regarding knowledge of their definitions, which are their referents in general.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses a common misconception: that all definitions of names in revelation are purely linguistic. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that the definitions of these names are generally acquired through exposure to speakers or reading their books, but they are not all the same. He categorizes names in the Quran and Sunnah into three types. The first type is defined by language (lugha), such as natural objects like the sun, moon, and stars. Their meanings are understood purely through linguistic knowledge. The second type is defined only by religious law (shar'), such as the names of obligatory acts (prayer, pilgrimage) and prohibited acts (usury, gambling). These require knowledge of the Sharia to understand their specific definitions. The third type is defined by conventional usage ('urf 'adi), which is the customary discourse of the people, such as marriage (nikah), sale (bay'), and possession (qabd). Their meanings are determined by common practice. Thus, the author emphasizes that one cannot rely solely on linguistic dictionaries to understand religious texts; one must also consider the legal and conventional contexts. This classification is essential for proper interpretation of the primary sources of Islam.

Key Terms
لغة (lugha): Linguistic definition based on the lexical meaning of a word, without consideration of religious or conventional specifics. [Note: Stable across Islamic sciences; distinct from istilah (technical term).]
شرع (shar'): Religious law; definitions established by divine revelation (Quran and Sunnah), which may differ from common linguistic usage. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, shar' is the source of legal definitions; here it is contrasted with language and custom.]
عرف عادي ('urf 'adi): Conventional usage; the customary meaning of a term in everyday discourse, not derived from language or religious law. [Note: In later usul, 'urf is a source of law; here it is a category of definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Religious definition
C) Conventional definition
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The meanings of words in revelation are not monolithic; they derive from three different sources, and interpreters must identify which source applies.

Definition by Example in Islamic Discourse
Ibn Taymiyya illustrates definition by example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition by example and formal logical definition, and apply the method of definition by example to terms in the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Definition by example clarifies meaning without formal genus and differentia.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that not all definitions require the formal structure of genus and differentia. Sometimes the best way to define a term is by pointing to an instance or describing a typical case. This method, called al-jawab bil-mithal, is essential for understanding many Quranic categories like muqtasid, sabiq, and zalim. In this lesson, you will learn how to identify and use this type of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the terms muqtasid, sabiq, zalim in the example. Ibn Taymiyya defines these by describing their actions.
The terms are from Quran 35:32.
2
Extract the example-based description
Notice that the definition is a description of actions, not a formal logical definition. The questioner knows the actions but not the category names.
3
Contrast with formal hadd
Compare this method to the logical definition with genus and differentia. Here, the definition works by bringing the thing to mind through an example.
Formal hadd would specify a genus and a differentia, which is not present here.
4
Apply to a new Islamic term
Try to define 'musalli' (one who prays) by example. Describe the actions of a typical musalli without using formal categories.
This helps internalize the method.

The answer by example: like when asked about the word 'bread', and he sees a loaf, and says: 'This!' For the knowledge of the person knows from it the 'type'. And when asked about 'the moderate', 'the foremost', and 'the unjust', and he says: 'The moderate' is the one who prays the obligatory prayer at its time and does not increase; and 'the unjust' is the one who delays it beyond the time; and 'the foremost' is the one who prays it at the beginning of the time and adds the regular supererogatory prayers; and similar explanation which is representation that benefits defining the named by 'the example' to bring it to mind, not because the questioner did not know the one praying at the beginning of time and during it and the one delaying beyond the time, but he did not know that these three are examples of 'the unjust', 'the moderate', and 'the foremost'. So when he knows that, he analogizes to what resembles it from 'the one who restricts to the obligatory', 'the one who increases over it', and 'the one who is deficient from it'. Then indeed the knowledge of the definitions of these names mostly occurs without asking for whoever directly addresses speakers of that language, or reads their books; for the knowledge of meanings of languages occurs likewise. And these definitions, some people suppose that they are linguistic definitions sufficient for knowing them through knowledge of language, and books compiled in language and translation books; but it is not so absolutely. Rather, the names mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah are three kinds: (a) those whose definition is known by 'language' like the sun, the moon, the star and the like; (b) and those which are not known except by 'the Law' like the names of legal obligations and legal prohibitions, like prayer, pilgrimage, usury, and gambling; (c) and those which are known by 'customary usage' which is the usage of discourse by the word like the name of marriage, sale, possession, and others. This is regarding the knowledge of their definitions which are what they refer to in general.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage discusses the method of definition by example (jawab bil-mithal) and then classifies the types of names in the Quran and Sunnah. Ibn Taymiyya begins by illustrating definition by example: when asked about the word 'khubz' (bread), one can point to a loaf and say 'this'. Similarly, when asked about the categories 'muqtasid' (moderate), 'sabiq' (foremost), and 'zalim' (unjust) from Quran 35:32, one can describe their actions: the moderate prays the obligatory prayer on time without extra, the unjust delays it, and the foremost prays early and adds supererogatory prayers. This type of definition works by bringing the thing to mind, not because the questioner is unaware of the actions but because they do not know that these are the categories. Ibn Taymiyya then notes that knowledge of such definitions often comes from direct engagement with speakers of the language or from reading their books, not from formal inquiries. He then refutes the notion that all definitions are purely linguistic. Some people mistakenly think that definitions of terms in revelation can be obtained solely from language dictionaries or translation books. But this is not absolute. The names in the Quran and Sunnah fall into three categories: (1) Linguistic: known by language alone, e.g., sun, moon, star; (2) Legal: known only by Sharia, e.g., prayer, pilgrimage, usury, gambling; (3) Customary: known by common usage in discourse, e.g., marriage, sale, possession. This classification shows that understanding the meanings of these terms requires more than just linguistic knowledge; it requires knowledge of the Sharia and customary practices. All examples are drawn from Islamic sciences: prayer, hajj, etc.

Three Types of Names in the Quran and Sunnah
Ibn Taymiyya classifies names into three types based on their source of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any term from the Quran and Sunnah as linguistic, legal, or customary based on its source of definition.

Core Idea
Not all terms are defined by language; some require Sharia, others custom.

Ibn Taymiyya refutes the notion that all definitions in the Islamic tradition are purely linguistic. He categorizes names in the Book and Sunnah into three types: those known by language (like sun and moon), those known only by Sharia (like prayer and pilgrimage), and those known by customary usage (like marriage and sale). This classification is crucial for proper understanding of Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three categories: linguistic, legal, and customary. Understand that each has a different source of definition.
The categories are exhaustive according to Ibn Taymiyya.
2
Match examples to categories
For each example Ibn Taymiyya gives (sun, prayer, marriage), determine which source defines it. Sun is linguistic, prayer is legal, marriage is customary.
Legal terms require knowledge of Sharia; customary terms require knowledge of common usage.
3
Understand why this matters
Realize that using the wrong source leads to misinterpretation. For example, defining salat (prayer) by language alone would miss its Sharia meaning.
This is a common mistake that Ibn Taymiyya warns against.
4
Apply to a new term
Classify 'zakat' as legal, 'bayt' (house) as linguistic, 'qabd' (possession) as customary. Provide reasoning.
Test your understanding by classifying other terms from the Quran.

The answer by example: like when asked about the word 'bread', and he sees a loaf, and says: 'This!' For the knowledge of the person knows from it the 'type'. Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Essence-Existence Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya identifies two false principles and refutes the first: essence vs. existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two corrupt principles and explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the essence-existence distinction.

Core Idea
The essence-existence distinction is a mental projection, not an external reality.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique by exposing two foundational errors in the logicians' system. The first is the separation of essence (mahiyya) from existence (wujud) in the external world. He argues that this mistake stems from confusing mental conception with external reality. In this lesson, we trace his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two corrupt principles
Read the second paragraph: 'وهذا الكلام الذي ذكروه مبني على أصلين فاسدين'. What are these two principles? One is the distinction between mahiyya and wujud, the other is between essential and accidental.
Ibn Taymiyya only refutes the first here.
2
Understand the logicians' claim about mahiyya
The logicians say mahiyya has a fixed reality in the external world separate from its existence. They think this because a thing can be known before it exists.
This is the first principle.
3
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He argues that this is a confusion: what is known is in the mind, not externally. He compares it to saying 'non-existence is a thing', which is utterly corrupt.
He emphasizes that mental existence is not external existence.

Their statement is based, according to their leaders like Ibn Sina and others, on what is coupled with 'lam' in your saying 'li-annahu' (because), meaning the proof. Some of their later scholars like al-Razi understood it as an attribute subsisting in the described, so confusion increased... corruption. Then they contradicted themselves in this matter, as I have elaborated elsewhere when I transmitted their statements and contradictions, as I mentioned from Ibn Sina's 'Isharat' and other statements of theirs, since I did not here narrate their very words; rather the intent is to alert to the comprehensive points by which the falsity of many of their logical statements becomes apparent. And this speech they mentioned is built upon two corrupt principles: the distinction between 'mahiyya' and its existence; then the distinction between its essential and its accidental. So the first principle: their statement that the mahiyya has a fixed reality in the external world other than its existence. This is their statement that 'the realities of absolute species—which are the mahiyyas of species, genera, and all universals—exist in concrete individuals.' It resembles, in some respects, the statement of one who says 'non-existence is a thing.' I have elaborated on that elsewhere. This is among the most corrupt things. The origin of their error is that they saw that a thing before its existence is known, desired, and distinguished between the possible and impossible, etc. So they said: 'If it were not established, it would not be so, just as we speak about the realities of things which are their mahiyyas regardless of their existence in the external world.' Thus they imagined erroneously that these realities and mahiyyas are things established in the external world. The investigation is that all of that is something existing and established in the mind, not in the external realm. What is estimated in minds may be broader than what exists in concrete individuals. It is existing and established in the mind, and in reality it is neither existing nor established. So distinguishing between 'wujud' and 'thubut' while claiming that both are in the external world is a great error; likewise distinguishing between 'wujud' and 'mahiyya' while claiming that both are in the external world. The confusion arose from the fact that it became customary to call what exists in the mind 'mahiyya,' taking the term 'mahiyya' from 'ma huwa?' like other names taken from interrogative sentences, as they say 'kayfiyya' and 'ayniyya.' It is said 'mahiyya' and 'mayiyya,' and they are derived names. It is what is said in answer to 'ma huwa?' to depict the thing in the soul of the questioner. Since 'mahiyya' is attributed to the question 'ma huwa?', and the questioner only requests the depiction of the thing in itself, the answer is what is said in response to 'ma huwa?' depicting the thing in the soul of the questioner, which is mental establishment, whether that statement exists in the external world or not. Thus, by convention, 'mahiyya' is mostly applied to what is in the mind, and 'wujud' is applied to what is in the external world. This is a verbal, conventional matter. And if qualified, it is said: 'mental existence is the mahiyya that is in the mind'; and if it is said: 'the mahiyya of a thing in the external world is the very existence in the external world.' So the existence of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in the external world, as the leading thinkers among Ahl al-Sunna and all people of affirmation among the theologians, such as Abu Muhammad ibn Kullab, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Abu Abdullah ibn Karram and their followers—let alone the leading Salaf and great imams—have agreed upon. They agreed that 'non-existent' has no essence in the external world before its existence. As for in the mind, the very mahiyya that is in the mind is also its existence in the mind. If by 'mahiyya' is meant what is in the mind and by 'wujud' what is in the external world, then this mahiyya is other than existence. But that does not...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' distinction between essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud). Ibn Taymiyya begins by identifying that the logicians' discourse is based on two false principles: (1) the distinction between mahiyya and its existence, and (2) the distinction between essential and accidental attributes. He then focuses on the first principle. 

The logicians claim that mahiyya has a fixed reality in the external world distinct from its existence. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the statement 'non-existence is a thing,' which he considers utterly corrupt. He traces the origin of their error: they observe that a thing can be known, desired, and distinguished before its existence, and then mistakenly conclude that its reality must be established externally. 

The correct view, Ibn Taymiyya argues, is that such mental content exists and is established only in the mind, not externally. He emphasizes that what is estimated in the mind may be broader than what exists in external reality. He then attacks the logicians' distinction between 'wujud' (existence) and 'thubut' (establishment) as a great error, since both are claimed to be external. 

Ibn Taymiyya explains the linguistic origin of the confusion: the term 'mahiyya' is derived from the interrogative 'ma huwa?' (what is it?), which asks for a mental depiction. By convention, 'mahiyya' came to refer to what is in the mind, while 'wujud' refers to what is external. This is merely a verbal convention, not a real distinction in external reality.

He then presents the correct doctrine, agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna and leading theologians: the existence of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in the external world. Similarly, in the mind, the mahiyya is identical to its mental existence. If one distinguishes terminologically, then mahiyya (mental) and wujud (external) are different, but this is only a conventional distinction, not a real one. The passage concludes by affirming that non-existent things have no essence externally before existence.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya): In this passage, mahiyya denotes the mental concept or essence that answers 'what is it?' (ma huwa?), existing only in the mind by convention, not as a separate reality in the external world. [Note: In earlier Islamic philosophy (e.g., Ibn Sina), mahiyya often refers to a real essence that can be considered apart from existence. Ibn Taymiyya redefines it as purely mental.]
الوجود (al-wujūd): Wujud refers to external existence or reality, as opposed to mental existence. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the wujud of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in that realm. [Note: In the Peripatetic tradition, wujud is often an accident added to essence; Ibn Taymiyya rejects this and asserts identity.]
الثبوت (al-thubūt): Thubut means 'establishment' or 'fixedness.' Ibn Taymiyya uses it to refer to the mental 'existence' or conceptual stability, which he distinguishes from external existence. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, thubut is used for certainty of legal evidence; here it is contrasted with wujud in an ontological sense.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, why do logicians mistakenly think mahiyya has an extramental reality?
A) Because they confuse mental existence with external existence.
B) Because they rely on revelation rather than reason.
C) Because they follow Aristotle uncritically.
D) Because they deny the existence of universals.

Key Takeaway
The essence-existence distinction is a mental convention, not a real separation in the external world.

Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, evaluate the statement: 'The possibility of a creation before it exists is a real property in the external world.' Determine whether this is a similar confusion of mental and external existence.

The Linguistic Origin and the Identity of Essence and Existence
Ibn Taymiyya explains the linguistic origin of the mahiyya-wujud distinction and the correct identity doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic origin of the mahiyya-wujud distinction and summarize the correct doctrine of the identity of essence and existence in external reality.

Core Idea
Mahiyya and wujud are distinct by convention, identical in reality.

Ibn Taymiyya further deconstructs the logicians' error by tracing the term 'mahiyya' to its linguistic roots. He shows that the distinction is merely conventional and that, in external reality, a thing's essence is identical to its existence. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic derivation of 'mahiyya'
Ibn Taymiyya explains that 'mahiyya' comes from the question 'ma huwa?' which asks for a mental picture. This shows the term's natural home is the mind.
Similar derived terms: kayfiyya, ayniyya.
2
Understand the conventional distinction
By convention, 'mahiyya' is used for what is in the mind, and 'wujud' for what is external. This is a verbal convention, not a real separation.
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly calls it 'amr lafzi istilahi'.
3
Learn the correct doctrine of identity
In external reality, the existence of a thing is identical to its essence. Many Sunni theologians agree. Similarly, in the mind, the mental mahiyya is identical to mental existence.
Thus, if we keep the conventional terms, mahiyya (mental) is different from wujud (external), but that difference is only terminological.

Source Text
