فالأصل الأول : قولهم إن الماهية لها حقيقة ثابتةٌ في الخارج غيرَ وجودها . وهذا هو قولهم بـ «أن حقائق الأنواع المطلقة - التي هي ماهيات الأنواع والأجناس وسائر الكليات - موجودة في الأعيان». وهو يُشبه - من بعض الوجوه - قولَ مَنْ يقول : «المعدوم شيء». وقد بسطت الكلام على ذلك في غير هذا الموضع . وهذا من أفسد ما يكون .
وإنما أصلُ ضلالهم أنهم رأوا الشيء قبل وجوده - يُعلم، ويُراد، ويميز بين المقدور عليه والمعجوز عنه، ونحو ذلك . فقالوا : ولو لم يكن ثابتاً لما كان كذلك، كما أنا نتكلم في حقائق الأشياء التي هي ماهياتها مع قطع النظر عن وجودها في الخارج، فنتخيل الغلط أن هذه الحقائق والماهيات أمورٌ ثابتة في الخارج.
التفريق بين والتحقيق أن ذلك كله أمر موجود وثابت في الذهن، لا في الخارج عن الذهن. والمقدَّرُ في الأذهان قد يكون أوسعَ من الموجود في الأعيان. وهو موجود وثابت في الذهن، وليس هو في نفس الأمر ، لا موجوداً ولا ثابتاً. فالتفريق بين الوجود / و «الثبوت» مع دعوى أن كليهما في الخارج غلط عظيم ؛ وكذلك التفريق بين «الوجود»
و «الماهية» مع دعوى أن كليهما في الخارج .
وإنما نشأت الشبهة من جهة أنه غلب على أن ما يوجد في الذهن [٥٧] يوجد في الذهن [٥٧] يسمى مأخذ لفظ «ماهية»، وما يوجد في الخارج يسمى «وجوداً» . لأن الماهية» مأخوذة من قولهم «ما هو ؟ كسائر الأسماء المأخوذة من الجُمَل الاستفهامية، كما يقولون «الكيفية» و «الأينية». ويقال «ماهية» و «مايية»، وهي أسماء مولدة. وهي المَقُول في جواب «ما هو ؟» بما يصوّر الشيء في نفس السائل . فلما كانت الماهية منسوبة إلى الاستفهام بـ «ما هو ؟»، والمستفهم إنما يطلب الماهية» تصوير الشيء في نفسه، كان الجواب عنها هو المقول في جواب «ما هو ؟» بما يصور الشيء في نفس السائل ، وهو الثبوتُ الذهني، سواء كان ذلك المقول موجوداً في الخارج أو لم يكن . فصار بحكم الاصطلاح أكثر ما يُطلَق الماهية» على ما في الذهن، ويُطلق «الوجود» على ما في الخارج. فهذا أمر لفظي اصطلاحي .
وإذا قيد، وقيل: «الوجود الذهني كان هو الماهية التي في الذهن»، وإذا قيل: ماهية الشيء في الخارج كان هو عين وجوده الذي في الخارج» .
فوجود الشيء في الخارج عين ماهيته في الخارج، كما اتفق على ذلك أئمة النظار المنتسبين إلى أهل السنة والجماعة وسائر أهل الإثبات من المتكلمة الصفاتية وغيرهم كأبي محمد بن كلاب وأبي الحسن الأشعري، وأبي عبد الله بن كرام، وأتباعهم - دع أئمة أهل السنة والجماعة من السلف، والأئمة الكبار .
واتفقوا على أن «المعدوم» ليس له في الخارج ذات قبل وجوده. وأما في الذهن، فنفس ماهيته التي في الذهن هي أيضاً وجوده الذي في الذهن. وإذا أريد بـ «الماهية» ما في الذهن وبـ «الوجود ما في الخارج كانت هذه الماهية غير الوجود». لكن ذلك لا
Their statement is based, according to their leaders like Ibn Sina and others, on what is coupled with 'lam' in your saying 'li-annahu' (because), meaning the proof. Some of their later scholars like al-Razi understood it as an attribute subsisting in the described, so confusion increased... corruption. Then they contradicted themselves in this matter, as I have elaborated elsewhere when I transmitted their statements and contradictions, as I mentioned from Ibn Sina's 'Isharat' and other statements of theirs, since I did not here narrate their very words; rather the intent is to alert to the comprehensive points by which the falsity of many of their logical statements becomes apparent. And this speech they mentioned is built upon two corrupt principles: the distinction between 'mahiyya' and its existence; then the distinction between its essential and its accidental. So the first principle: their statement that the mahiyya has a fixed reality in the external world other than its existence. This is their statement that 'the realities of absolute species—which are the mahiyyas of species, genera, and all universals—exist in concrete individuals.' It resembles, in some respects, the statement of one who says 'non-existence is a thing.' I have elaborated on that elsewhere. This is among the most corrupt things. The origin of their error is that they saw that a thing before its existence is known, desired, and distinguished between the possible and impossible, etc. So they said: 'If it were not established, it would not be so, just as we speak about the realities of things which are their mahiyyas regardless of their existence in the external world.' Thus they imagined erroneously that these realities and mahiyyas are things established in the external world. The investigation is that all of that is something existing and established in the mind, not in the external realm. What is estimated in minds may be broader than what exists in concrete individuals. It is existing and established in the mind, and in reality it is neither existing nor established. So distinguishing between 'wujud' and 'thubut' while claiming that both are in the external world is a great error; likewise distinguishing between 'wujud' and 'mahiyya' while claiming that both are in the external world. The confusion arose from the fact that it became customary to call what exists in the mind 'mahiyya,' taking the term 'mahiyya' from 'ma huwa?' like other names taken from interrogative sentences, as they say 'kayfiyya' and 'ayniyya.' It is said 'mahiyya' and 'mayiyya,' and they are derived names. It is what is said in answer to 'ma huwa?' to depict the thing in the soul of the questioner. Since 'mahiyya' is attributed to the question 'ma huwa?', and the questioner only requests the depiction of the thing in itself, the answer is what is said in response to 'ma huwa?' depicting the thing in the soul of the questioner, which is mental establishment, whether that statement exists in the external world or not. Thus, by convention, 'mahiyya' is mostly applied to what is in the mind, and 'wujud' is applied to what is in the external world. This is a verbal, conventional matter. And if qualified, it is said: 'mental existence is the mahiyya that is in the mind'; and if it is said: 'the mahiyya of a thing in the external world is the very existence in the external world.' So the existence of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in the external world, as the leading thinkers among Ahl al-Sunna and all people of affirmation among the theologians, such as Abu Muhammad ibn Kullab, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Abu Abdullah ibn Karram and their followers—let alone the leading Salaf and great imams—have agreed upon. They agreed that 'non-existent' has no essence in the external world before its existence. As for in the mind, the very mahiyya that is in the mind is also its existence in the mind. If by 'mahiyya' is meant what is in the mind and by 'wujud' what is in the external world, then this mahiyya is other than existence. But that does not...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' distinction between essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud). Ibn Taymiyya begins by identifying that the logicians' discourse is based on two false principles: (1) the distinction between mahiyya and its existence, and (2) the distinction between essential and accidental attributes. He then focuses on the first principle. 

The logicians claim that mahiyya has a fixed reality in the external world distinct from its existence. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the statement 'non-existence is a thing,' which he considers utterly corrupt. He traces the origin of their error: they observe that a thing can be known, desired, and distinguished before its existence, and then mistakenly conclude that its reality must be established externally. 

The correct view, Ibn Taymiyya argues, is that such mental content exists and is established only in the mind, not externally. He emphasizes that what is estimated in the mind may be broader than what exists in external reality. He then attacks the logicians' distinction between 'wujud' (existence) and 'thubut' (establishment) as a great error, since both are claimed to be external. 

Ibn Taymiyya explains the linguistic origin of the confusion: the term 'mahiyya' is derived from the interrogative 'ma huwa?' (what is it?), which asks for a mental depiction. By convention, 'mahiyya' came to refer to what is in the mind, while 'wujud' refers to what is external. This is merely a verbal convention, not a real distinction in external reality.

He then presents the correct doctrine, agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna and leading theologians: the existence of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in the external world. Similarly, in the mind, the mahiyya is identical to its mental existence. If one distinguishes terminologically, then mahiyya (mental) and wujud (external) are different, but this is only a conventional distinction, not a real one. The passage concludes by affirming that non-existent things have no essence externally before existence.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya): In this passage, mahiyya denotes the mental concept or essence that answers 'what is it?' (ma huwa?), existing only in the mind by convention, not as a separate reality in the external world. [Note: In earlier Islamic philosophy (e.g., Ibn Sina), mahiyya often refers to a real essence that can be considered apart from existence. Ibn Taymiyya redefines it as purely mental.]
الوجود (al-wujūd): Wujud refers to external existence or reality, as opposed to mental existence. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the wujud of a thing in the external world is identical to its mahiyya in that realm. [Note: In the Peripatetic tradition, wujud is often an accident added to essence; Ibn Taymiyya rejects this and asserts identity.]
الثبوت (al-thubūt): Thubut means 'establishment' or 'fixedness.' Ibn Taymiyya uses it to refer to the mental 'existence' or conceptual stability, which he distinguishes from external existence. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, thubut is used for certainty of legal evidence; here it is contrasted with wujud in an ontological sense.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Greek philosophy.
B) The interrogative 'ma huwa?' (what is it?).
C) The word 'wujud' (existence).
D) The Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essence and existence is a verbal convention; in external reality, they are identical.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Critique of Prioritizing Essential Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that essential attributes are prior in conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the logicians' claim that essential attributes are prior in conception, and identify the flaws in their reasoning.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the essential/accidental distinction as arbitrary convention, not grounded in reality.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational doctrine of Aristotelian logic: that some attributes are 'essential' (dhati) and prior in conception, while others are merely accidental. In this passage, he shows that the logicians' examples are inconsistent and that their prioritization of certain attributes is a pure convention, not a reflection of external reality. He uses examples like human, number, and color to demonstrate that all necessary concomitants are equally inseparable from the subject, and that the distinction between inside and outside the essence has no basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the claim that essential attributes (dhati) precede the conception of the subject, and that they are 'constitutive parts' of the quiddity.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's counterexamples
He uses 'human' (rational, animal), 'four' (number, even), 'blackness' (color, accident). He shows that in each case, when the attribute is conceived, the subject cannot be conceived without it, just like the logicians' essential attributes.
Notice the inconsistency: sometimes logicians treat 'coloredness' as essential, sometimes not.
3
Analyze the two-fold refutation
First, the prioritization is a report of their own convention, not a fact about reality. External realities are not dependent on our mental ordering. Second, if it were innate, all people would recognize it without teaching.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
There is no witness in external reality or innate disposition for some attributes being 'inside' the essence and others 'outside'. The distinction is arbitrary.

بطلان تقديم تصور الذاتي ومعلوم أن جميع الصفات اللازمة منها ما هو خاص بالموصوف يصلح أن يكون فصلاً»، ومنها ما هو مشترك بينه وبين غيره وكل منهما في الخارج واحد . فكما أن السوادَ» هو «اللون» وهو «العرض) و هو «القائم بغيره» و «الثلاثة» هي «العدد» وهي «الفرد»، فَـ الإنسان هو الحيوان» وهو «الناطق»، و «الفرس» هو «الحيوان» وهو «الصاهل». وهكذا سائر المحدودات. وما ذكروه من أن ما جعلوه هو «الذاتي» يتقدم تصوره في الذهن لتصور الموصوف، دون الآخر، فباطل من وجهين . أحدهما: إن هذا خبر عن وَضْعِهم، إذ هم يقدّمون هذا في أذهانهم ويؤخرون هذا. وهذا تحكُّم محض . فكل من قَدَّم هذا دون هذا فإنما قلدهم في ذلك . ومعلوم أن الحقائق الخارجية المستغنية عنا لا تكون تابعة لتصوراتنا، بل تصوراتنا تابعة لها. فليس إذا فَرَضْنا هذا مقدَّماً وهذا مؤخراً يكون هذا في الخارج كذلك. وسائر بني آدم الذين لم يقلدوهم في هذا الوضع لا يستحضرون هذا التقديم والتأخير . ولو كان هذا فطرياً لكانت الفطرة تدركه بدون التقليد المغيّر لها، كما تدرك سائر الأمور الفطرية. والذي في الفطرة أن هذه اللوازم كلها لوازم للموصوف، وقد تخطر بالبال وقد لا تخطر، وكلما خطرت كان الإنسان أعلم بالموصوف، وإذا لم تخطر كان علمه بصفاته أقل . أما أن يكون هذا خارجاً عن الذات وهذا داخلاً في الذات، فهذا تحكُّم ليس له شاهد لا في الخارج ولا في الفطرة . لا سيما وهم يقولون: «الذاتي» يتقدم على الماهية في الذهن وفي الخارج، ويسمونه «الجزء المقوّم لها». ويقولون : أجزاء الماهية متقدمة عليها في الذهن وفي الخارج، لأن الماهية مركبة منها ، وكل مركب فإنه مسبوق بمفرداته .
['Natiqiyya' and 'Sahiliyya' for the animal - the human and the horse. Both, when the described with the attribute occurs to the mind, it is not possible to conceive the described without the attribute. And if it is said: It is possible that 'four' and 'three' occur to the mind and be understood without it occurring to the mind that they are an even or odd 'number', it is said: It is possible that 'human' occurs to the mind without it occurring to the mind that he is 'rational' or that he is 'animal'. And if it is said: This is not a complete conception of 'human', it is said: This is not a complete conception of 'four' or 'three'. Likewise the accident which is 'blackness', when it occurs to the mind that it is 'blackness' and it does not occur that it is 'color', or it does not occur to the mind that it is an 'accident' or 'attribute of another' or 'subsisting in another' and so on. For it is not possible to conceive in the mind 'blackness' or whiteness and conceive that it is not 'subsisting in another', rather if they occur together then it must be known that it subsists in another, just as when the body that is sensitive, growing, moving by will occurs to the mind with 'human', then it must be known that it is described by that. Rather the necessity of that for 'color' in the mind is stronger. So making these attributes that are necessary in the mind for these described things not 'essential' to them, and these 'essential' to 'human', is pure arbitrary judgment. Perhaps the opposite is closer - if the distinction between them is valid. And for this they contradict themselves in examples. For they say: 'Animality' is essential to 'animal', like human, horse and others, so the animal is the common essential and 'rational' is the distinguishing essential. And they say: 'Numberhood' is not a common essential to 'even and odd', nor is evenness and oddness a distinguishing essential to 'even and odd'. As for 'coloredness', sometimes they make it like 'animality' and make it essential, and sometimes they make it like 'numberhood' and make it non-essential. The reason for that is their claim that a specific number being even or odd may be hidden and not known except through an intermediate - which is the proof, unlike the fact that human, horse, donkey, camel, monkey and the like are animals, for that is clear to everyone.
Invalidity of prioritizing the conception of the essential: And it is known that all necessary attributes, among them what is specific to the described and suitable to be a differentia, and among them what is common between it and others, and each of them in the external world is one. Just as 'blackness' is 'color' and is 'accident' and is 'subsisting in another', and 'three' is 'number' and is 'odd', so 'human' is 'animal' and is 'rational', and 'horse' is 'animal' and is 'whinnying'. And so on for all defined things. And what they mentioned - that what they made 'essential' its conception precedes in the mind the conception of the described, unlike the other - is false from two aspects. One: This is a report about their convention, as they prioritize this in their minds and postpone that. This is pure arbitrary judgment. So whoever prioritizes this over that only follows them in that. It is known that external realities, independent of us, are not dependent on our conceptions, rather our conceptions are dependent on them. So if we suppose this as prior and that as posterior, that is not so in the external world. And the rest of the children of Adam who do not follow them in this convention do not recall this prioritizing and postponing. And if this were innate, then innate disposition would perceive it without the tradition that alters it, as it perceives other innate matters. What is in the innate disposition is that these concomitants are all concomitants of the described, and they may occur to the mind or not, and whenever they occur the human is more knowledgeable of the described, and if they do not occur his knowledge of its attributes is less. As for this being outside the essence and that being inside the essence, this is arbitrary judgment that has no witness either in the external world or in innate disposition. Especially as they say: The 'essential' precedes the quiddity in the mind and in the external world, and they call it the 'constitutive part' of it. And they say: The parts of the quiddity are prior to it in the mind and in the external world, because the quiddity is composed of them, and every composite is preceded by its components.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' doctrine of essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. He begins by examining the relationship between a concept and its necessary concomitants in the mind. Using examples like 'human' and 'rational' or 'animal', and 'four' and 'even', he shows that when a subject is conceived along with an attribute, it is impossible to conceive the subject without that attribute. However, the logicians arbitrarily deem some of these attributes 'essential' and others 'accidental'. For instance, they consider 'animality' essential to 'animal' but deny that 'numberhood' is essential to 'even and odd'. They contradict themselves in examples, sometimes treating 'coloredness' as essential, sometimes not. The reason they give—that some attributes are known only through a proof—is inconsistent. Ibn Taymiyya then explicitly states the invalidity of prioritizing essential attributes in conception. He argues that all necessary attributes, whether specific or common, are identical in external reality. The logicians' claim that essential attributes are prior in conception is pure arbitrary judgment. External realities do not conform to our mental conventions; rather, our conceptions depend on reality. Moreover, people who do not follow the logicians do not have this mental prioritization. If it were innate, it would be universally recognized without traditional instruction. In reality, these concomitants may or may not occur to the mind, and their occurrence increases knowledge of the subject. The logicians' claim that some attributes are inside the essence and others outside has no basis in external reality or innate disposition. Finally, he notes that they claim the essential precedes the quiddity and is a constitutive part, but this is just a further assumption.

Key Terms
ذاتي (dhati)
لازم (lazim)
فصل (fasl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the main reason the logicians' claim that essential attributes are prior in conception is false?
A) It is based on a proof that only works for numbers, not for substances.
B) It is a pure arbitrary judgment that has no basis in external reality or innate disposition.
C) All attributes are equally prior, so no distinction is possible.
D) The logicians themselves admit it is a convention.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental attributes is an arbitrary convention, not a reflection of the nature of reality.

The Corruption of the Logicians' Distinction: Mutawatirat and Hissiyyat as Shared Proofs
Refutation of logicians on private vs shared knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological objection to the logicians' claim that mutawatirat and sensory perceptions are private proofs, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Mutawatirat and sensory perceptions are often shared, not private—so they can be universal proofs.

The logicians argued that propositions known through tawatur (widespread transmission) or sense are specific to those who have direct access and thus cannot be used as proofs against all opponents. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya begins his refutation by pointing out that this distinction is corrupt. He shows that many sensory perceptions and mutawatirat are actually shared by entire communities, making them valid proofs against anyone who has received the information. Understanding this critique is essential for evaluating the epistemological foundations of Islamic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Locate in the Arabic the statement that mutawatirat, mujarrabat, and hadsiyat are specific to those who know them and thus not proofs against others. Mark the phrase 'فلا تكون حجة على غيره'.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's central objection
Find where he declares this distinction corrupt ('بينا أن هذا تفريق فاسد'). Understand that this is the thesis he will defend.
3
Analyze the argument about sensory perceptions
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya divides sensory perceptions into private (e.g., what Zaid alone sees) and shared (e.g., seeing the sun). This shows that not all sensory knowledge is private.
He uses the example of shared sight of the sun, moon, stars, and local landmarks.
4
Apply the same logic to mutawatirat
Observe that he extends the same structure to mutawatirat: knowledge of Mecca, the sea, and the prophets is shared by all except isolated individuals. Therefore, mutawatirat can be universal proofs.
He mentions 'فإن هؤلاء قد تواتر خبرهم إلى عامة بني آدم'.
5
Evaluate the critique's strength
Reflect on whether Ibn Taymiyya's examples conclusively refute the logicians' claim. Consider his acknowledgment of fringe cases where reports haven't reached—does that weaken his argument?
This step assesses internal validity.

المقام الثالث - الناس ليسوا سواء في احتياجهم إلى «دليل»
الناس دون بعض، فهذا أمر بين فإن كثيراً من الناس تكون القضية عنده «حِسّية» أو «مُجرَّبة» أو «مبرهنة» أو «متواترة»، وغيره إنما عَرَفها بالنظر والاستدلال.
ولهذا كثير من الناس لا يحتاج في ثبوت المحمول» لـ «الموضوع» إلى «دليل» لنفسه، بل لغيره. ويبين ذلك لغيره بأدلة هو غني عنها، حتى يضرب له أمثالاً، ويقول له «أليس كذا؟ أليس كذا؟»، ويحتج عليه من الأدلة العقلية والسمعية بما يكون «حداً أوسط» عند المخاطب مما لا يحتاج إليه المستدِلُّ. بل قد يعلم الشيء بـ «الحسن» ويستدل على ثبوته لغيره بـ «الدليل».
وهذا أكبر من أن يحتاج إلى تمثيل فما أكثر من يرى الكواكب ويرى الهلال وغيره، فيقول «قد طلع الهلال». وتكون هذه القضية له حسية»، وقد تكون عند غيره مشكوكاً فيها، أو مظنونة أو خبرية، بل قد يظنها كذباً إذا صَدَّق المنجِّمَ الخارص القائل: «إنه لا يُرَى (۱) ) .
بطلان منع المنطقيين الاحتجاج
بـ «المتواترات» و «المجربات» و «الحَدْسِيَّات»
وقد ذَكَر مَن ذَكَر مِن هؤلاء المنطقيين أن القضايا المعلومة بـ «التواتر» و «التجربة» و «الحَدْس» يختص بها مَنْ علمها بهذا الطريق فلا تكون حجة على غيره ؛ بخلاف غيرها، فإنها مشتركة يُحتج بها على المنازع.
[۷۹] وقد بينا في غير هذا الموضع أن هذا تفريق فاسد.
فإن الحسيات) الظاهرة والباطنة تنقسم أيضاً إلى خاصة وعامة وليس ما رآه زيد أو شَمَّه أو ذاقه أو لَمَسه يجب اشتراك الناس فيه، وكذلك ما وجده في نفسه من جوعه وعطشه وألمه ولذته . لكن بعض الحسيات قد تكون مشتركة بين الناس، كاشتراكهم المحسوسات في رؤية الشمس والقمر والكواكب، وأخص من ذلك اشتراك أهل البلد الواحد في رؤية عندهم من جبل وجامع ونهر وغير ذلك من الأمور المخلوقة والمصنوعة.
وكذلك الأمور المعلومة بـ «التواتر» و «التجارب قد يشترك فيها عامة الناس، كاشتراك الناس في العلم بوجود مكة ونحوها من البلاد المشهورة ؛ واشتراكهم في وجود البحر - وأكثَرُهم ما رآه واشتراكهم في العلم بوجود موسى وعيسى ومحمد وادعائهم النبوة، ونحو ذلك. فإن هؤلاء قد تواتر خبرهم إلى عامة بني آدم، وإن قدر مَنْ لم يبلغه أخبارهم فهم في أطراف المعمورة لا في الوسط .
وكذلك «المجرّبات»، فعامة الناس قد جربوا أن شرب الماء يحصل معه الرّيُّ، و أن قطع العنق يحصل معه الموت، وأن الضرب الشديد يوجب الألم.
والعلم بهذه القضية الكُلّية «تجربي». فإن الحس» إنما يدرك رياً معيناً، وموت شخص معين. وألم شخص معين . أما كون كل من فُعِل به ذلك يحصل له مثل ذلك» / فهذه القضية الكلية لا تُعلم بـ «الحس»، [۸۰] بل بما يتركب من «الحس والعقل». وليس «الحس» هنا هو «السمع».
وهذا النوع قد يسميه بعض الناس كله «تجربيات»، وبعضهم يجعله نوعين: تجربیات» و «حدسيات». فإن كان الحس» المقرون بـ «العقل من فعل الإنسان، كأكله وشربه وتناوله الدواء، سماه «تجربياً»، وإن كان خارجاً عن قدرته، كتغير أشكال القمر عند مقابلة الشمس، سماه «حَدْسياً». والأول أشبه باللغة. فإن العرب تقول «رجل مجرب - بالفتح - لمن قد جربته الأمور وأحكمته، وإن كانت تلك من أنواع البلايا التي لا تكون باختياره.
وذلك أن «التجربة» تحصل بنظره و اعتباره و تدبره كحصول الأثر المعيَّن دائراً مع
The third station: People are not equal in their need for a proof.
People are beneath each other, for this is an obvious matter: many people have a proposition that is to them sensory, or experimental, or demonstrative, or mutawatir, while others only know it through reflection and inference.
For this reason, many people do not need a proof for the affirmation of the predicate for the subject for themselves, but for others. And he makes it clear to others with proofs that he himself is independent of, even to the point that he strikes for him examples and says to him 'Is it not so? Is it not so?', and argues against him from rational and revealed proofs as a middle term for the interlocutor, which the arguer does not need. Rather, he may know something through sense and infer its existence for others through proof.
This is too great to need exemplification: how many see the planets and see the crescent and others, and say 'The crescent has appeared'. This proposition is sensory for him, while it may be doubted by others, or presumed or reported, rather they may think it false if they believe the astrologer who predicts that it will not be seen.
The invalidity of the logicians' prohibition of arguing with mutawatirat, mujarrabat, and hadsiyat.
And some of these logicians have mentioned that propositions known through tawatur, experiment, and intuition are specific to those who know them in this way, so they are not a proof against others; unlike others, which are common and are used as proof against the opponent.
[79] And we have shown elsewhere that this distinction is corrupt.
For sensory perceptions, both apparent and inner, also divide into particular and general. It is not necessary that what Zaid saw, or smelled, or tasted, or touched be shared among people, nor what he finds within himself of hunger, thirst, pain, and pleasure. But some sensory perceptions can be shared among people, like their common perception of seeing the sun, moon, and stars; and more specific than that is the sharing of the people of one city in seeing what they have of mountains, mosques, rivers, and other created and manufactured things.
Similarly, matters known through tawatur and experiments can be shared among the general public, like people's shared knowledge of the existence of Mecca and similar famous cities; and their shared knowledge of the existence of the sea - and most of them have not seen it - and their shared knowledge of the existence of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad and their claims to prophethood, and so on. For the reports of these have reached the generality of mankind, and if there is someone whose reports have not reached him, they are on the fringes of the inhabited world, not in the center.
Similarly, experimental matters: the general public has experienced that drinking water brings about satiation, that cutting the neck brings about death, and that severe beating causes pain.
Knowledge of this universal proposition is experimental. For sense only perceives a particular satiation, a particular death, and a particular pain. As for that everyone who does that gets such and such, this universal proposition is not known through sense, [80] but through a combination of sense and intellect. And 'sense' here is not hearing.
This type some people call entirely 'experiential', and some make it two types: experimental and intuitive. If the sense combined with the intellect is from human action, like eating, drinking, and taking medicine, they call it experimental; if it is beyond his power, like the change of the moon's phases when facing the sun, they call it intuitive. And the first is more in line with the language. For the Arabs say 'a man mujarrab (with fatha) for one whom matters have tested and made experienced, even if those are types of trials that are not by his choice.
And that is because experience is obtained by his observation, consideration, and reflection, like obtaining a specific effect recurring with
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that certain types of propositions—specifically mutawatirat (widely transmitted reports), mujarrabat (experimental generalizations), and hadsiyat (intuitive judgments)—are not universal proofs that can be used against any opponent. He begins by stating the obvious fact that people are not equal in their need for proofs; some know things directly through sense, experiment, demonstration, or tawatur, while others need inference. He gives examples: one who sees the crescent moon knows it sensory, while another may doubt it. Then he identifies the logicians' position: that these types of knowledge are private to those who have them and cannot be used as proofs against others, unlike other propositions which are common. Ibn Taymiyya declares that he has already shown this distinction to be corrupt. He argues that sensory perceptions themselves are divided into particular and general; some sensations are private (e.g., what Zaid alone sees), but many are shared (e.g., seeing the sun). Similarly, mutawatirat like knowledge of Mecca, the sea, or the existence of the prophets are shared by all people except isolated individuals on the fringes of the world. Experimental knowledge also is common: everyone experiences that drinking water quenches thirst, cutting the neck causes death, etc. He clarifies that the universal experimental proposition is known through a combination of sense and intellect, not sense alone. He then explains a terminological division: some call all such knowledge 'experiential', while others distinguish 'experiential' (when the action is under one's control, like eating) from 'intuitive' (when the action is beyond human power, like lunar phases). He notes that the first usage is more in line with Arabic language. The passage is a sustained argument that the logicians' restriction is unfounded because these types of knowledge are in fact shared widely.

Key Terms
المتواترات
الحسيات
الدليل
المنطقيون
تفريق فاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, which of the following is true about mutawatirat (widely transmitted reports)?
A) They are known only by those who have directly experienced the event.
B) They are shared by the general public and can be used as proofs against opponents.
C) They are less certain than sensory perceptions.
D) They cannot be used as proofs because they are based on testimony.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim by showing that mutawatirat and many sensory perceptions are shared, thus they can serve as universal proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a mutawatir report from Islamic history (e.g., the existence of the Prophet Muhammad) and explain why it qualifies as a universal proof according to Ibn Taymiyya's argument.
?

Mujarrabat and Hadsiyat: Common Experimental Knowledge as Universal Proofs
Refutation on experimental and intuitive knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that mujarrabat (experimental generalizations) are universal proofs because they are based on shared human experience, and distinguish between mujarrabat and hadsiyat according to his terminological discussion.

Core Idea
Experimental knowledge is common to all humanity, making it a universal proof despite the logicians' objection.

The logicians claimed that experimental knowledge (mujarrabat) and intuitive knowledge (hadsiyat) are private to those who have conducted the experiments or had the intuitions. Ibn Taymiyya counters this by emphasizing that basic experimental facts—like drinking water quenches thirst—are known to everyone through repeated experience. He also clarifies the relationship between mujarrabat and hadsiyat, noting a terminological division. Understanding this argument is crucial for recognizing the epistemological role of everyday experience in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the logicians' claim about mujarrabat and hadsiyat
In the Arabic, find the line 'يختص بها مَنْ علمها بهذا الطريق فلا تكون حجة على غيره'. This is the claim being refuted.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counterexample for mujarrabat
Identify the examples he gives: drinking water brings satiation, cutting the neck causes death, severe beating causes pain. Note that he says 'عامة الناس قد جربوا' (the general public has experienced).
3
Understand the structure of experimental knowledge
Notice his clarification: sense alone gives particular instances, but the universal proposition ('everyone who does that gets such and such') is formed by combining sense and intellect. This shows experimental knowledge is not purely private sensation.
He says 'هذه القضية الكلية لا تُعلم بـ الحس بل بما يتركب من الحس والعقل'.
4
Analyze the terminological division between mujarrabat and hadsiyat
Find where he mentions that some call all such knowledge 'experiential', while others divide it into experimental (actions under human control) and intuitive (actions beyond human control, like lunar phases).
He prefers the broader usage in line with Arabic language: 'رجل مجرب'.
5
Evaluate the force of this part of the critique
Consider whether showing that experimental knowledge is common suffices to refute the logicians. Does the existence of shared experiences like drinking water undermine their claim that mujarrabat are private? Why or why not?
This step assesses the validity of Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.

Similarly, matters known through tawatur and experiments can be shared among the general public, like people's shared knowledge of the existence of Mecca and similar famous cities; and their shared knowledge of the existence of the sea - and most of them have not seen it - and their shared knowledge of the existence of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad and their claims to prophethood, and so on. For the reports of these have reached the generality of mankind, and if there is someone whose reports have not reached him, they are on the fringes of the inhabited world, not in the center.
Similarly, experimental matters: the general public has experienced that drinking water brings about satiation, that cutting the neck brings about death, and that severe beating causes pain.
Knowledge of this universal proposition is experimental. For sense only perceives a particular satiation, a particular death, and a particular pain. As for that everyone who does that gets such and such, this universal proposition is not known through sense, [80] but through a combination of sense and intellect. And 'sense' here is not hearing.
This type some people call entirely 'experiential', and some make it two types: experimental and intuitive. If the sense combined with the intellect is from human action, like eating, drinking, and taking medicine, they call it experimental; if it is beyond his power, like the change of the moon's phases when facing the sun, they call it intuitive. And the first is more in line with the language. For the Arabs say 'a man mujarrab (with fatha) for one whom matters have tested and made experienced, even if those are types of trials that are not by his choice.
And that is because experience is obtained by his observation, consideration, and reflection, like obtaining a specific effect recurring with
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that certain types of propositions—specifically mutawatirat (widely transmitted reports), mujarrabat (experimental generalizations), and hadsiyat (intuitive judgments)—are not universal proofs that can be used against any opponent. He begins by stating the obvious fact that people are not equal in their need for proofs; some know things directly through sense, experiment, demonstration, or tawatur, while others need inference. He gives examples: one who sees the crescent moon knows it sensory, while another may doubt it. Then he identifies the logicians' position: that these types of knowledge are private to those who have them and cannot be used as proofs against others, unlike other propositions which are common. Ibn Taymiyya declares that he has already shown this distinction to be corrupt. He argues that sensory perceptions themselves are divided into particular and general; some sensations are private (e.g., what Zaid alone sees), but many are shared (e.g., seeing the sun). Similarly, mutawatirat like knowledge of Mecca, the sea, or the existence of the prophets are shared by all people except isolated individuals on the fringes of the world. Experimental knowledge also is common: everyone experiences that drinking water quenches thirst, cutting the neck causes death, etc. He clarifies that the universal experimental proposition is known through a combination of sense and intellect, not sense alone. He then explains a terminological division: some call all such knowledge 'experiential', while others distinguish 'experiential' (when the action is under one's control, like eating) from 'intuitive' (when the action is beyond human power, like lunar phases). He notes that the first usage is more in line with Arabic language. The passage is a sustained argument that the logicians' restriction is unfounded because these types of knowledge are in fact shared widely.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that experimental knowledge is universal because it is based on common human experiences of cause and effect, refuting the logicians' claim of privacy.

The Dispute Over the Meaning of Qiyas
Author reports the terminological dispute about whether qiyas applies to analogical or categorical syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions in the dispute over the meaning of 'qiyas' and explain why Ibn Taymiyya endorses the majority view that it is literal for both analogical and categorical reasoning.

Core Idea
Qiyas includes both analogical and categorical reasoning; restricting it to one type distorts Islamic logic.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports a key terminological dispute among scholars: does the word 'qiyas' refer literally to analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), to categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul), or to both? The answer determines how Islamic reasoning is classified and taught. Ibn Taymiyya endorses the majority view that it is literal for both, arguing that 'rational qiyas' (al-qiyas al-'aqli) encompasses all valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term Under Dispute
The word 'qiyas' is the subject of disagreement. Note that the dispute is about its literal (haqiqa) versus figurative (majaz) usage, not about its logical validity.
2
List the Three Positions
First: some usul scholars (like al-Ghazali) say it is literal for analogical, figurative for categorical. Second: Ibn Hazm says the opposite. Third: the majority, including many Hanbali and Shafi'i jurists, say it is literal for both.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's Endorsement
He explicitly calls the majority view 'correct' (al-sawab) and supports it with the concept of 'rational qiyas' that covers both types. This reflects his broader project of unifying Islamic reasoning.
4
Recognize the Implications
By accepting both meanings as literal, Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Islamic intellectual tradition does not need to choose between fiqh-based analogy and Greek-style syllogism; both are forms of the same rational process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۳) هو أبو الفتح محمد بن علي بن محمد الحلواني الحنبلي . تفقه على القاضي أبي علي يعقوب وب تلميذ القاضي أبي يعلى . توفي سنة ٥٠٥ هـ ـ «طبقات الحنابلة» .
People have disputed concerning the meaning of 'qiyas'. And they have disputed concerning the meaning of 'qiyas'. So a group of scholars of usul said: It is used literally for 'analogical syllogism' and metaphorically for 'categorical syllogism' – like Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and Abu Muhammad al-Maqdisi, and others. And another group said: Rather the opposite – it is literal for 'categorical syllogism' and metaphorical for 'analogical syllogism' – like Ibn Hazm and others. And the majority of scholars said: Rather it is literal for both, and 'rational qiyas' encompasses both. This is the statement of most of those who spoke on usul al-din and usul al-fiqh and types of rational sciences, and it is correct. And it is the statement of the majority of followers of the four imams and others, like al-Shaykh Abu Hamid [al-Isfara'ini], and al-Qadi Abu al-Tayyib, and their likes; and like al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, and al-Qadi Ya'qub, and al-Hulwani, and Abu al-Khattab, and Ibn 'Aqil, and Ibn [the footnote continues]
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a critical report by Ibn Taymiyya of the terminological dispute among scholars concerning the word 'qiyas'. He presents three positions. The first, held by usul scholars like al-Ghazali and Ibn Qudama (al-Maqdisi), is that 'qiyas' is literally (haqiqa) applied to analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil) and metaphorically (majaz) to categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). The second, held by Ibn Hazm and others, reverses this: literal for categorical, metaphorical for analogical. The third, which Ibn Taymiyya endorses, is that of the majority (jumhur) of scholars: 'qiyas' is literal for both, and 'rational qiyas' (al-qiyas al-'aqli) encompasses both types. This majority includes many Hanbali, Shafi'i, and other scholars. Ibn Taymiyya considers this correct (al-sawab). The dispute is essentially about whether the term qiyas originally denoted the analogical reasoning used in fiqh or the Aristotelian categorical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya's position reflects his broader argument that both forms are inherently the same logical operation. For example, the unanimous agreement that wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating can be expressed as an analogical argument (wine is like nabidh in intoxication) or as a categorical syllogism (every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant).

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya affirms that 'qiyas' is literally used for both analogical and categorical reasoning, reflecting the unity of rational inference in Islam.

The Universal Reality of Proof
Ibn Taymiyya asserts the essence of proof is the same in all forms of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' emphasis on formal structure and explain why he holds that the essence of proof is necessary entailment.

Core Idea
Proof is defined by necessary entailment, not by syllogistic form.

Ibn Taymiyya here challenges the widespread assumption among logicians that the validity of an argument depends on its being cast in a specific formal mold – categorical or analogical. He argues that all proofs, regardless of their verbal or mental representation, share a single essence: the necessary connection between evidence and conclusion. This principle undercuts the entire formalist project of defining logic as the study of syllogistic figures and moods. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and see how it applies to Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the passage and pinpoint where Ibn Taymiyya states that the form of proof is irrelevant. He says: 'وَبِأَىِّ صُورَةٍ ذِهْنِيَّةٍ أَوْ لَفْظِيَّةٍ صُوِّرَ الدَّلِيلُ فَحَقِيقَتُهُ وَاحِدَةٌ'. What is he claiming about the essence of proof?
2
Extract the definition of proof
Ibn Taymiyya defines proof by its function: 'كَوْنَهُ مُسْتَلْزِمًا لِلْحُكْمِ لَازِمًا لِلْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ'. Identify the two conditions: that the proof entails the ruling and that it is necessary for the subject. This is the core of his definition.
3
Contrast with logicians' view
Logicians distinguish between categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) and analogy (qiyas tamthil) as different forms. Ibn Taymiyya asserts they share the same reality. How does his view differ from the logicians' emphasis on formal structure?
4
Grasp the epistemological point
He says the heart (al-qalb) grasps the necessity without verbal expression. This implies that reasoning is natural and universal. Why does he emphasize that people deduce proofs even without using technical terminology?
5
Trace the regress argument
The last sentence: 'فَالْعِلْمُ بِذَلِكَ الْمَلْزُومِ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيِّنًا بِنَفْسِهِ أَوْ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ.' This shows that the necessary connection must be either self-evident or proven by another proof. How does this prevent infinite regress?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كالسكر الذي هو أعم من النبيذ المتنازع فيه وأخص من التحريم. وقد يكون الدليل مساويا في العموم والخصوص للحكم ولمحله. وبأي صورة ذهنية أو لفظية صور الدليل فحقيقته واحدة. وإنما يعتبر في كونه دليلا هو كونه مستلزما للحكم لازما للمحكوم عليه. فهذا هو جهة دلالته، سواء صور قياس شمول وتمثيل أو لم يصور كذلك. وهذا أمر يعقله القلب وإن لم يعبر عنه اللسان. ولهذا كانت أذهان بني آدم تستدل بالأدلة على المدلولات وإن لم يعبروا عن ذلك بالعبارات المبينة لما في نفوسهم. وقد يعبرون بعبارات مبينة لمعانيهم وإن لم يسلكوا اصطلاح طائفة معينة من أهل الكلام ولا المنطق ولا غيرهم. فالعلم بذلك الملزوم لا بد أن يكون بينا بنفسه أو بدليل آخر.
And this is like 'intoxicant' which is more general than the disputed nabidh and more specific than 'prohibition'. And the proof may be equal in generality and specificity to the ruling and to its subject. And in whatever mental or verbal form the proof is depicted, its reality is one. And what is considered in its being a proof is its being entailing the ruling and necessary for that which is ruled upon. So this is the aspect of its indication, whether it is depicted as a categorical syllogism or an analogy or not depicted as such. And this is a matter grasped by the heart even if not expressed by the tongue. Therefore, the minds of human beings deduce proofs for what is proven, even if they do not express that with statements clarifying what is in their souls. And they may express with clear statements of their meanings even if they do not follow the terminology of a specific group of theologians, logicians, or others. So knowledge of that necessary thing must be self-evident or by another proof.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage establishes Ibn Taymiyya's core epistemological principle: the logical form of a proof (whether categorical or analogical) is irrelevant to its essence. He uses an example from fiqh: 'intoxicant' is a genus relative to the specific drink (nabidh) but a species relative to 'prohibition'. Similarly, a proof may be more general, equal, or more specific relative to the ruling and its subject. What matters is the necessary connection (istiqlal) — that the proof entails the ruling and is necessary for the subject. This necessity is grasped by the heart (al-qalb) directly, even without verbal articulation. Thus, people naturally reason using proofs intuitively, without needing the formal terminology of any particular group like the theologians or logicians. The knowledge of this necessary connection must be either self-evident or established by another proof, leading to a regress that terminates in self-evident truths.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Metaphysics Cannot Know the Creator
Author claims metaphysics does not know Creator or creation due to its universal subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that metaphysics, by having absolute existence as its subject, cannot provide knowledge of the particular Creator.

Core Idea
Metaphysics studies a universal; the Creator is a particular.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against the philosophers' claim that metaphysics (al-'ilm al-ilahi) gives knowledge of the Creator and creation. He points out that its subject is absolute universal existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq al-kulli), which is a universal abstract concept. Since universals cannot capture the particularity of individual existents, metaphysics fails to deliver knowledge of the specific Creator or any particular created being. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
The philosophers claim that metaphysics (first philosophy) studies the highest causes, including the Creator. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this by examining the subject matter of that science.
2
State the subject of metaphysics
According to the passage, the subject of metaphysics is absolute universal existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq al-kulli), which is divided into categories like necessary/possible, eternal/originated, substance/accident.
3
Recognize the universal nature
This subject is a universal abstraction, not a concrete particular. It covers all existents as a genus but does not specify any individual.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the subject is a universal, the science can only discuss universal properties and cannot deliver knowledge of the particular Creator or any specific created being. Hence it fails its claimed purpose.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : ليس «العلم الإلهي» عندهم علماً بالخالق ولا بالمخلوق. الثالث أن يقال : العلم الأعلى عندهم، الذي هو الفلسفة الأولى والحكمة العليا، علم ما بعد الطبيعة باعتبار الاستدلال ـ وما هو «قبلها باعتبار «الوجود» ـ وهو الذي يسميه طائفة منهم «العلم الإلهي» ـ وموضوع هذا العلم هو الوجود المُطْلَقُ الكلي المنقسم إلى «واجب» و «ممکن»؛ و «قدیم» و «محدث»؛ و «جوهر» و «عَرَض». وقد أورد بعض المتأخرين من الشيعة المصنفين في علمهم ما ذكر أنه «الأسرار الخفيّة في العلوم العقلية عليهم سؤالاً . قال : إن كان موضوعه «كل موجود» فلا يبحث فيه عن عوارض كل موجود، وإن كان أخص من ذلك كـ «الواجب» و «الممكن» فذلك جزء منه . فيقال له : القسمة نوعان : قسمة الكلي إلى جزئياته، وقسمة «الكل» إلى أجزائه. والقسمة الثانية هي المعروفة في الأمر العام كما يقول العلماء «باب القسمة ويذكرون قسمة المواريث والمغانم والأرض، وغير ذلك. ومنه قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَنَبِّتْهُمْ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسْمَةٌ بَيْنَهُمْ كُلُّ شِرْبٍ تُحضر ﴾ [سورة القمر : ٢٨]. ومنه قوله تعالى :
The third aspect: 'Al-Ilahiyya' according to them is not knowledge of the Creator nor of creation. The third is to say: The highest knowledge according to them, which is first philosophy and the highest wisdom, is the science of metaphysics in terms of inference—and it is prior to it in terms of existence—and it is what a group of them call 'divine science'—and the subject of this science is absolute, universal existence divided into necessary and possible, eternal and originated, substance and accident. And one of the later Shi'a authors in their science has raised what he mentioned as 'the hidden secrets in the intellectual sciences' as a question. And from it is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them; each drink is attended' [Surah al-Qamar: 28]. And from it is the saying of Allah Almighty:

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the claim that metaphysics (al-'ilm al-ilahi) provides knowledge of the Creator or creation. Ibn Taymiyya states that its subject is absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq al-kulli), which is a universal divided into categories like necessary/possible, eternal/originated, substance/accident. Since it deals with universals, it cannot yield knowledge of particular existents. Then an objection from a Shi'a scholar is presented: if the subject is 'every existent,' then the science cannot investigate the accidents of every existent individually; if it is a subset like necessary/possible, then that is just a part of existence. The response: division is of two types—division of the universal into its particulars (like dividing 'man' into Zayd, 'Amr) and division of the whole into its parts (like dividing a house into rooms). The second type is the one used in general matters (like division of inheritance), and it allows that the subject 'absolute existence' is a whole whose parts are the categories; thus the science studies the accidents of that whole, not of each individual. The example from the Qur'an about water division illustrates the second type. The response defends the coherence of the subject matter, though Ibn Taymiyya's overall point remains critical.

The Two Types of Division in Ibn Taymiyya's Response
Author distinguishes two types of division to respond to an objection about metaphysics' subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between division of a universal into particulars and division of a whole into parts, and apply this distinction to understand the defense of a science's subject matter.

Core Idea
Division can be of a universal into particulars or of a whole into parts; the latter allows a science to study its subject as a whole.

After stating his critique, Ibn Taymiyya presents an objection from a Shi'a author who questions how metaphysics can have 'absolute existence' as its subject if it does not study the accidents of every existent or if it limits itself to necessary and possible. Ibn Taymiyya's response introduces a crucial logical distinction: division (qisma) can be either division of a universal into its particulars or division of a whole into its parts. This lesson unpacks that distinction and how it resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The Shi'a author says: if the subject is 'every existent', then metaphysics does not study the accidents of each existent; if it is more specific like necessary/possible, then it is only a part, not the whole subject.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's classification of division
He says division is of two types: division of a universal into particulars (e.g., dividing 'man' into Zayd, 'Amr) and division of a whole into parts (e.g., dividing a house into roof, walls).
3
Apply the second type to the subject
Absolute existence is a whole (kull) that can be divided into its parts: necessary/possible, eternal/originated, etc. The science studies the properties of this whole, not each individual part separately.
4
Confirm the resolution
Thus the objection fails: metaphysics does not need to examine each existent individually; it studies the universal properties of existence as a whole, and its division into categories is a division of a whole into its parts.

الوجه الثالث : ليس «العلم الإلهي» عندهم علماً بالخالق ولا بالمخلوق. الثالث أن يقال : العلم الأعلى عندهم، الذي هو الفلسفة الأولى والحكمة العليا، علم ما بعد الطبيعة باعتبار الاستدلال ـ وما هو «قبلها باعتبار «الوجود» ـ وهو الذي يسميه طائفة منهم «العلم الإلهي» ـ وموضوع هذا العلم هو الوجود المُطْلَقُ الكلي المنقسم إلى «واجب» و «ممکن»؛ و «قدیم» و «محدث»؛ و «جوهر» و «عَرَض». وقد أورد بعض المتأخرين من الشيعة المصنفين في علمهم ما ذكر أنه «الأسرار الخفيّة في العلوم العقلية عليهم سؤالاً . قال : إن كان موضوعه «كل موجود» فلا يبحث فيه عن عوارض كل موجود، وإن كان أخص من ذلك كـ «الواجب» و «الممكن» فذلك جزء منه . فيقال له : القسمة نوعان : قسمة الكلي إلى جزئياته، وقسمة «الكل» إلى أجزائه. والقسمة الثانية هي المعروفة في الأمر العام كما يقول العلماء «باب القسمة ويذكرون قسمة المواريث والمغانم والأرض، وغير ذلك. ومنه قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَنَبِّتْهُمْ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسْمَةٌ بَيْنَهُمْ كُلُّ شِرْبٍ تُحضر ﴾ [سورة القمر : ٢٨]. ومنه قوله تعالى :
The third aspect: 'Al-Ilahiyya' according to them is not knowledge of the Creator nor of creation. The third is to say: The highest knowledge according to them, which is first philosophy and the highest wisdom, is the science of metaphysics in terms of inference—and it is prior to it in terms of existence—and it is what a group of them call 'divine science'—and the subject of this science is absolute, universal existence divided into necessary and possible, eternal and originated, substance and accident. And one of the later Shi'a authors in their science has raised what he mentioned as 'the hidden secrets in the intellectual sciences' as a question. He said: If its subject is 'every existent' then it does not investigate the accidents of every existent, and if it is more specific like 'the necessary' and 'the possible' then that is a part of it. So it is said to him: Division is of two types: division of the universal into its particulars, and division of the whole into its parts. The second division is what is known in general matters as the scholars say 'the chapter of division' and they mention the division of inheritances, spoils, land, and other things. And from it is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them; each drink is attended' [Surah al-Qamar: 28]. And from it is the saying of Allah Almighty:
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the claim that metaphysics (al-'ilm al-ilahi) provides knowledge of the Creator or creation. Ibn Taymiyya states that its subject is absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq al-kulli), which is a universal divided into categories like necessary/possible, eternal/originated, substance/accident. Since it deals with universals, it cannot yield knowledge of particular existents. Then an objection from a Shi'a scholar is presented: if the subject is 'every existent,' then the science cannot investigate the accidents of every existent individually; if it is a subset like necessary/possible, then that is just a part of existence. The response: division is of two types—division of the universal into its particulars (like dividing 'man' into Zayd, 'Amr) and division of the whole into its parts (like dividing a house into rooms). The second type is the one used in general matters (like division of inheritance), and it allows that the subject 'absolute existence' is a whole whose parts are the categories; thus the science studies the accidents of that whole, not of each individual. The example from the Qur'an about water division illustrates the second type. The response defends the coherence of the subject matter, though Ibn Taymiyya's overall point remains critical.

قسمة
قسمة الكلي إلى جزئياته
قسمة الكل إلى أجزائه

B) Dividing a house into roof and walls
C) Dividing animals into rational and irrational
D) Dividing existents into necessary and possible

Key Takeaway
The logical distinction between dividing a universal into particulars and dividing a whole into parts resolves the objection about the scope of metaphysics' subject matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Original Arabic Meanings of 'Kalima' and 'Harf'
Author defines 'kalima' and 'harf' according to Arabic usage, critiquing grammarians' convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the original Arabic meanings of 'kalima' and 'harf' from later grammatical conventions and cite evidence from the prophetic hadith and early authorities.

Core Idea
Kalima originally meant speech, not a word; harf meant a letter, not a particle.

When you open a grammar book, you find 'kalima' divided into noun, verb, and particle. But is this the original Arabic understanding? In this passage, the author argues that this division is a later convention of grammarians, not the language of the Arabs. He takes us back to the time of the Prophet and early scholars to show what 'kalima' and 'harf' really meant. Understanding this shift is essential for critically evaluating arguments that rely on these terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
What is the author arguing about the terms 'kalima' and 'harf'? He says the grammarians' technical usage is not original Arabic but a mere convention.
2
Examine the prophetic hadith
Look at the hadith: 'Whoever recites the Quran, for every harf he gets ten good deeds... I do not say that 'alif lam meem' is a harf, but 'alif' is a harf, 'lam' is a harf, 'meem' is a harf.' How does the author use this to prove that 'harf' means a single letter?
3
Analyze the example of al-Khalil
When al-Khalil asked about pronouncing 'al-zā' from 'Zayd', his students said 'zā' (a noun), and he corrected them that the harf is 'za'. What does this show about the distinction between the name of a letter and the letter itself?
4
Contrast with Sibawayh's division
Sibawayh divided words into noun, verb, and particle. How does the author reconcile this? He notes that Sibawayh distinguished the particle as 'came for a meaning', differentiating it from letters of the alphabet such as 'alif', 'ba', 'ta', which are nouns when isolated.
5
Connect to the teaching of children
The author says children are taught the names of individual letters first, then the combined alphabet, then numbers. How does this support the original meaning that 'harf' refers to a single letter?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الثالث : معنى «الكلمة» و«الحرف» عند العرب كان «قولاً». وأما تسمية الاسم وحده «كلمة» ، والفعل وحده «كلمة»، والحرف وحده «كلمة»، لا يصح تسمية «كلمات» مثل «هل» و «بل»، فهذا اصطلاح محض لبعض النحاة، ليس هذا من لغة العرب أصلاً . المفردات وإنما تسمى العرب هذه المفردات حروفاً». ومنه قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : إنما هي من قرأ القرآن فله بكل (حرف) عشر حسنات. أما إني لا أقول (الم) حرف، ولكن حروف) (ألف) حرف، و (لام) حرف و (ميم) حرف. والذي عليه محققوا العلماء أن المراد بـ «الحرف الاسم ،وحده ،والفعل وحرف المعنى، لقوله «ألف حرف»، وهذا اسم. ولهذا لما سأل الخليل أصحابه عن النطق بـ «الزاء» من «زید»، فقالوا: «زا»، فقال : نطقتم بـ «الاسم»، وإنما الحرف» «زَة». ومنه قول أبي الأسود الدولي ، وذكر له لفظة من الغريب وقال : هذا (حرف) لم يبلغك» ؛ فقال: «كل (حرف) لم يبلغ عَمَّك فافعل به كذا . ولهذا ذكر سيبويه في أول كتابه التقسيم إلى «اسم» و «فعل» و «حرف جاء لمعنى ليس باسم ولا فعل». فجعل الفصلَ من النوع الثالث أنه حرف جاء لمعنى وليس باسم ولا فعل، فميزه بقوله جاء لمعنى عن حروف الهجاء ، مثل «ألف» «با» «تا»، فإن هذه حروف هجاء . وهذه الألفاظ أسماء تُعرب إذا عُقدت ،ورُكبت، ولكن إذا نطق بها قبل التركيب نطق بها ساكنة، كما يُنْطَق بأسماء العدد قبل التركيب والعقد، فيقال واحد، اثنان، ثلاثة . ولهذا يعلَّم الصبيان في أول الأمر أسماء الحروف المفردة: ا، ب، ت، ث؛ ثم المركبة، وهو أبجد هوز، حُطّي، ويعلمون أسماء الأعداد: واحد، اثنان، ثلاثة .
Third station - third aspect: The meaning of 'kalima' and 'harf' among the Arabs was 'speech'. As for calling the noun alone 'kalima', the verb alone 'kalima', and the particle alone 'kalima', it is not valid to call (words like) 'hal' and 'bal' 'kalimat' — this is a mere convention of some grammarians, not from the Arabic language originally. Rather, the Arabs call these individual items 'huruf'. Among this is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever recites the Quran, for every harf he gets ten good deeds. But I do not say that 'alif lam meem' is a harf, rather (they are) letters: 'alif' is a harf, 'lam' is a harf, 'meem' is a harf.' And the consensus of verifying scholars is that what is meant by 'harf' is the noun alone, the verb, and the meaning-based particle, because of his saying 'alif is a harf', and this is a noun. Hence when al-Khalil asked his students about the pronunciation of 'al-zā' from 'Zayd', they said 'zā', and he said: 'You pronounced the noun; the harf is 'za'.' Among this is the saying of Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali: he was mentioned a strange word and said 'This is a harf that has not reached you'; he replied: 'Every harf that has not reached your uncle, do such-and-such with it.' Hence Sibawayh mentioned at the beginning of his book the division into 'noun', 'verb', and 'particle that comes for a meaning not a noun nor a verb'. So he distinguished the third type as a particle that comes for a meaning and is not a noun nor a verb, distinguishing it by his saying 'comes for a meaning' from the letters of the alphabet, such as 'alif', 'bā', 'tā', for these are letters of the alphabet. And these words are nouns that are inflected when combined and compounded, but if pronounced before compounding they are pronounced vowelless, just as the names of numbers are pronounced before compounding and combining: one says 'wāḥid, ithnān, thalātha'. Hence children are taught first the names of the individual letters: a, b, t, th; then the combined ones, which is 'abjad hawaz, ḥuṭṭī'; and they are taught the names of the numbers: wāḥid, ithnān, thalātha.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the grammarians' convention of using the terms 'kalima' and 'harf' in a technical sense. The author argues that originally in Arabic, 'kalima' means 'speech' (qawl), not the grammatical categories of noun, verb, and particle. He cites the Prophet's hadith where 'harf' means a letter of the alphabet, and the examples of al-Khalil and Abu al-Aswad to show that 'harf' refers to a single letter, not a grammatical particle. The author then notes that Sibawayh's division into noun, verb, and particle is a later convention, and that the letters of the alphabet (like alif, ba, ta) are actually nouns when isolated but are pronounced as single letters. The passage concludes by relating this to how children are taught: they first learn the names of the letters as individual 'harf', then the combined alphabet, and similarly the names of numbers. The key point is that the technical terminology of the grammarian is not the original Arabic usage, and thus arguments based on that convention may be flawed.

Key Terms
كلمة
حرف
اسم

Key Takeaway
The original Arabic meaning of 'harf' is a single letter of the alphabet, not a grammatical particle; grammarians later shifted the term.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Existence as Subject
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that existence is the subject of divine science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' highest science is not about external reality but only about mental universals.

Core Idea
Universal existence is only in the mind, so knowledge of it is not knowledge of God or creatures.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that existence (al-wujud) is the subject of divine science. He argues that universal existence is a mental construct, not a real entity. If their highest science is about such a universal, it cannot be a science of external reality—neither of the Creator nor of creation. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians claim that existence is the subject of divine science. A potential objector might divide existence into all existents or part of it (Necessary/Possible). Ibn Taymiyya notes that the correct subject is universal existence divided into types, not a whole divided into parts.
2
Recognize the logicians' correction
The logicians themselves correct the objection, saying their subject is universal existence (wujud kulli) and its essential accidents. But Ibn Taymiyya then attacks this very notion.
This sets up his critique of the universal.
3
Follow the critique: universal existence only in the mind
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal existence is universal only in the mind, not in external reality. Therefore, a science based on it is about mental concepts, not real entities.
He lists other mental universals: 'thing', 'essence', 'reality', etc.
4
Assess the consequence
If their highest science is about such mental universals, it is not knowledge of any external existent—neither the Creator nor creatures. It is epistemically inferior.
5
Contrast with Islamic science
In contrast, the highest knowledge in Islam is knowledge of Allah (ma'rifat Allah) and His attributes, which is knowledge of external realities, not mere mental abstractions.
This is implied by Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قال القائل : «الوجود» الذي هو موضوع العلم الإلهي عندهم إما أن يكون كل موجود أو بعضه، وهو الواجب أو الممكن، كان هذا الحصر خطأ منه، لأن موضوعه الوجود الكلي المنقسم إلى أنواعه، لا الكل المنقسم إلى أجزائه . ومعلوم أن الوجود الكلي يتكلمون في لواحقه الذاتية لا في لواحق كل موجود . لكن الذي تبين به خساسة ما عند القوم ونقص قدره أن هذا الوجود الكلي إنما يكون كلياً في الذهن لا في الخارج . فإذا كان هذا هو العلم الأعلى عندهم لم يكن العلَم الأعلى عندهم علماً بشيء موجود في الخارج، بل علماً بأمر مشترك بين جميع الموجودات، وهو مسمى «الوجود»، وذلك كمسمى «الشيء»، و «الذات»، و «الحقيقة»، و «النفس»، و «العين»، و «الماهية»، ونحو ذلك من المعاني العامة . ومعلوم أن العلم بهذا ليس هو علماً بموجود في الخارج، لا بالخالق ولا بالمخلوق؛ وإنما هو علم بأمر مشترك كلي تشترك فيه الموجودات، لا يوجد إلا في الذهن. ومن المتصورات ما يشترك فيه الموجود والمعدوم، كقولنا «مذكور» و «معلوم» و «مخبر عنه» . فهذا أعم من ذاك .
If someone says: 'Existence, which is the subject of divine science according to them, is either all existent or part of it, and it is the Necessary or the Possible,' this restriction is mistaken, because its subject is universal existence divided into its types, not the whole divided into its parts. And it is known that they speak of the accidental properties of universal existence, not of the accidental properties of every existent. But what reveals the baseness of what these people have and the deficiency of its value is that this universal existence is universal only in the mind, not in external reality. So if this is their highest science, then their highest science is not a science of something existing externally, but rather a science of a universal commonality among all existents, which is called 'existence', and similarly the name 'thing', 'essence', 'reality', 'self', 'eye', 'quiddity', and similar general meanings. And it is known that knowledge of this is not knowledge of an external existent, neither of the Creator nor of the creature; rather it is knowledge of a universal commonality shared by existents, which does not exist except in the mind. And among the conceptualized things are those shared by existent and non-existent, like our saying 'mentioned', 'known', 'informed about'. This is more general than that.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that existence (al-wujud) is the subject of divine science (al-'ilm al-ilahi). Ibn Taymiyya begins by presenting a potential objection: someone might say existence is either all existents (totality) or a part (the Necessary or Possible). He responds that this division is wrong because the subject is a universal concept (wujud kulli) divided into species (anwa'), not a physical whole divided into parts. He then argues that such universal existence is purely mental, not external. Hence, the logicians' 'highest science' is not about anything real—neither God nor creatures—but about abstract mental commonalities like 'thing', 'essence', etc. He even notes that some concepts like 'mentioned' include both existent and non-existent, making them even more general. Therefore, their science is epistemically inferior.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd)
علم إلهي (ʿilm ilāhī)
كلي (kullī)
لواحق ذاتية (lawāḥiq dhātiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because existence is divided incorrectly

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's key point: universal existence is a mental construct, so a science based on it is not knowledge of real existents, including God.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logicians' Highest Science
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the logicians' highest science with the Islamic highest science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the logicians' and Muslims' concepts of the highest science and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' concept is epistemologically empty.

Core Idea
The highest science for Muslims is knowledge of Allah; for logicians it is a mental abstraction with no real foundation.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly contrasts what logicians consider the highest science — knowledge of 'existence' as a universal — with what Muslims consider the highest science — knowledge of Allah. He argues that the logicians' concept is nothing but a mental construct with no external reality, while knowledge of Allah is the foundation of all sciences and leads to perfection. This lesson will help you dissect his critique and understand its implications for the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing conceptions
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya defines the highest science for Muslims (knowledge of Allah) and for logicians (knowledge of existence as a universal). Notice how he contrasts their status and effects.
2
Extract the core objection
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's main argument: the logicians' concept has no external reality, is a mere commonality, does not entail knowledge of particulars, and does not perfect the soul.
3
Analyze the attack on the categories
Examine how Ibn Taymiyya dismisses the division of existence into substance and accident, and the five substances (body, matter, form, soul, intellect). He argues only body is externally real; the rest are mental constructs.
Note his appeal to Ibn Hazm as a witness.
4
Assess the strength of the critique
Evaluate whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique is logically sound. Consider his epistemological assumptions (nominalism vs realism) and whether his alternative (knowledge of Allah) adequately replaces the logicians' framework.

المقام الثالث - الوجه الثالث : العلم الأعلى عند المنطقيين وعند المسلمين وهذا بخلاف العلم الأعلى عند المسلمين فإنه العلم بالله الذي هو في نفسه العلم الأعلى عند المسلمين أعلى من غيره من كل وجه والعلم به أعلى العلوم من كل وجه، والعلم به أصل لكل علم وهم يسلمون أن العلم به إذا حصل على الوجه التام يستلزم العلم بكل موجود. وهذا بخلاف العلم بمسمى «الوجود». فإن هذا لا حقيقة له في الخارج؛ ولا العلم بالقدر المشترك يستلزم العلم بأجناسه وأنواعه وما يتميز به كل شيء؛ بل ليس فيه إلا علم بقدر مشترك لا تصور له في الخارج؛ وإنما هو علم بهذه المشتركات. وليس في مجرد العلم بذلك ما يوجب كمال النفس، بل ولا في العلم بأقسامه العامة. فإنا إذا علمنا أن «الوجود» ينقسم إلى «جوهر» و «عرض»، وأن أقسام «الجوهر» خمسة كما زعموه (1). مع أن ذلك ليس بصحيح، ولا يثبت مما ذكروه إلا «الجسم». وأما «المادة»، و «الصورة»، و «النفس»، و «العقل»، فلا يثبت لها حقيقة في الخارج، إلا أن يكون جسماً أو عرضاً. ولكن ما يثبتونه يعود إلى أمر مقدر في النفس لا في الخارج، كما قد بسط في موضعه. وقد اعترف بذلك من ينصرهم ويعظمهم كأبي محمد بن حزم (2) وغيره. تعظيم ابن حزم الأندلسي لأهل المنطق، ولتعظيمه المنطق رواه بإسناده إلى متى الترجمان الذي ترجمه إلى العربية. ومع هذا فاعترف بما ذكرناه وقد بسط ذلك في موضعه. ونحن نفرض هنا وجود ذلك في الخارج. فالعلم بانقسام ذلك إلى «جواهر خمسة»، وانقسام «العرض» إلى الأنواع التسعة (4). مع أنه لم يقم دليل على انقسامه إلى تسعة عند بعضهم. وقد أنشدوا فيها:
Third Station - Third Aspect: The highest science according to the logicians and according to the Muslims. And this is contrary to the highest science according to the Muslims, for it is knowledge of Allah, who is in Himself — the highest science according to the Muslims is higher than others from every aspect, and knowledge of Him is the highest of sciences from every aspect, and knowledge of Him is the foundation of every science, and they concede that knowledge of Him, if attained in a complete manner, entails knowledge of every existent. And this is contrary to knowledge of the named 'existence'. For this has no reality in external existence; nor does knowledge of the commonality entail knowledge of its genera and species and what distinguishes each thing; rather it contains only knowledge of a commonality that has no external conception; it is only knowledge of these commonalities. And mere knowledge of that does not necessitate perfection of the soul, nor even knowledge of its general divisions. For if we know that 'existence' is divided into 'substance' and 'accident', and that the divisions of 'substance' are five as they claim (1) — even though that is not correct, and nothing is established from what they mention except 'body'. As for 'matter', 'form', 'soul', 'intellect', no reality in external existence is established for them, unless it is a body or an accident. But what they establish returns to something estimated in the mind, not in external existence, as has been elaborated in its place. (2) And this has been acknowledged by those who support them and magnify them, such as Abu Muhammad ibn Hazm (3) and others. Ibn Hazm al-Andalusi's magnification of the people of logic — and his magnification of logic he transmitted with his chain of transmission from Matta al-Tarjuman who translated it into Arabic. Yet he acknowledged what we have mentioned, and this has been elaborated in its place. (4) And we assume here the external existence of that. So knowledge of its division into five substances, and division of accident into nine types, even though no proof has been established for its division into nine according to some of them. And they have recited in it:
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison. Ibn Taymiyya first states the topic: the highest science according to logicians and Muslims. He then presents the Islamic view: the highest science is knowledge of Allah, which is superior in every respect, is the foundation of all sciences, and if perfect leads to knowledge of everything. In contrast, knowledge of the concept of 'existence' is a mere mental commonality with no external reality. Such knowledge does not lead to perfection of the soul. He attacks the logicians' divisions of existence into substance and accident, and substance into five types (body, matter, form, soul, intellect), arguing that only body is real; the others are mental constructs. He even notes that supporters like Ibn Hazm acknowledged this. Ibn Taymiyya then hypothetically grants external existence to their categories but still insists that their divisions are unproven and lead nowhere. The passage ends with a poetic reference, implying the futility of such schema.

جوهر
عرض
مادة
صورة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' highest science (knowledge of universal existence) is a mere mental abstraction, while the Islamic highest science (knowledge of Allah) is the true foundation of all knowledge.
