Equivocal Names and the Common Universal Meaning
Ibn Taymiyya explains that equivocal names require a shared universal concept, and notes scholarly dispute on divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of equivocation (homonymy, univocality, and graded equivocation) in the context of divine names, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya insists on a common universal meaning.

Core Idea
Equivocal names share a core meaning despite graded intensity – tashkik.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a key logical and theological puzzle: how can terms like 'existence', 'living', 'knowing' apply to both God and creation without implying either total difference (homonymy) or exact sameness (univocality)? He presents the category of mushakkaka (graded equivocation) as the solution, arguing that these names have a common universal meaning that exists in the mind, serving as the basis for real distinctions. Understanding this is crucial for avoiding both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and complete negation (ta'til) when speaking about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of equivocation mentioned
Find in the Arabic the three categories: ishtirak lafzi (homonymy), ishtirak ma'nawi (univocality with equal individuals), and tashkik (graded univocality). Note how Ibn Taymiyya positions tashkik as a subtype of tawatu' (general equivocation).
2
Analyze the whiteness example
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of 'whiteness' applied to snow and ivory. The common meaning is the quality of whiteness, but it differs in intensity. How does this illustrate his point?
3
Apply the same logic to existence
He extends the analogy to 'existence' for the Necessary (God) and the possible (creatures). Identify the common universal (existence) and the gradation (necessary existence is more complete).
4
Connect to divine names
Recognize that all names and attributes of God (Living, Knowing, etc.) follow this same pattern of mushakkaka.
5
Locate the ground of division (mawrid al-taqsim)
He introduces the term 'mawrid al-taqsim' as the common universal that is divided into particulars (e.g., existent divided into necessary and possible). Why is this concept necessary? Without a common ground, the division would be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك ولهذا كان الحذاق يختارون أن الأسماء المقولة عليه وعلى غيره مقولة بطريق التشكيك، الذي هو نوع من التواطئء العام، ليست بطريق الاشتراك اللفظي، ولا بطريق الاشتراك المعنوي الذي تتماثل أفراده، بل بطريق الاشتراك المعنوي الذي تتفاضل أفراده . كما يطلق لفظ [١٤٠] البياض والسواد على الشديد كبياض الثلج، وعلى ما دونه كبياض العاج . فكذلك لفظ «الوجود» يطلق على الواجب والممكن، وهو في الواجب أكمل وأفضلُ من فضل هذا البياض على هذا البياض . لكن هذا التفاضل في الأسماء المشككة لا يمنع أن يكون أصل المعنى مشتركاً كلياً بينهما . فلا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك، وإن كان ذلك لا يكون إلا في الذهن. وذلك هو مورد التقسيم» - تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته. إذا قيل «الموجود ينقسم إلى واجب و ممكن فإن مورد التقسيم مشترك بين الأقسام. ثم كون وجود هذا الواجب أكمل من وجود الممكن لا يمنع أن يكون مسمى الوجود» معنى كلياً مشتركاً بينهما . وهكذا في سائر الأسماء والصفات المطلقة على الخالق والمخلوق كاسم الحيّ ،والعليم ،والقدير ،والسميع والبصير؛ وكذلك في صفاته، كعلمه، وقدرته، ورحمته، ورضاه، وغضبه، وفَرحه، وسائر ما نطقت به الرسل من أسمائه وصفاته . والناس تنازعوا في هذا الباب. فقالت طائفة كأبي العباس الناشي(١) من شيوخ
It is necessary in equivocal (mushakkaka) names for there to be a common universal meaning. That is why the sharp-witted choose that the names predicated of Him and of others are predicated by way of tashkik, which is a kind of general tawatu' (equivocation), not by way of homonymy (ishtirak lafzi), nor by way of univocality (ishtirak ma'nawi) whose individuals are equal, but by way of univocality whose individuals differ in degree. Just as the term 'whiteness' and 'blackness' is applied to the intense, like the whiteness of snow, and to what is less, like the whiteness of ivory. Similarly, the term 'existence' is applied to the necessary and the possible, and it is in the necessary more complete and better than the preference of this whiteness over that whiteness. But this gradation in equivocal names does not prevent that the original meaning is a common universal between them. So it is necessary in equivocal names for there to be a common universal meaning, even if that only exists in the mind. And that is the ground of division – the division of the universal into its particulars. When it is said 'the existent is divided into necessary and possible', the ground of division is common between the divisions. Then the fact that the existence of the necessary is more complete than the existence of the possible does not prevent that the denomination 'existence' is a common universal meaning between them. And similarly in all the names and attributes applied to the Creator and the creature, like the names Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing; and likewise in His attributes, like His knowledge, power, mercy, pleasure, anger, joy, and all that the messengers have spoken of His names and attributes. And people have disagreed on this topic. A group, like Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi(1) from among the senior scholars,
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of equivocal names (mushakkaka) as applied to God and creation. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting that such names must have a common universal meaning. He explains that scholars prefer the category of tashkik (graded equivocation), which is a type of general tawatu' but not pure homonymy (different meanings) nor pure univocality (identical meaning). The example of whiteness shows that the same term applies to different intensities. Similarly, 'existence' applies to God (necessary) and possible things, with God's existence being more perfect. However, this difference does not nullify the common universal concept that the mind abstracts, which serves as the basis for dividing 'existent' into necessary and possible. This principle extends to all divine names and attributes. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya notes that scholars have differed on this issue, citing Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi as an example of a critic of the logicians' view.

مورد التقسيم

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refines the logic of divine names: they are mushakkaka, sharing a core universal meaning in the mind while differing in perfection in reality.

Ambiguous Names Require a Common Universal Meaning
Ibn Taymiyya argues that ambiguous names require a shared universal meaning, refuting those who deny commonality between Creator and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that equivocal names (mushtarakka) share a common universal meaning and explain why this is necessary for coherent theological discourse.

Core Idea
Even ambiguous names like 'existence' must share a common core meaning to be intelligible.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a central problem in Islamic theology: how can we speak about God and creation using the same words (e.g., 'Living', 'Knowing') without implying that God is like creation? He argues that such names are equivocal (mushtarakka) – they apply to different things with varying intensity, but nevertheless share a common conceptual meaning. This common meaning is essential for logical division and for the validity of theological arguments. Let's unpack his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states his thesis: 'لا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك'. This is the core: equivocal names need a universal meaning.
2
Distinguish the Three Types of Predication
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes four types: verbal homonymy (ishtirāk lafẓī), univocity with identical individuals (ishtirāk maʿnawī mutamāthil), and univocity with gradation (ishtirāk maʿnawī mutafāḍil). The third is his focus. Notice how he categorizes equivocal names as a subset of general univocity.
3
Extract the Key Example: Existence
The example of 'existence' (wujūd) is used to illustrate the point. It is predicated of the Necessary (God) and the contingent, but in God it is more complete. Yet, as he argues, 'the existence of the contingent necessitates the existence of the Necessary', so there is a common factor.
He uses the concept of 'mawrid al-taqsīm' - the locus of division - to show that without a shared meaning, division is impossible.
4
Understand the Historical Dispute
Ibn Taymiyya presents three positions: (1) names are literal for God, metaphorical for creation (some Muʿtazila); (2) metaphorical for God, literal for creation (Jahmiyya, Bāṭiniyya, philosophers); (3) literal for both (majority, including Ibn Taymiyya). He then criticizes those who inconsistently accept literality for some terms but deny it for others.
5
Recognize the Broader Application to Divine Attributes
He extends the argument to all divine names and attributes (Living, Knowing, etc.). The same reasoning that establishes a common meaning for 'existence' applies to all of them. This refutes the deniers who saw no commonality.
This is a critique of those who deny the reality of divine attributes or treat them as mere metaphors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك ولهذا كان الحذاق يختارون أن الأسماء المقولة عليه وعلى غيره مقولة بطريق التشكيك، الذي هو نوع من التواطئء العام، ليست بطريق الاشتراك اللفظي، ولا بطريق الاشتراك المعنوي الذي تتماثل أفراده، بل بطريق الاشتراك المعنوي الذي تتفاضل أفراده . كما يطلق لفظ البياض والسواد على الشديد كبياض الثلج، وعلى ما دونه كبياض العاج . فكذلك لفظ «الوجود» يطلق على الواجب والممكن، وهو في الواجب أكمل وأفضلُ من فضل هذا البياض على هذا البياض . لكن هذا التفاضل في الأسماء المشككة لا يمنع أن يكون أصل المعنى مشتركاً كلياً بينهما . فلا بد في الأسماء المشككة من معنى كلي مشترك، وإن كان ذلك لا يكون إلا في الذهن. وذلك هو مورد التقسيم» - تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته. إذا قيل «الموجود ينقسم إلى واجب و ممكن فإن مورد التقسيم مشترك بين الأقسام. ثم كون وجود هذا الواجب أكمل من وجود الممكن لا يمنع أن يكون مسمى الوجود» معنى كلياً مشتركاً بينهما . وهكذا في سائر الأسماء والصفات المطلقة على الخالق والمخلوق كاسم الحيّ ،والعليم ،والقدير ،والسميع والبصير ؛ وكذلك في صفاته، كعلمه، وقدرته، ورحمته، ورضاه، وغضبه، وفَرحه، وسائر ما نطقت به الرسل من أسمائه وصفاته . الخلاف في الأسماء التي تطلق عليه تعالى وعلى العباد والناس تنازعوا في هذا الباب. فقالت طائفة كأبي العباس الناشي من شيوخ المعتزلة الذين كانوا أسبق من أبي علي هي حقيقة في الخالق، مجاز في المخلوق. وقالت طائفة من الجَهْمية والباطنية والفلاسفة، بالعكس: هي مجاز في الخالق، حقيقة في المخلوق. وقال جماهير الطوائف : هي حقيقة فيهما. وهذا قول طوائف النظار من المعتزلة، والأشعرية، والكَرَّامية، والفقهاء، وأهل الحديث، والصوفية. وهو قول الفلاسفة . لكن كثيراً من هؤلاء يتناقض، فيقر في بعضها بأنها حقيقة، كاسم «الموجود»، و «النفس»، و «الذات»، و «الحقيقة»، ونحو ذلك. وينازع في بعضها لِشُبَهِ نُفَاة الجميع. والقولُ فيما نفاه نظيرُ القول فيما أثبته، ولكن هو لقصوره فرّق بين المتماثلين . ونفي الجميع يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً) . وقد عُلم أن «الموجود» ينقسم إلى واجب وممكن وقديم وحادث، وغني وفقير، ومفعول وغير مفعول؛ وأن وجود الممكن يستلزم وجود الواجب، ووجودَ المحدث يستلزم وجود القديم، ووجود الفقير يستلزم وجود الغني، ووجود المفعول يستلزم وجود غير المفعول. وحينئذ فبين الوجودين أمر مشترك، و «الواجب» يختص بما يتميز به. فكذلك القول في الجميع . والأسماء المشككة متواطئة باعتبار القدر المشترك. ولهذا كان المتقدمون المشككة هي المتواطئة من نظار الفلاسفة وغيرهم لا يخصون المشككة باسم ، بل لفظ «المتواطئة» يتناول ذلك كله . فالمشككة قسم من المتواطئة العامة»، وقسيم «المتواطئة الخاصة». وإذا كان كذلك فلا بد في المشككة من إثبات قدر مشترك كلي، وهو مسمى المتواطئة العامة». وذلك لا يكون مطلقاً إلا في الذهن وهذا مدلول قياسهم البرهاني».
It is necessary in equivocal (mushtarakka) names that there be a common universal meaning. For this reason, the perspicacious choose that names predicated of Him and of others are predicated by way of tashkīk (equivocity), which is a type of general univocity (al-tawāṭu' al-ʿāmm), not by way of verbal homonymy (ishtirāk lafẓī), nor by way of conceptual univocity whose individuals are identical, but by way of conceptual univocity whose individuals differ in degree. As the word 'whiteness' and 'blackness' is used for the intense, like the whiteness of snow, and for what is less than it, like the whiteness of ivory. Similarly, the word 'existence' is used for the Necessary and the contingent, and it is more complete and more excellent in the Necessary than the excess of this whiteness over this whiteness. However, this gradation in equivocal names does not prevent the original meaning from being common and universal between them. So it is necessary in equivocal names that there be a common universal meaning, even if that only occurs in the mind. That is the locus of division (mawrid al-taqsīm) – the division of the universal into its particulars. When it is said 'the existent is divided into necessary and contingent', the locus of division is common among the divisions. Then the fact that the existence of this Necessary is more complete than the existence of the contingent does not prevent the name 'existence' from being a common universal meaning between them. And likewise in all other names and attributes applied to the Creator and the creature, like the name 'the Living', 'the Knowing', 'the Powerful', 'the Hearing', 'the Seeing'; and also in His attributes, like His knowledge, His power, His mercy, His pleasure, His anger, His joy, and all that the Messengers have spoken of His names and attributes. The dispute concerning the names that are applied to Him, exalted, and to the servants: people have disagreed on this matter. A group like Abū al-ʿAbbās al-Nāshī, one of the early Muʿtazila who preceded Abū ʿAlī, said they are literal (ḥaqīqa) in the Creator, metaphorical (majāz) in the creature. A group from the Jahmiyya, Bāṭiniyya, and philosophers said the opposite: they are metaphorical in the Creator, literal in the creature. The majority of groups said they are literal in both. This is the view of groups of the speculative theologians from the Muʿtazila, Ashʿariyya, Karrāmiyya, jurists, people of ḥadīth, and Sufis. And it is the view of the philosophers. But many of these contradict themselves: they affirm in some of them that they are literal, like the name 'existent', 'self', 'essence', 'reality', and the like, and they dispute in some of them due to doubts of those who deny all. The statement regarding what they deny is analogous to the statement regarding what they affirm, but it is due to his deficiency that he differentiates between two similar things. And the denial of all is impossible for an existent being. It is known that 'the existent' is divided into necessary and contingent, eternal and originated, wealthy and poor, effected and non-effected; and that the existence of the contingent necessitates the existence of the Necessary, the existence of the originated necessitates the existence of the eternal, the existence of the poor necessitates the existence of the wealthy, and the existence of the effected necessitates the existence of the non-effected. Then, between the two existences there is a common matter, and 'the Necessary' is distinguished by what differentiates it. So likewise the statement regarding all. The equivocal names are univocal (mutawāṭi'a) with respect to the common factor. For this reason, the earlier philosophers and others among the speculative theologians did not single out the equivocal as a name, but rather the term 'mutawāṭi'a' encompasses all that. So the equivocal is a division of the general univocal and a counterpart of the specific univocal. If this is so, then in the equivocal it is necessary to affirm a common universal factor, which is the denotation of the general univocal. And that does not exist absolutely except in the mind, and this is the import of their demonstrative syllogism.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' treatment of ambiguous (mushtarakka) names, particularly in relation to divine attributes. He argues that even in equivocal names—where meaning varies by degree—there must be a common universal meaning that makes them intelligible. He begins by stating this necessity and notes that the perspicacious choose that names predicated of God and creation are predicated by tashkīk (equivocity), which is a type of general univocity (tawāṭu' ʿāmm). This is not pure verbal homonymy (ishtirāk lafẓī) where meanings are entirely different, nor is it pure conceptual univocity where individuals are identical; rather, it is conceptual univocity where individuals differ in intensity, like 'whiteness' applied to snow and ivory. Similarly, 'existence' is applied to the Necessary (God) and the contingent, but in God it is more complete. He stresses that this gradation does not negate a common meaning; otherwise, the division of 'existent' into necessary and contingent would be impossible, as the locus of division (mawrid al-taqsīm) must be shared. He then extends this principle to all divine names and attributes: Living, Knowing, etc. Next, he presents the historical dispute: some early Mu'tazilites said these names are literal for God, metaphorical for creation; Jahmiyya, Bāṭiniyya, and philosophers said the opposite; the majority—including later Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, jurists, ḥadīth scholars, Sufis, and even philosophers—say they are literal in both. Ibn Taymiyya criticizes those who inconsistently accept literality for some terms (like 'existent') but deny it for others, arguing that the same reasoning applies. He then demonstrates that 'existent' is divided into necessary/contingent, etc., and that the existence of the latter entails the former, so there is a common factor. Finally, he clarifies that equivocal names are actually a subset of general univocal (mutawāṭi'a), since they share a common conceptual core, which is only fully realized in the mind. This underpins the demonstrative syllogism (qiyās burhānī) used in theology.

Key Terms
المشككة
المتواطئة العامة
الوجود

D) Because all names are univocal.

Key Takeaway
Equivocal names like 'existence' require a common universal meaning, which is the basis for logical division and the intelligibility of theological discourse about God and creation.

Defining Evidence: Certain and Probable Proofs
Author defines الدليل and classifies it into certain and probable based on concomitant strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between certain (qaṭ'ī) and probable (ẓannī) evidence by analyzing the strength of the necessary concomitance (luzūm).

Core Idea
Evidence is defined by necessary concomitance—certain if the tie is necessary, probable if merely apparent.

How do we know when a piece of evidence gives certainty versus mere probability? Ibn Taymiyya presents the Muslim theoreticians' definition of evidence (dalīl) and its classification. This lesson unpacks that definition and equips you to evaluate the strength of any Islamic theological argument by examining the concomitance between evidence and what it proves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of dalīl
Find the two formulations of 'evidence' in the Arabic: one requiring knowledge of the evidence to necessitate the sought, the other requiring correct consideration to lead to knowledge or preponderant belief. Note the difference in certainty.
The second definition allows for probable outcomes.
2
Separate the terminological dispute
Some restrict 'evidence' to the certain type, calling the probable type 'imārah'. The majority call both 'evidence'. Understand the two camps to avoid confusion when reading other scholars.
Ibn Taymiyya sides with the majority.
3
Extract the principle of necessary concomitance
The passage states: evidence necessitates the thing evidenced. If the concomitance is two-way, each can prove the other. This is the core logical mechanism.
Concomitance can be certain (luzūm qaṭ'ī) or apparent (ẓāhir).
4
Apply the classification to the creation example
The author gives creation as certain evidence for the Creator. Check why: existence of creation necessitates the existence of the Creator because creation without a creator is impossible. This is a paradigm of certain concomitance.
Other examples, like smoke indicating fire, are only probable because the concomitance can fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد «الدليل» عند النظار: ولهذا عَدَل نظار المسلمين عن طريقهم. فقالوا: «الدليل» هو المرشد إلى المطلوب؛ وهو الموصل إلى المقصود؛ وهو ما يكون العلم به مستلزماً للعلم بالمطلوب. أو ما يكون النظر الصحيحُ فيه موصلاً إلى المطلوب؛ وهو ما يكون النظر الصحيح فيه موصلاً إلى علم، أو إلى اعتقاد راجح . ولهم نزاع اصطلاحي هل يسمى هذا الثاني «دليلاً»، أو يخص باسم «الإمارة» . والجمهور يسمون الجميع دليلاً». ومن أهل الكلام من لا يسمى بـ «الدليل» إلا الأول . ثم الضابط في الدليل أن يكون مستلزماً للمدلول فكل ما كان مستلزماً لغيره أمكن أن يُستدل به عليه فإن كان التلازم من الطرفين أمكن أن يستدل بكل منهما على الآخر. فيستدل المستدل بما عَلِمه منهما على الآخر الذي لم يعلمه . ثم إن كان «اللزوم قطعياً كان الدليل قطعياً». وإن كان ظاهراً - وقد يتخلف - كان الدليل «ظنّياً». فالأول كدلالة المخلوقات على خالقها سبحانه وتعالى، وعلمه، وقدرته، ومشيئته ورحمته، وحكمته. فإن وجودها مستلزم لوجود ذلك، ووجودها بدون ذلك ممتنع . فلا توجد إلا دالة على ذلك .
The definition of 'evidence' (al-dalīl) according to the theoreticians: For this reason, the theoreticians of the Muslims turned away from their [the logicians'] path. They said: 'The evidence is that which guides to the sought; it is that which leads to the desired; it is that knowledge of which necessitates knowledge of the sought. Or it is that correct consideration of which leads to the sought; it is that correct consideration of which leads to knowledge or to a preponderant belief.' And there is a terminological dispute among them: whether this second [type] is called 'evidence' or is specifically named 'indication' (al-imārah). The majority call all of it 'evidence'. Among the theologians, some do not call 'evidence' except the first. Then the principle concerning evidence is that it necessitates the thing evidenced (al-madlūl). So everything that necessitates something else can be used to reason to it. And if the concomitance is mutual, each can be used to reason to the other. So the reasoner uses what he knows of the two to [reason to] the other which he does not know. Then, if the concomitance is certain, the evidence is certain. And if it is apparent but may be invalidated, the evidence is probable. The first is like the indication of created things to their Creator—glorified and exalted be He—and to His knowledge, power, will, mercy, and wisdom. For their existence necessitates that existence, and their existence without that is impossible. So they do not exist except as indicating that.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of 'evidence' (al-dalīl) as understood by Muslim theoreticians, in contrast to logicians. The author states that the definition hinges on necessary concomitance (talāzum): knowledge of the evidence entails knowledge of what is sought. Two definitions are given: one strict (knowledge of the evidence necessitates knowledge of the sought) and one broader (correct consideration of the evidence leads to knowledge or probable belief). There is a terminological dispute: some restrict 'evidence' to the first, calling the second 'indication' (imārah), but the majority apply 'evidence' to both. The author then lays down the principle: evidence must necessitate the thing evidenced. If the concomitance is mutual, each can serve as evidence for the other. Finally, evidence is classified based on the strength of the concomitance: certain (qaṭ'ī) if the concomitance is necessary, and probable (ẓannī) if it is merely apparent and can be invalidated. The example given is the creation as certain evidence for the existence and attributes of the Creator, because it is impossible for creation to exist without Him.

مدلول
لزوم قطعي

Key Takeaway
Evidence is defined by necessary concomitance; certain evidence has a necessary tie, while probable evidence has an apparent tie that may fail.

Definition of Dalil and its Conditions
Author defines dalil, outlines its conditions, and distinguishes qat'i from dhanni evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential condition (ḍābiṭ) for evidence (dalīl) as implicative of its indicated, and distinguish between certain (qaṭʿī) and speculative (ẓannī) evidence.

Core Idea
Evidence is valid only if it necessarily implies its indicated — that's the controller (ḍābiṭ).

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents the definition of dalīl as used by Muslim theologians. He outlines the essential condition: every evidence must be implicative of what it indicates. He also distinguishes between certain and speculative evidence, and clarifies that evidence can be existence or non-existence. This definition is foundational for evaluating arguments in kalām, fiqh, and uṣūl.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions of dalīl
The author gives two definitions: one that yields knowledge, and one that yields preponderant belief. Identify both in the Arabic.
2
Find the essential condition (ḍābiṭ)
The author states: 'الضابط في الدليل أن يكون مستلزماً للمدلول'. Explain what it means for evidence to be implicative.
Implication means that if the evidence is true, the indicated must also be true.
3
Distinguish qaṭʿī and ẓannī evidence
The author explains that if the implication is necessary, the evidence is certain; if merely apparent, it is speculative. Identify the examples given for each.
Certain evidence example: created universe indicating the Creator. Speculative evidence example: a sign that may sometimes fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا عَدَل نظار المسلمين عن طريقهم. فقالوا: «الدليل» هو المرشد إلى المطلوب؛ وهو الموصل إلى المقصود؛ وهو ما يكون العلم به مستلزماً للعلم بالمطلوب. أو ما يكون النظر الصحيحُ فيه موصلاً إلى المطلوب؛ وهو ما يكون النظر الصحيح فيه موصلاً إلى علم، أو إلى اعتقاد راجح . ولهم نزاع اصطلاحي هل يسمى هذا الثاني «دليلاً»، أو يخص باسم «الإمارة» . والجمهور يسمون الجميع دليلاً». ومن أهل الكلام من لا يسمى بـ «الدليل» إلا الأول . ثم الضابط في الدليل أن يكون مستلزماً للمدلول فكل ما كان مستلزماً لغيره أمكن أن يُستدل به عليه فإن كان التلازم من الطرفين أمكن أن يستدل بكل منهما على الآخر. فيستدل المستدل بما عَلِمه منهما على الآخر الذي لم يعلمه . القطعي والظني ثم إن كان «اللزوم قطعياً كان الدليل قطعياً». وإن كان ظاهراً - وقد يتخلف - مثال الدليل القطعي كان الدليل «ظنّياً». فالأول كدلالة المخلوقات على خالقها سبحانه وتعالى، وعلمه، وقدرته، ومشيئته ورحمته، وحكمته. فإن وجودها مستلزم لوجود ذلك، ووجودها بدون ذلك ممتنع . فلا توجد إلا دالة على ذلك . ومثل دلالة خبر الرسول على ثبوت ما أخبر به عن الله، فإنه لا يقول عليه إلا الحق، إذ كان معصوماً في خبره عن الله لا يستقر في خبره عنه خطأ ألبتة . فهذا دليل مستلزم لمدلوله لزوماً واجباً، لا ينفك عنه بحال، وسواء كان قد يكون الملزوم المستدلّ به وجوداً» أو «عدَماً». فقد يكون الدليل وجوداً» و «عدماً»، ويُستدل وجوداً بكل منهما على «وجود» و«عدم». فإنه يُستدل بثبوت الشيء على انتفاء «نقيضه» وعدماً وضده، ويُستدل بانتفاء نقيضه على ثبوته ويُستدل بثبوت الملزوم على ثبوت اللازم، وبانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم . بل كلُّ دليل يُستدل به فإنه ملزوم لمدلوله. وقد دخل في هذا كل ما ذكروه وما لم يذكروه .
And therefore the Muslim theoreticians deviated from their path. They said: 'al-dalīl' is that which guides to the sought; it is that which leads to the intended; it is that whose knowledge necessitates knowledge of the sought. Or it is that whose correct consideration leads to the sought; it is that whose correct consideration leads to knowledge or to a preponderant belief. And they have a terminological dispute: whether this second type is called 'dalīl' or is specifically named 'imārah'. The majority call all of them 'dalīl'. And among the theologians are those who call only the first 'dalīl'. Then the general rule for al-dalīl is that it be implicative of the indicated. For everything that is implicative of something else, it is possible to use it as evidence for that other. If the implication is mutual, it is possible to use each of them as evidence for the other. So the person seeking evidence uses what he knows of the two for the other he does not know. Al-qaṭ'ī and al-ẓannī: Then if the implication is certain, the evidence is certain. And if it is apparent but may be deficient, the evidence is speculative. Example of certain evidence: The first type is like the indication of created things upon their Creator, Blessed and Exalted, and upon His knowledge, power, will, mercy, and wisdom. For their existence necessitates His existence, and their existence without that is impossible. So they only exist as indicating that. And like the indication of the report of the Messenger upon the affirmation of what he reported about God, for he does not speak except the truth, since he was infallible in his reporting about God; no error ever settles in his reporting about Him. So this is evidence that necessitates its indicated with a necessary implication that never separates from it. And it may be that the implicans used as evidence is existence or non-existence. So evidence may be existence or non-existence, and each is used as evidence for existence and non-existence. For one uses the affirmation of a thing to infer the negation of its contradictory and its opposite, and one uses the negation of its contradictory to infer its affirmation, and one uses the affirmation of the implicans to infer the affirmation of the necessary consequent, and the negation of the consequent to infer the negation of the implicans. Rather, every evidence that is used is an implicans of its indicated. And included in this is everything they mentioned and what they did not mention.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of 'al-dalīl' (evidence) as understood by the Muslim theologians (nuzzār). The author first contrasts their approach with that of the Greek logicians. The theologians define al-dalīl in two ways: (1) something whose knowledge necessarily leads to knowledge of the sought (al-maṭlūb), which yields certain knowledge; (2) something whose correct consideration leads to knowledge or a preponderant belief (iʿtiqād rājiḥ), which yields speculative knowledge. The author notes a terminological dispute: some restrict 'dalīl' to the first type and call the second 'imārah' (sign), but the majority call both 'dalīl'. The essential condition (ḍābiṭ) for any evidence is that it be implicative (mustalzim) of its indicated (madlūl). If the implication is mutual, either can serve as evidence for the other. The author then classifies evidence into certain (qaṭʿī) if the implication is necessary and unbreakable, and speculative (ẓannī) if the implication is only apparent and may be absent. As examples of certain evidence, he gives the created universe indicating the Creator—since contingent existence necessarily entails a Necessary Existence—and the prophetic reports, because the Prophet is infallible. He then elaborates that evidence can be existence or non-existence, and illustrates the logical rules: affirming the implicans affirms the consequent, negating the consequent negates the implicans, etc. This comprehensive principle covers all types of evidence, including those the logicians recognize.

المدلول
اللزوم
القطعي

The Necessary Evidence and Four Types of Inference
Defines evidence as necessarily implicative and lists four types of inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given relationship qualifies as evidence (dalil) by checking for necessary entailment, and classify the direction of inference into one of the four types.

Core Idea
Evidence is a necessary antecedent; from it, four inferential moves are possible.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya defines what makes something evidence (dalil) in the eyes of theologians. He insists that evidence must be necessarily implicative—the conclusion cannot fail to follow. He then lists four types of inference that can be drawn from an evidence, covering both existence and non-existence. Understanding this definition is crucial because it sets the standard for what counts as a proof in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential property of evidence
Find the phrase 'مستلزم لمدلوله لزوماً واجباً' – it tells you that the relationship between dalil and its madlul is necessary, not probabilistic.
The Prophet's report is given as an example: his infallibility makes his report necessarily true.
2
Recognize that evidence can be existence or non-existence
The passage says 'سواء كان قد يكون الملزوم المستدلّ به «وجوداً» أو «عدماً»' – evidence is not limited to positive facts; absence can also serve as evidence.
This expands the concept beyond what some might assume.
3
List the four types of inference
Extract from the Arabic: (a) from existence of a thing to non-existence of its opposite, (b) from non-existence of opposite to existence, (c) from existence of the entailing thing (malzum) to existence of the consequent (lazim), (d) from non-existence of the consequent to non-existence of the entailing thing.
These cover all combinations of existence and non-existence for the evidence and conclusion.
4
Understand the unifying principle
Every evidence is a malzum for its madlul. The four types are all special cases of this necessary relationship, and the definition covers even unmentioned cases.
This is a comprehensive definition that transcends particular examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - حد «الدليل» عند النظار
ومثل دلالة خبر الرسول على ثبوت ما أخبر به عن الله، فإنه لا يقول عليه إلا الحق، إذ كان معصوماً في خبره عن الله لا يستقر في خبره عنه خطأ ألبتة.
فهذا دليل مستلزم لمدلوله لزوماً واجباً، لا ينفك عنه بحال، وسواء كان قد يكون الملزوم المستدلّ به «وجوداً» أو «عدماً». فقد يكون الدليل «وجوداً» و«عدماً»، ويُستدل بكل منهما على «وجود» و«عدم». فإنه يُستدل بثبوت الشيء على انتفاء «نقيضه» وبانتفاء نقيضه على ثبوته ويُستدل بثبوت الملزوم على ثبوت اللازم، وبانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم. بل كل دليل يُستدل به فإنه ملزوم لمدلوله. وقد دخل في هذا كل ما ذكروه وما لم يذكروه.
The third position: Definition of 'evidence' according to the theologians. And like the indication of the Prophet's report of the establishment of what he informed about from Allah, for he does not say about it except the truth, since he is infallible in his report from Allah, no error settles in his report about Him at all. So this is an evidence necessarily entailing its implication, a necessary entailment, not separable from it in any case, and regardless of whether the entailing thing used as evidence may be existence or non-existence. So evidence may be existence and non-existence, and each is used to infer existence and non-existence. For one infers from the existence of a thing to the non-existence of its opposite, and from the non-existence of its opposite to its existence, and one infers from the existence of the entailing thing to the existence of the consequent, and from the non-existence of the consequent to the non-existence of the entailing thing. Rather, every evidence used for inference is an entailing thing for its implication. And this includes all that they mentioned and what they did not mention.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes evidence (dalil) from the perspective of the theologians (nuzzar). It emphasizes that evidence must be necessarily implicative—the relationship between evidence and its conclusion is necessary, not probabilistic. The author uses the example of the Prophet's report as a case of infallible evidence. Then it explains that the evidence (the antecedent) can be either existence or non-existence, and from each we can infer either existence or non-existence. It lists four types of inference: (1) from the existence of a thing to the non-existence of its opposite (if A exists, then not-A does not exist), (2) from the non-existence of its opposite to its existence, (3) from the existence of the antecedent to the existence of the consequent, and (4) from the non-existence of the consequent to the non-existence of the antecedent. Finally, it asserts that every evidence is an antecedent (malzum) for its implication, and this definition covers all cases, including those not explicitly mentioned by logicians.

Key Terms
دليل
ملزوم
لازم

Checkpoint

A) From existence of a thing to non-existence of its opposite
B) From non-existence of a thing to existence of its opposite
C) From existence of an antecedent to existence of consequent
D) From non-existence of consequent to non-existence of antecedent

Key Takeaway
Evidence in Islamic theology is defined by necessary entailment, and from any evidence, four valid inferential directions are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the four types of inference is being used in the following: 'If there is an effect, there is a cause. But there is no cause, therefore there is no effect.' Answer: From non-existence of consequent (cause) to non-existence of antecedent (effect) – type 4.
?
Consider the statement: 'The Quran is the word of Allah because it is miraculous.' Is the miraculousness a necessary antecedent for its being the word of Allah? Explain using the definition of evidence.
